'''Pakistan''' ({{IPAc-en|audio=En-us-Pakistan.ogg|Ëˆ|p|Ã¦|k|áµ»|s|t|Ã¦|n}} or {{IPAc-en|audio=En-us-Pakistan-2.ogg|p|É‘Ë?|k|áµ»|Ëˆ|s|t|É‘Ë?|n}}), officially the '''Islamic Republic of Pakistan''', is a country in [[South Asia]]. It is the [[List of countries by population|sixth-most populous]] country with a population exceeding [[Demographics of Pakistan|201 million people]].&lt;ref name=&quot;USCB&quot; /&gt; It is the 36th largest country in the world in terms of area with an area covering 881,913&amp;nbsp;km&lt;sup&gt;2&lt;/sup&gt; ({{nowrap|340,509 sq mi}}). Pakistan has a {{convert|1046|km|mi|adj=on}} [[coastline]] along the [[Arabian Sea]] and the [[Gulf of Oman]] in the south and is bordered by [[India]] to the east, [[Afghanistan]] to the west, [[Iran]] to the southwest and [[China]] in the far northeast respectively. It is separated from [[Tajikistan]] by Afghanistan's narrow [[Wakhan Corridor]] in the north, and also shares a [[maritime border]] with [[Oman]].

The territory that now constitutes Pakistan is considered a [[cradle of civilization]]{{Sfn|Wright|2010|ps=:Quote: &quot;The Indus civilization is one of three in the 'Ancient East' that, along with [[Mesopotamia]] and [[Ancient Egypt|Pharonic Egypt]], was a cradle of early civilization in the Old World (Childe 1950).  Mesopotamia and Egypt were longer lived, but coexisted with Indus civilization during its florescence between 2600 and 1900 B.C.  Of the three, the Indus was the most expansive, extending from today's northeast Afghanistan to Pakistan and India.&quot;}}&lt;ref name=&quot;feuerstein&quot;&gt;{{cite book|last=Feuerstein|first=Georg|author2=Subhash Kak |author3=David Frawley |title=In search of the cradle of civilization: new light on ancient India|publisher=Quest Books|location=Wheaton, Illinois|year=1995|page=147|url=https://books.google.com/?id=kbx7q0gxyTcC&amp;printsec=frontcover&amp;dq=In+Search+of+the+Cradle+of+Civilization|isbn=978-0-8356-0720-9}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;Yasmeen Niaz Mohiuddin, ''Pakistan: a global studies handbook.'' ABC-CLIO publishers, 2006, ISBN 1-85109-801-1&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.globalpost.com/dispatch/news/regions/asia-pacific/india/121116/indus-civilization-2000-years-old-archaeologists|title=Archaeologists confirm Indian civilization is 2000 years older than previously believed|author=|date=16 November 2012|work=globalpost.com}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://nation.com.pk/editorials/10-May-2015/cradle-of-civilisation|title=Cradle Of Civilisation|author=|date=|work=nation.com.pk}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://tribune.com.pk/story/1100222/rich-heritage-ancient-civilisations-offer-potential-for-regional-linkages-says-aziz/|title=Rich heritage: Ancient civilisations offer potential for regional linkages, says Aziz - The Express Tribune|author=|date=10 May 2016|work=tribune.com.pk}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=A3aOCwAAQBAJ|title=Killing Civilization: A Reassessment of Early Urbanism and Its Consequences|first=Justin|last=Jennings|date=15 April 2016|publisher=UNM Press|via=Google Books}}&lt;/ref&gt; which was previously home to several [[Prehistory of Pakistan|ancient cultures]], including the [[Mehrgarh]] of the [[Neolithic]] and the Bronze Age [[Indus Valley Civilisation]], and was later home to kingdoms ruled by people of different faiths and cultures, including [[Hindu]]s, [[Indo-Greek Kingdom|Indo-Greeks]], [[Muslim conquest in the Indian subcontinent|Muslims]], [[Timurid dynasty|Turco-Mongols]], [[Afghan (ethnonym)|Afghans]] and [[Sikh]]s. The area has been ruled by numerous empires and dynasties, including the Indian [[Mauryan Empire]], the Persian [[Achaemenid Empire]], [[Alexander the Great|Alexander of Macedonia]], the Arab [[Umayyad Caliphate]], the [[Delhi Sultanate]], the [[Mongol Empire]], the [[Mughal Empire]], the [[Durrani Empire]], the [[Sikh Empire]] and the [[British Raj|British Empire]].

Pakistan is unique among Muslim countries as it is the only country to have been created in the name of Islam.&lt;ref&gt;Hussain, Rizwan . &quot;Pakistan.&quot; In ''The Oxford Encyclopedia of the Islamic World''. ''Oxford Islamic Studies Online''. 04-Apr-2016. &lt;&lt;nowiki&gt;http://www.oxfordislamicstudies.com/article/opr/t236/e0616&lt;/nowiki&gt;&gt;.&lt;/ref&gt; As a result of the [[Pakistan Movement]] led by [[Muhammad Ali Jinnah]] and the [[Indian Subcontinent|subcontinent]]'s struggle for independence, Pakistan was created in 1947 as an independent nation for [[Muslim]]s from the regions in the east and west of the Subcontinent where there was a Muslim majority. Initially a [[Dominion of Pakistan|dominion]], Pakistan adopted a [[Constitution of Pakistan|new constitution]] in 1956, becoming an Islamic republic. A [[Bangladesh Liberation War|civil war]] in 1971 resulted in the secession of [[East Pakistan]] as the new country of [[Bangladesh]].

Pakistan is a [[Federation|federal]] [[parliamentary republic]] consisting of [[Administrative units of Pakistan|four provinces and four federal territories]]. It is an [[Ethnic groups in Pakistan|ethnically]] and [[Languages of Pakistan|linguistically]] diverse country, with a similar variation in its [[Geography of Pakistan|geography]] and [[Fauna of Pakistan|wildlife]]. A [[Regional power|regional]] and [[middle power]],&lt;ref name=&quot;Buzan2004&quot;&gt;{{cite book|author=Barry Buzan|title=The United States and the great powers: world politics in the twenty-first century|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=XvtS5hKg9jYC&amp;pg=PR8|accessdate=27 December 2011|year=2004|publisher=Polity|isbn=978-0-7456-3374-9|pages=71, 99}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Solomon&quot;&gt;{{cite web|author=Hussein Solomon|title=South African Foreign Policy and Middle Power Leadership|url=http://www.iss.co.za/Pubs/Monographs/No13/Solomon.html|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/web/20020624231948/http://www.iss.co.za/Pubs/Monographs/No13/Solomon.html|archivedate=24 June 2002|accessdate=27 December 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt; Pakistan has the [[List of countries by number of troops|seventh largest standing armed forces]] in the world and is also a [[Nuclear power in Pakistan|nuclear power]] as well as a declared [[List of states with nuclear weapons|nuclear-weapons]] state, being the only nation in the [[Muslim world]], and the second in [[South Asia]], to have that status. It has a semi-industrialised [[Economy of Pakistan|economy]] with a well-integrated [[Agriculture in Pakistan|agriculture sector]], its [[economy]] is the [[List of countries by GDP (PPP)|26th largest]] in the world in terms of purchasing power and [[List of countries by GDP (nominal)|45th largest]] in terms of nominal GDP and is also characterized among the [[emerging and growth-leading economies]] of the world.&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite web
|url=http://www.dawn.com/news/1218182|title=Pakistan an emerging market economy: IMF|last=Iqbal|first=Anwar|date=2015-11-08|website=www.dawn.com|access-date=2016-02-27}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite web
|url=http://www.globaldashboard.org/2012/02/13/is-pakistan-an-emerging-market/|title=Is Pakistan an emerging market?|last=Kaplan|first=Seth|access-date=2016-02-27}}&lt;/ref&gt;

The post-independence history of Pakistan has been characterised by periods of military rule, political instability and [[Indo-Pakistani wars and conflicts|conflicts with neighbouring India]]. The country continues to face challenging problems, including [[Human overpopulation|overpopulation]], [[Terrorism in Pakistan|terrorism]], [[Poverty in Pakistan|poverty]], [[Education in Pakistan#Literacy rate|illiteracy]], and [[Corruption in Pakistan|corruption]]. Despite these factors it maintains strategic endowments and development potential while it has made substantial progress in reducing poverty giving it the second lowest headcount poverty rate in [[South Asia]].&lt;ref name=&quot;auto&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.worldbank.org/en/country/pakistan/overview|title=Pakistan Overview|work=worldbank.org}}&lt;/ref&gt; The nation has recently witnessed a rapid expansion of its prosperous middle class, the 18th largest worldwide.&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite web|url=http://tribune.com.pk/story/973649/pakistan-has-18th-largest-middle-class-in-the-world-report|work=ExpressTribune|access-date=2016-06-16}}&lt;/ref&gt; Pakistan's [[Pakistan Stock Exchange|stock exchange]] is Asia's highest performing stock market and, as of 2016, is part of the [[MSCI]]'s emerging markets index.&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite web|url=http://www.ft.com/fastft/2016/06/15/pakistan-unsung-winner-in-mscis-em-decision/|title=Fast FT|last=Membership|first=FT|website=www.ft.com|access-date=2016-06-15}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Bloomberg ranks Pakistan among world's top ten stock market performers|url=http://www.dailytimes.com.pk/national/04-Jul-2015/bloomberg-ranks-pakistan-among-world-s-top-ten-stock-market-performers|work=DailyTimes}}&lt;/ref&gt; It is a member of the [[United Nations]], the [[Commonwealth of Nations]], the [[Next Eleven|Next Eleven Economies]], [[Shanghai Cooperation Organisation]], [[Economic Cooperation Organisation|ECO]], [[Uniting for Consensus|UfC]], [[Developing Eight|D8]], [[Cairns Group]], [[Kyoto Protocol]], [[International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights|ICCPR]], [[Regional Cooperation for Development|RCD]], [[UNCHR]], [[Asian Infrastructure Investment Bank]], [[Group of Eleven]], [[Chinaâ€“Pakistan Free Trade Agreement|CPFTA]], [[Group of 24]], the [[G20 developing nations]], [[ECOSOC]], founding member of the [[Organisation of Islamic Cooperation]], [[South Asian Association for Regional Cooperation|SAARC]] and [[CERN]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://tribune.com.pk/story/671298/thumbs-up-pakistan-meets-criteria-for-cern/|title=Thumbs up: Pakistan meets criteria for CERN|work=The Express Tribune}}&lt;/ref&gt;

==Etymology==&lt;!--linked--&gt;
The name ''[[:wikt:Pakistan|Pakistan]]'' literally means &quot;[[-stan|Land of]] the Pure&quot; in [[Urdu]] and [[Persian language|Persian]]. It comes from the word ''[[:wikt:Ù¾Ø§Ú©|pÄ?k]]'' meaning ''pure'' in [[Persian language|Persian]] and [[Pashto language|Pashto]]&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|title=A Dictionary of Pashto|last=Raverty|first=Henry George|url=http://dsalsrv02.uchicago.edu/cgi-bin/philologic/getobject.pl?c.0:1:1478.raverty}}&lt;/ref&gt; while the word [[-stan|''istÄ?n'']] is a Persian word meaning ''place of''; it is a cognate of the [[Sanskrit]] word ''[[:wikt:à¤¸à¥?à¤¥à¤¾à¤¨|sthÄ?na]]'' ([[Devanagari]]: {{lang|sa|à¤¸à¥?à¤¥à¤¾à¤¨}} {{IPA-sa|stÌªÊ°aË?nÉ™|}}).&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.sanskritdictionary.com/sth%C4%81na/274192/1l|title=Monier-Williams Sanskrit Dictionary|date=1872|accessdate=28 April 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;

It was coined in 1933 as ''Pakstan'' by [[Choudhry Rahmat Ali]], a [[Pakistan Movement]] activist, who published it in his pamphlet ''[[Pakistan Declaration|Now or Never]]'',&lt;ref name=&quot;nowornever&quot;&gt;{{cite web|author=Choudhary Rahmat Ali|title=Now or never: Are we to live or perish for ever?|url=http://www.columbia.edu/itc/mealac/pritchett/00islamlinks/txt_rahmatali_1933.html|accessdate=4 December 2007|date=28 January 1933|publisher=Columbia University}}&lt;/ref&gt; using it as an [[acronym]] (&quot;thirty million Muslim brethren who live in PAKSTAN&quot;) referring to the names of the five northern regions of the [[British Raj]]: [[Punjab, Pakistan|'''P'''unjab]], [[Khyber Pakhtunkhwa|'''A'''fghania]], [[Kashmir|'''K'''ashmir]], [[Sindh|'''S'''indh]], and [[Balochistan, Pakistan|Baluchis'''tan''']].&lt;ref name=&quot;Now or Never&quot;&gt;{{cite journal|author=Choudhary Rahmat Ali|title=Now or Never. Are we to live or perish forever?|date=28 January 1933|url=http://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Now_or_Never;_Are_We_to_Live_or_Perish_Forever%3F}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Ikram1995&quot;&gt;{{cite book|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=7q9EubOYZmwC&amp;pg=PA177 |title=Indian Muslims and partition of India |publisher=Atlantic Publishers &amp; Dist |accessdate=23 December 2011 |author=[[S. M. Ikram]] |date=1 January 1995|isbn=978-81-7156-374-6|pages=177â€“}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.chaudhryrahmatali.com/now%20or%20never/index.htm|title=Rahmat Ali ::Now or Never|publisher=The Pakistan National Movement|accessdate=14 April 2011|author=Rahmat Ali|page=2|deadurl=yes|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/20110419012150/http://www.chaudhryrahmatali.com:80/now%20or%20never/index.htm|archivedate=19 April 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt; The letter ''i'' was incorporated to ease pronunciation and form the linguistically correct and meaningful name.&lt;ref name=&quot;davison&quot;&gt;{{cite journal |author=Roderic H. Davidson|title=Where is the Middle East?|journal=Foreign Affairs|volume=38|pages=665â€“675|year=1960|doi=10.2307/20029452|issue=4}}&lt;/ref&gt;

==History==
{{Main article|History of Pakistan}}{{See also|Outline of South Asian history}}
{{Contains Urdu text|compact=yes}}

===Early and medieval age===
{{main article|Indo-Greek Kingdom|Indus Valley Civilization|Vedic Civilization|Sikh Empire|Mughal Empire}}
[[File:TNMStandingBuddha.jpg|thumb|150px|right|[[Standing Buddha]] from [[Gandhara]]]]
Some of the earliest ancient human civilisations in South Asia originated from areas encompassing present-day Pakistan.&lt;ref&gt;Petraglia, Michael D.; Allchin, Bridget (2007), &quot;Human evolution and culture change in the Indian subcontinent&quot;, in Michael Petraglia, Bridget Allchin, The Evolution and History of Human Populations in South Asia: Inter-disciplinary Studies in Archaeology, Biological Anthropology, Linguistics and Genetics, Springer, ISBN 978-1-4020-5562-1&lt;/ref&gt; The earliest known inhabitants in the region were [[Soanian]] during the [[Lower Paleolithic]], of whom stone tools have been found in the [[Soan Valley]] of [[Punjab, Pakistan|Punjab]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|author=Parth R. Chauhan|title=An Overview of the Siwalik Acheulian &amp; Reconsidering Its Chronological Relationship with the Soanian â€“ A Theoretical Perspective|url=http://www.assemblage.group.shef.ac.uk/issue7/chauhan.html#distribution|work=Sheffield Graduate Journal of Archaeology|publisher=University of Sheffield|accessdate=22 December 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt; The [[Indus River|Indus region]], which covers most of present day Pakistan, was the site of several successive ancient cultures including the Neolithic [[Mehrgarh]]&lt;ref name=&quot;vipul&quot; /&gt; and the Bronze Age [[Indus Valley Civilisation]] (2800â€“1800 BC) at [[Harappa]] and [[Mohenjo-Daro]].&lt;ref name=&quot;Arnett2006&quot;&gt;{{cite book|title=India Unveiled |url=https://books.google.com/books?id=Tmn91va2e4UC&amp;pg=PT180|author=Robert Arnett|publisher=Atman Press|accessdate=23 December 2011|date=15 July 2006|isbn=978-0-9652900-4-3|pages=180â€“}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Mohenjo-Daro|url=http://www.mnsu.edu/emuseum/archaeology/sites/middle_east/mohenjo_daro.html|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/web/20100601181841/http://www.mnsu.edu/emuseum/archaeology/sites/middle_east/mohenjo_daro.html|archivedate=22 December 2011|author=Meghan A. Porter|publisher=Minnesota State University|accessdate=15 January 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt;

The [[Vedic period|Vedic Civilization]] (1500â€“500 BC), characterised by [[Indo-Aryan peoples|Indo-Aryan]] culture, laid the foundations of [[Hinduism]], which would become well established in the region.&lt;ref name=&quot;guide&quot;&gt;{{cite book|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=WJMlW-zDE14C |title=Pakistan: a primary source cultural guide |publisher=The Rosen Publishing Group Inc |accessdate=23 October 2011|pages=58â€“59,100â€“102|author=Marian Rengel|location=New York, NY|isbn=0-8239-4001-2|year=2004}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.britannica.com/EBchecked/topic/503627/Rigveda|title=Britannica Online â€“ Rigveda|publisher=EncyclopÃ¦dia Britannica|accessdate=16 December 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt; [[Multan]] was an important Hindu pilgrimage centre.&lt;ref name=&quot;taxila&quot; /&gt; The Vedic civilisation flourished in the ancient [[Gandhara|GandhÄ?ran]] city of Taká¹£aÅ›ilÄ?, now [[Taxila]] in Punjab.&lt;ref name=&quot;vipul&quot;&gt;{{cite book |author=Vipul Singh|title=The Pearson Indian History Manual for the UPSC Civil Services Preliminary Examination|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=wsiXwh_tIGkC&amp;printsec=frontcover#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false|accessdate=28 December 2011|publisher=Dorling Kindesley, licensees of Pearson Education India|isbn=81-317-1753-4|pages=3â€“4, 15, 88â€“90, 152, 162|year=2008}}&lt;/ref&gt; Successive ancient empires and kingdoms ruled the region: the Persian [[Achaemenid Empire]] around 519 BC), [[Alexander the Great]]'s empire in 326 BC&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|title=Government Leaders, Military Rulers, and Political Activists|url=https://books.google.com/?id=vSwi2TYabS4C&amp;pg=PA7&amp;dq=alexander+founded+empire+in+india+327#v=onepage&amp;q=alexander%20founded%20empire%20in%20india%20327%3F&amp;f=false|publisher=The Oryx Press|accessdate=15 April 2012|editor=David W. del Testa|year=2001|location=Westport, Connecticut|isbn=1-57356-153-3|page=7}}&lt;/ref&gt;  and the [[Maurya Empire]] founded by [[Chandragupta Maurya]] and extended by [[Ashoka the Great]] until 185 BC.&lt;ref name=&quot;vipul&quot; /&gt; The [[Indo-Greek Kingdom]] founded by [[Demetrius I of Bactria|Demetrius of Bactria]] (180â€“165 BC) included Gandhara and Punjab and reached its greatest extent under [[Menander I|Menander]] (165â€“150 BC), prospering the [[Greco-Buddhism|Greco-Buddhist]] culture in the region.&lt;ref name=&quot;vipul&quot; /&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Guide to Historic Taxila|url=http://www.heritage.gov.pk/html_Pages/guide_to_historic_taxila.htm|author=Ahmad Hasan Dani|publisher=The National Fund for Cultural Heritage|accessdate=15 January 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt; Taxila had one of the earliest universities and centres of higher education in the world.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|author=Joseph Needham|title=A selection from the writings of Joseph Needham|year=1994|publisher=McFarland &amp; Co|isbn=978-0-89950-903-7|quote=When the men of Alexander the Great came to Taxila in India in the fourth century BC they found a university there the like of which had not been seen in Greece, a university which taught the three Vedas and the eighteen accomplishments and was still existing when the Chinese pilgrim Fa-Hsien went there about AD 400.|page=24}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|author1=Hermann Kulke|author2=Dietmar Rothermund|title=A History of India|year=2004|publisher=Routledge|isbn=0-415-32919-1|quote=In the early centuries the centre of Buddhist scholarship was the University of Taxila.|page=157}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite journal|author1=Balakrishnan Muniapan|author2=Junaid M. Shaikh|title=Lessons in corporate governance from Kautilya's Arthashastra in ancient India|journal=World Review of Entrepreneurship, Management and Sustainable Development 2007|year=2007|volume=3|issue=1|pages=50â€“61|doi=10.1504/WREMSD.2007.012130}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|author=Radha Kumud Mookerji|title=Ancient Indian Education: Brahmanical and Buddhist|edition=2nd|year=1951|origyear=reprint 1989|publisher=Motilal Banarsidass|isbn=81-208-0423-6|pages=478â€“479}}&lt;/ref&gt;

The Medieval period (642â€“1219 AD) is defined by the spread of [[Islam]] in the region. During this period, [[Sufi]] [[Dawah|missionaries]] played a pivotal role in converting a majority of the regional Buddhist and Hindu population to Islam.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|author=Ira Marvin Lapidus|title=A history of Islamic societies|year=2002|publisher=Cambridge University Press|isbn=0-521-77933-2|pages=382â€“384}}&lt;/ref&gt; The [[Rai Dynasty]] (489â€“632 AD) of [[Sindh]], at its zenith, ruled this region and the surrounding territories.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|author=Andre Wink|title=Al Hind the Making of the Indo Islamic World|year=1996|publisher=Brill Academic Publishers|isbn=90-04-09249-8|page=152}}&lt;/ref&gt; The [[Pala Dynasty]] was the last Buddhist empire that under [[Dharmapala (emperor)|Dharampala]] and [[Devapala (Pala dynasty)|Devapala]] stretched across [[South Asia]] from what is now [[Bangladesh]] through [[Northern India]] to Pakistan.

The Arab conqueror [[Muhammad bin Qasim]] conquered [[Indus valley]] from [[Sindh]] to [[Multan]] in southern [[Punjab (Pakistan)|Punjab]] in 711 AD.&lt;ref name=&quot;Information of Pakistan&quot; /&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://scroll.in/article/721012/the-curious-case-of-dressing-up-an-8th-century-arab-as-the-true-founder-of-pakistan|title= Why some in Pakistan want to replace Jinnah as the founder of the country with an 8th-century Arab }}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.dawn.com/news/735610/figuring-qasim-how-pakistan-was-won|title=Figuring Qasim: How Pakistan was won|publisher=Dawn|accessdate=19 February 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.dawn.com/news/1175127/the-first-pakistani|title=The first Pakistani?|publisher=Dawn|accessdate=19 February 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.dawn.com/news/1098562/muhammad-bin-qasim-predator-or-preacher|title=Muhammad Bin Qasim: Predator or preacher?|publisher=Dawn|accessdate=19 February 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt; The Pakistan government's official chronology identifies this as the point where the &quot;foundation&quot; of Pakistan was laid.&lt;ref name=&quot;Information of Pakistan&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.infopak.gov.pk/History.aspx|title= History in Chronological Order |publisher=Ministry of Information and Broadcasting, Government of Pakistan|accessdate=15 January 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.dawn.com/news/1125484|title=What is the most blatant lie taught through Pakistan textbooks?|author=Rubina Saigol|year=2014|publisher=Herald|accessdate=14 August 2014}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.dawn.com/news/1164469|title=A case for Gandhara|author=Shazia Rafi|year=2015|publisher=Dawn|accessdate=19 February 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt; This conquest set the stage for the [[Muslim conquest in the Indian subcontinent|rule of several successive Muslim empires]] in the region, including the [[Ghaznavid Empire]] (975â€“1187 AD), the [[Ghorid]] Kingdom and the [[Delhi Sultanate]] (1206â€“1526 AD). The [[Lodi dynasty]], the last of the Delhi Sultanate, was replaced by the [[Mughal Empire]] (1526â€“1857 AD). The Mughals introduced Persian literature and high culture, establishing the roots of [[Indo-Persian culture]] in the region.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|author=Robert L. Canfield|url=https://books.google.com/?id=g3JhKNSk8tQC&amp;pg=PA1#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false|title=Turko-Persia in historical perspective|year=2002|publisher=Cambridge University Press|accessdate=28 December 2011|pages=4â€“21|isbn=978-0-521-52291-5}}&lt;/ref&gt; In the early 16th century, the region remained under the [[Mughal Empire]] ruled by [[Indian Emperor|Muslim emperors]].&lt;ref name=&quot;Cambridge University Press&quot; /&gt; By the early 18th century, the increasing European influence slowly disintegrated the [[Mughal Empire|empire]] as the lines between commercial and political dominance were increasingly blurred.&lt;ref name=&quot;Cambridge University Press&quot; /&gt;

[[File:Weeks Edwin Lord An Open-Air Restaurant Lahore.jpg|thumb|[[Edwin Lord Weeks]] illustration of an open-air restaurant near [[Wazir Khan Mosque]], [[Lahore]].]]
During this time, the English [[East India Company]] had established coastal outposts.&lt;ref name=&quot;Cambridge University Press&quot;&gt;Metcalf, B.; Metcalf, T. R. (9 October 2006), A Concise History of Modern India (2nd ed.), Cambridge University Press, ISBN 978-0-521-68225-1&lt;/ref&gt; Control over the seas, greater resources, technology, and military [[Force protection|force projection]] by [[East India Company]] of [[British Empire]] led it to increasingly flex its military muscle; a factor that was crucial in allowing the [[Company rule in India|Company to gain control]] over the [[Indian Subcontinent|subcontinent]] by 1765 and sidelining the European competitors.&lt;ref&gt;Asher, C. B.; Talbot, C (1 January 2008), India Before Europe (1st ed.), Cambridge University Press, ISBN 978-0-521-51750-8&lt;/ref&gt; Expanding access beyond [[Bengal]] and the subsequent increased strength and size of its [[British Army|army]] enabled it to annex or subdue most of region by the 1820s.&lt;ref name=&quot;Cambridge University Press&quot; /&gt; To many historians, this marked the starting of region's colonial period.&lt;ref name=&quot;Cambridge University Press&quot; /&gt;  By this time, with its economic power severely curtailed by the [[British parliament]] and itself effectively made an arm of British administration, the [[Company rule in India|Company]] began to more consciously enter non-economic arenas such as education, social reform, and culture.&lt;ref name=&quot;Cambridge University Press&quot; /&gt; Such reforms included the enforcement of [[English Education Act 1835|English Education Act]] in 1835 and the introduction of the [[Indian Civil Service (British India)|Indian Civil Service]] (ICS).&lt;ref&gt;Jalal, Ayesha (1994). The Sole Spokesman: Jinnah, the Muslim League and the Demand for Pakistan. Cambridge UK: Cambridge South Asian Studies.&lt;/ref&gt; Traditional [[Madrasa|''madrasahs'']] â€“ primary institutions of higher learning for [[Indian Muslims|Muslims]] in the [[Indian Subcontinent|subcontinent]] â€“ were no longer supported by the [[English rule of India|English Crown]], and nearly all of the [[Madrasa|''madrasahs'']] lost their financial endowment.&lt;ref&gt;Stephen Evans, &quot;Macaulay's minute revisited: Colonial language policy in nineteenth-century India,&quot; Journal of Multilingual and Multicultural Development (2002) 23#4 pp. 260â€“281&lt;/ref&gt;

===Colonial period===
{{main article|Pakistan Movement|Aligarh Movement|British Raj}}

{{multiple image
 |align=right
