}}
The gradual decline of the [[Mughal Empire]] in the early 18th century enabled the [[Sikh Empire]]'s influence to control larger areas until the [[United Kingdom|British]] [[British East India Company|East-India Company]] gained ascendancy over the [[Indian subcontinent]].&lt;ref name=&quot;congress&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://lcweb2.loc.gov/frd/cs/profiles/Pakistan.pdf|title=Country Profile: Pakistan|publisher=Library of Congress|format=PDF|year=2005|pages=2, 3, 6, 8|accessdate=28 December 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt; The [[Indian Rebellion of 1857|rebellion]] in 1857 (or [[Sepoy mutiny]]) was the region's major armed struggle against the [[British Empire]] and [[Queen Victoria]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.thenagain.info/webchron/india/sepoyreb.html|title=Sepoy Rebellion: 1857|publisher=Thenagain.info|date=12 September 2003|accessdate=19 December 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt; Divergence in the [[Hinduâ€“Islamic relations|relationship]] between [[Hinduism]] and [[Islam]] created a major rift in [[British Indian Empire|British India]]; thus instigating racially motivated [[Violence against Muslims in India|religious violence in India]].&lt;ref name=&quot;Online Encyclopedia of Mass Violence&quot;&gt;{{cite web|last1=Markovits|first1=Claude|title=India from 1900 to 1947|url=http://www.massviolence.org/india-from-1900-to-1947?cs=print|website=http://www.massviolence.org|publisher=Online Encyclopedia of Mass Violence|accessdate=2 February 2015|date=2 November 2007}}&lt;/ref&gt; The [[Hindiâ€“Urdu controversy|language controversy]] further escalated the tensions between Hindus and Muslims.&lt;ref name=&quot;Sang-i MÄ«l Pablikeshanz&quot;&gt;{{cite book|last=AkÌ²hÌ²tar|first=AltÄ?f á¸¤usain á¸¤Ä?lÄ« ; TalkÌ²hÌ²Ä«á¹£, Salim|title=á¸¤ayÄ?t-i jÄ?ved|date=1993|publisher=Sang-i MÄ«l Pablikeshanz|location=LÄ?haur|isbn=9693501861|url=}}&lt;/ref&gt; The [[Bengali Renaissance|Hindu renaissance]] witnessed the awakening of intellectualism in traditional [[Hinduism]] and saw the emergence of more assertive influence in the social and political spheres in British India.&lt;ref name=&quot;State University of New York Press&quot;&gt;{{cite book|last1=Coward|first1=ed. by Harold G.|title=Modern Indian responses to religious pluralism|date=1987|publisher=State University of New York Press|location=Albany, N.Y.|isbn=0887065724}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Sarup &amp; Sons&quot;&gt;{{cite book|last1=Sarkar|first1=R.N.|title=Islam related Naipual [sic]|date=2006|publisher=Sarup &amp; Sons|location=New Delhi|isbn=8176256935|edition=1st}}&lt;/ref&gt; [[Aligarh Movement|Intellectual movement]] to counter the [[Bengali Renaissance|Hindu renaissance]] was led by Sir [[Sir Syed Ahmed Khan|Syed Ahmad Khan]] who helped founding the [[All-India Muslim League]] in 1901 and envisioned as well as advocated for the [[two-nation theory]].&lt;ref name=&quot;congress&quot; /&gt; In contrast to the [[Indian National Congress|Indian Congress]]'s [[Anti-British sentiment|anti-British]] efforts, the [[All-India Muslim League|Muslim League]] was a [[Anglomania|pro-British]] whose political program [[British heritage of Pakistan|inherited the British values]] that would shape Pakistan's future [[Pakistani society|civil society]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|last1=Qureshi|first1=M. Naeem|title=Pan-Islam in British Indian politics : a study of the Khilafat movement, 1918 â€“ 1924|date=1999|publisher=Brill|location=Leiden [u.a.]|isbn=978-9004113718}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;byQureshi, M. Naeem ''Pan-Islam in British Indian politics'', pp. 57,245 by M.Naeem Qureshi&lt;/ref&gt; In events during [[World War I]], [[British Intelligence]] foiled an [[anti-English]] [[Hinduâ€“German Conspiracy|conspiracy]] involving the nexus of [[Indian National Congress|Congress]] and the [[German Empire]].&lt;ref&gt;Chirol, Valentine (2006), Indian Unrest, Adamant Media Corporation, ISBN 0-543-94122-1.&lt;/ref&gt; The largely non-violent independence struggle led by the Indian Congress engaged millions of protesters in mass campaigns of [[Quit India Movement|civil disobedience]] in the 1920s and 1930s against the [[British Empire]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|author=John Farndon|title=Concise encyclopaedia|date=1 March 1999|publisher=Dorling Kindersley Limited|isbn=0-7513-5911-4|page=455}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Lak2008&quot;&gt;{{cite book|title=India express: the future of a new superpower|date=4 March 2008|publisher=Viking Canada|isbn=978-0-670-06484-7|author=Daniel Lak|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=YrDtAAAAMAAJ|accessdate=14 March 2012|page=113}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Brookings Institution Press&quot;&gt;{{cite book|last1=Cohen|first1=Stephen Philip|title=The idea of Pakistan|date=2004|publisher=Brookings Institution Press|location=Washington, D.C.|isbn=0815797613|edition=1st pbk.}}&lt;/ref&gt;

[[File:Old-sikh-man-carrying-wife1947.jpg|thumb|left|250px|Over 10 million people were uprooted from their homeland and travelled on foot, bullock carts and trains to their promised new home during the [[Partition of India]]. During the partition between 200,000 to 500,000 people were killed in the retributive genocide.&lt;ref name=&quot;migrate2&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://faculty.washington.edu/brass/Partition.pdf|title=The partition of India and retributive genocide in the Punjab, 1946â€“47: means, methods, and purposes|accessdate=19 December 2006}}&lt;/ref&gt;]]
The [[All-India Muslim League|Muslim League]] slowly rose to mass popularity in the 1930s amid fears of under-representation and neglect of [[Indian Muslims|Muslims]] in [[Indian politics|politics]]. In his presidential address of 29 December 1930, [[Muhammad Iqbal|Allama Iqbal]] called for &quot;the amalgamation of [[Northwestern India|North-West]] Muslim-majority Indian states&quot; consisting of [[Punjab, Pakistan|Punjab]], [[North-West Frontier Province (1901â€“55)|North-West Frontier Province]], [[Sindh|Sind]] and [[Baluchistan, Pakistan|Baluchistan]].&lt;ref name=&quot;iqbal1&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.columbia.edu/itc/mealac/pritchett/00islamlinks/txt_iqbal_1930.html|title=Sir Muhammad Iqbal's 1930 Presidential Address|work=Speeches, Writings, and Statements of Iqbal|accessdate=19 December 2006}}&lt;/ref&gt; [[Muhammad Ali Jinnah]], the [[founder of Pakistan]], greatly espoused the [[two-nation theory]] and led the Muslim League to adopt the [[Lahore Resolution]] of 1940, popularly known as the Pakistan Resolution.&lt;ref name=&quot;congress&quot; /&gt; In [[World War II]], [[Muhammad Ali Jinnah|Jinnah]] and [[British education|British educated]] [[Founding Fathers of Pakistan|founding fathers]] in the [[All-India Muslim League|Muslim League]] supported the [[United Kingdom]]'s [[United Kingdom in World War II|war efforts]], countering opposition against it whilst working towards [[Sir Syed]]'s [[Two-nation theory|vision]].&lt;ref name=&quot;Politact&quot;&gt;{{cite web|last1=Editorial work, no author.|title=Understanding Jinnah's Position on World War I and II Lessons to be learned|url=https://www.politact.com/global-security-studies/understanding-jinnahs-position-on-world-war-i-and-ii-lessons-to-be-learned.html|website=https://www.politact.coml|publisher=Politact|accessdate=3 February 2015|location=United Kingdom|date=5 January 2009}}&lt;/ref&gt;

As the [[1946 Cabinet Mission to India|cabinet mission]] failed in India, the [[United Kingdom|Great Britain]] announced the intentions to end its [[British Raj|''raj'']] in India in 1946â€“47.&lt;ref name=&quot;Academia Edu&quot;&gt;{{cite web|last1=Akram|first1=Wasim|title=Jinnah and cabinet Mission Plan|url=http://www.academia.edu/2146058/Jinnah_and_cabinet_Mission_Plan|website=http://www.academia.edu|publisher=Academia Edu|accessdate=3 February 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt; [[Indian nationalism|Nationalists]] in British India â€“ including [[Jawaharlal Nehru]] and [[Abul Kalam Azad]] of [[Indian National Congress|Congress]], [[Muhammad Ali Jinnah|Jinnah]] of [[All-India Muslim League|Muslim League]], and [[Master Tara Singh]] representing the Sikhsâ€”agreed to the proposed terms of transfer of power and independence in June 1947.&lt;ref name=&quot;jinnah&quot;&gt;{{cite book|author=Stanley Wolpert|title=Jinnah of Pakistan|publisher=Oxford University Press|year=2002|isbn=0-19-577462-0|pages=306â€“332}}&lt;/ref&gt; As the [[United Kingdom]] agreed upon [[Partition of India|partitioning of India]] in 1947, the modern state of Pakistan was established on [[Fourteenth of August|14 August 1947]] {{small|(27th of [[Ramadan]] in 1366 of the [[Islamic Calendar]])}} in amalgamating the [[Islam in India|Muslim-majority]] eastern and northwestern regions of [[British India]].&lt;ref name=&quot;Brookings Institution Press&quot; /&gt; It comprised the provinces of [[Balochistan (Pakistan)|Balochistan]], [[East Bengal]], the [[North-West Frontier Province (1901â€“1955)|North-West Frontier Province]], [[Punjab (Pakistan)|West Punjab]] and [[Sindh]]; thus forming Pakistan.&lt;ref name=&quot;congress&quot; /&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;jinnah&quot; /&gt; The [[Partition of India|partitioning]] of [[Punjab region|Punjab]] and [[Bengal]] led to the series of violent communal riots across [[India and Pakistan]]; millions of Muslims moved to Pakistan and millions of Hindus and Sikhs moved to India.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|author=William D. Rubinstein|title=Genocide: a history.|year=2004|publisher=Pearson Longman Publishers|isbn=0-582-50601-8|page=270}}&lt;/ref&gt; Dispute over [[Jammu and Kashmir (princely state)|Jammu and Kashmir]] led to the [[First Kashmir War]] in 1948.&lt;ref name=&quot;Bhaumik1996&quot;&gt;{{cite book|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=iftjFki3fhYC&amp;pg=PA6|title=Insurgent Crossfire: North-East India|publisher=Lancer Publishers|accessdate=15 April 2012|author=Subir Bhaumik|year=1996|isbn=978-1-897829-12-7|page=6}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.mtholyoke.edu/acad/intrel/uncom1.htm|title=Resolution adopted by the United Nations Commission for India and Pakistan|publisher=Mount Holyoke College|accessdate=19 January 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt;

===Independence and modern Pakistan===
{{Main article|Dominion of Pakistan|History of Pakistan}}
[[File:Pakistan.ogv|thumb|left|The [[United States|American]] [[Central Intelligence Agency|CIA]] film on Pakistan made in 1950 examines the history and geography of Pakistan.]]
{{quote box
|width     = 25em
|border    = 1px
|align     = right
|bgcolor   = #c6dbf7
|halign    = left
|quote     = &quot;You are free; you are free to go to your temples, you are free to go to your mosques or to any other place or worship in this State of Pakistan. You may belong to any religion or caste or creed â€“ that has nothing to do with the business of the State.&quot; 
|source    =â€”[[Muhammad Ali Jinnah]]'s first speech to the Constituent Assembly of Pakistan&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.columbia.edu/itc/mealac/pritchett/00islamlinks/txt_jinnah_assembly_1947.html|title=Muhammad Ali Jinnah's first Presidential Address to the Constituent Assembly of Pakistan (August 11, 1947)|work=JSpeech|accessdate=1 March 2016}}&lt;/ref&gt;
}}
After [[Creation of Pakistan|independence]] and the [[Partition of India|partition]] of [[Presidencies and provinces of British India|India]] in 1947, [[Muhammad Ali Jinnah]], the [[Party chair|President]] of [[All-India Muslim League|Muslim League]], became nation's first [[Governor-General of Pakistan|Governor-General]] as well as first [[Speaker of the National Assembly of Pakistan|President-Speaker]] of the [[Parliament of Pakistan|Parliament]].&lt;ref name=&quot;king-queen&quot; /&gt; Meanwhile, Pakistan's [[Founding Fathers of Pakistan|founding fathers]] agreed upon appointing [[Liaquat Ali Khan]], the [[Secretary General|secretary-general]] of the [[All-India Muslim League|party]], the nation's [[List of Prime Ministers of Pakistan|first]] [[Prime Ministers of Pakistan|Prime Minister]]. With [[Dominion of Pakistan|dominion status]] in the [[Commonwealth of Nations]], independent Pakistan had two [[British Monarchy|British monarchs]] before it became a republic.&lt;ref name=&quot;king-queen&quot;&gt;{{cite web|title=Pakistan|url=http://www.worldstatesmen.org/Pakistan.htm|work=worldstatesmen.org|accessdate=27 December 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt;

MaulÄ?nÄ? [[Shabbir Ahmad Usmani|ShabbÄ«r Ahmad UsmÄ?n]]Ä«, a respected DeobandÄ« Ê¿''Ä?lim'' (scholar) who occupied the position of Shaykh al-IslÄ?m in Pakistan in 1949, and [[Abul A'la Maududi|Maulana Mawdud]]&lt;nowiki/&gt;i of [[Jamaat-e-Islami|JamÄ?Ê¿at-i IslÄ?mÄ«]] played a pivotal role in the demand for an Islamic constitution. [[Abul A'la Maududi|MawdÅ«dÄ«]] demanded that the Constituent Assembly make an explicit declaration affirming the &quot;supreme sovereignty of God&quot; and the supremacy of the ''sharÄ«Ê¿ah'' in Pakistan.&lt;ref name=&quot;:0&quot;&gt;Hussain, Rizwan . &quot;Pakistan.&quot; In ''The Oxford Encyclopedia of the Islamic World''. ''Oxford Islamic Studies Online''. 04-Apr-2016. &lt;&lt;nowiki&gt;http://www.oxfordislamicstudies.com/article/opr/t236/e0616&lt;/nowiki&gt;&gt;.
&lt;/ref&gt;

A significant result of the efforts of the JamÄ?Ê¿at-i IslÄ?mÄ« and the Ê¿''ulamÄ?Ê¿'' was the passage of the [[Objectives Resolution]] in March 1949. The Objectives Resolution, which [[Liaquat Ali Khan]] called the second most important step in Pakistan's history, declared that &quot;sovereignty over the entire universe belongs to God Almighty alone and the authority which He has delegated to the State of Pakistan through its people for being exercised within the limits prescribed by Him is a sacred trust&quot;. The Objectives Resolution has been incorporated as a preamble to the constitutions of 1956, 1962, and 1973.&lt;ref name=&quot;:0&quot; /&gt;

[[Democracy in Pakistan|Democracy]] was stalled by the [[1958 Pakistani coup d'Ã©tat|martial law]] enforced by [[President of Pakistan|President]] [[Iskander Mirza]] who was [[Two man rule|replaced]] by [[Army Chief of Staff (Pakistan)|army chief]], General [[Ayub Khan (President of Pakistan)|Ayub Khan]]. Forming [[Constitution of Pakistan of 1962|presidential system]] in 1962, the country experienced [[Economic boom|exceptional growth]] until a [[Indo-Pakistani War of 1965|second war]] with [[India]] in 1965 which led to economic downfall and wide-scale [[Tashkent Agreement|public disapproval]] in 1967.&lt;ref name=&quot;Wynbrandt2009&quot;&gt;{{cite book|title=A brief history of Pakistan|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=xQGwgJnCPZgC&amp;pg=PA197|accessdate=27 December 2011|year=2009|publisher=Infobase Publishing|isbn=978-0-8160-6184-6|pages=190â€“197|author=James Wynbrandt}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;ChowdhuryMahmud2008&quot;&gt;{{cite book|title=Handbook on the South Asian economies|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=VBl8XLiRo3IC&amp;pg=PA72|accessdate=27 December 2011|publisher=Edward Elgar Publishing|author1=Anis Chowdhury|author2=Wahiduddin Mahmud|year=2008|isbn=978-1-84376-988-0|pages=72â€“75}}&lt;/ref&gt; [[Legal Framework Order, 1970|Consolidating]] the control from [[Ayub Khan (President of Pakistan)|Ayub Khan]] in 1969, President [[Yahya Khan]] had to deal with a devastating [[1970 Bhola cyclone|cyclone]] which caused 500,000 deaths in [[East Pakistan]].&lt;ref name=&quot;Mission with a Difference&quot;&gt;{{cite book|title=Mission with a Difference|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=PZ62tP_5a2AC&amp;pg=PA17|accessdate=13 March 2012|publisher=Lancer Publishers|page=17|id=GGKEY:KGWAHUGNPY9}}&lt;/ref&gt;
[[File:Ayubkhanandbhutto.jpg|thumb|upright|Signing of [[Tashkent Declaration]] to end [[Indo-Pakistani War of 1965|hostilities]] with [[India]] in 1965 in [[Tashkent]], [[Union of Soviet Socialist Republics (USSR)|USSR]], by President [[Field Marshal Ayub Khan|Ayub]] alongside [[Zulfikar Ali Bhutto|Bhutto]] (center) and [[Aziz Ahmed]] (left). ]]

In 1970, Pakistan held its [[Pakistani general election, 1970|first democratic elections]] since independence, that were meant to mark a transition from [[Legal Framework Order, 1970|military rule]] to democracy, but after the East Pakistani [[Awami League]] won against [[Pakistan Peoples Party]] (PPP); Yahya Khan and [[Establishment (Pakistan)|military establishment]] refused to hand over power.&lt;ref name=&quot;Jones&quot;&gt;{{cite book|author=Adam Jones|title=Genocide: A Comprehensive Introduction|year=2004|publisher=Routledge|isbn=978-0-415-35384-7|page=420}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Jones&quot; /&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Jahan&quot;&gt;{{cite book|author=R. Jahan|title=Teaching about genocide: issues, approaches, and resources|year=2004|publisher=Information Age Publishing|isbn=978-1-59311-074-1|pages=147â€“148|editor=Samuel Totten}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Jahan&quot; /&gt; [[Operation Searchlight]], a military crackdown on the Bengali nationalist movement, led to a declaration of independence and the waging of [[Bangladesh Liberation War|a war of liberation]] by the Bengali [[Mukti Bahini]] forces in East Pakistan, with support from India.&lt;ref name=&quot;Jahan&quot; /&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;1971war&quot;&gt;{{Cite news|url=http://news.bbc.co.uk/hi/english/static/in_depth/south_asia/2002/india_pakistan/timeline/1971.stm|title=1971 war summary|year=2002|publisher=BBC|accessdate=16 March 2009}}&lt;/ref&gt; However, in West Pakistan the conflict was described as a [[civil war]] as opposed to War of Liberation.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite journal
  |title=Anatomy of Violence: Analysis of Civil War in East Pakistan in 1971|last=Bose|first=Sarmila|journal=[[Economic and Political Weekly]]|issn=2349-8846|volume=40|issue=41|year=2005|pages=4463â€“71|doi=|jstor=4417267|via=[[JSTOR]]|registration=y}}&lt;/ref&gt;

Independent researchers think that between 300,000 and 500,000 civilians died during this period while the Bangladesh government puts the figure of dead at three million,&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite web|url=http://www.bbc.com/news/world-asia-16207201|title=Bangladesh war: The article that changed history â€“ BBC News|website=BBC News|language=en-GB|access-date=2016-03-03}}&lt;/ref&gt; a number which is now universally regarded as excessively inflated.&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite book|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=PpPCBAAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PA216&amp;lpg=PA216&amp;dq=three+million+killed+1971+some+scholars+consider+to+be+inflated&amp;source=bl&amp;ots=xpdyLBfHf6&amp;sig=LFbx-N0yX9lKjD54laJoYZ9lGbM&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;ved=0ahUKEwjDkLGsk_nLAhWBMpQKHXa1DFsQ6AEINTAF#v=onepage&amp;q=three%20million%20killed%201971%20some%20scholars%20consider%20to%20be%20inflated&amp;f=false|title=The Longest August: The Unflinching Rivalry Between India and Pakistan|last=Hiro|first=Dilip|publisher=Nation Books|year=2015|isbn=9781568585031|location=|pages=216}}&lt;/ref&gt; Some academics such as Rudolph Russel and Rounaq Jahan accused both sides&lt;ref name=&quot;:5&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.hawaii.edu/powerkills/SOD.CHAP8.HTM|title=STATISTICS OF PAKISTAN'S DEMOCIDE|publisher=|accessdate=10 February 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt; of genocide whereas others such as Richard Sisson and Leo E. Rose believe there was no genocide.&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite book|title=The Genocide Debate: Politicians, Academics, and Victims|last=Beachler|first=Donald|publisher=Springer|year=2011|isbn=9780230337633|location=|pages=16}}&lt;/ref&gt; [[Preemptive strikes]] on [[India]] by the Pakistan's [[Pakistan Air Force|air force]], [[Pakistan Navy|navy]], and [[Pakistan Marines|marines]], in response to India's support for the insurgency in East Pakistan, sparked the [[Indo-Pakistani War of 1971|conventional war]] in 1971 which witnessed the Indian victory and [[East Pakistan]] gaining [[Independence of Bangladesh|independence]] as [[Bangladesh]].&lt;ref name=&quot;Jahan&quot; /&gt;

With Pakistan [[Instrument of Surrender (1971)|surrendering]] in the war, Yahya Khan was replaced by [[Zulfikar Ali Bhutto]] as [[President of Pakistan|President]]; the country worked towards promulgating [[Constitution of Pakistan|constitution]] and putting the country on roads of [[Democracy in Pakistan|democracy]]. [[Democracy in Pakistan|Democratic]] rule resumed from 1972 to 1977â€“ an era of self-[[consciousness]], intellectual [[Socialism in Pakistan|leftism]], [[Nationalism in Pakistan|nationalism]], and nationwide reconstruction.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|author=M. Zafar|url=http://www.defencejournal.com/2001/september/arena.htm|title=How Pakistan Army moved into the Political Arena|work=Defence Journal|accessdate=15 March 2009}}&lt;/ref&gt; During this period, Pakistan embarked on ambitiously developing the [[Pakistan and its Nuclear Detterent Program|nuclear deterrence]] in 1972 in [[Mutually assured destruction|a view to prevent]] any [[Foreign interventionism|foreign invasion]]; the country's [[KANUPP|first]] [[nuclear power plant]] was inaugurated, also the same year.&lt;ref name=&quot;&amp;quot;Strategic Studies&amp;quot;&quot;&gt;{{cite web|title=Bhutto was father of Pakistan's Atom Bomb Programme|url=http://www.iiss.org/whats-new/iiss-in-the-press/press-coverage-2007/may-2007/bhutto-was-father-of-pakistani-bomb/?locale=en|work=International Institute for Strategic Studies|accessdate=19 December 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Hoodbhoy&quot;&gt;{{cite web|author=Pervez Amerali Hoodbhoy|authorlink=Pervaiz Hoodbhoy|title=Pakistan's nuclear bayonet|publisher=''[[The Herald (Pakistan)|The Herald]]''|date=23 January 2011|url=http://www.dawn.com/2011/02/16/herald-exclusive-pakistans-nuclear-bayonet.html|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/web/20110218212415/http://www.dawn.com/2011/02/16/herald-exclusive-pakistans-nuclear-bayonet.html|archivedate=18 February 2011|accessdate=9 September 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt; Accelerated in response to [[Smiling Buddha|first nuclear test]] by [[India]] in 1974, this [[Pakistan and Nuclear Weapons|crash program]] completed in 1979.&lt;ref name=&quot;Hoodbhoy&quot; /&gt; [[Democracy in Pakistan|Democracy]] ended with [[Operation Fair Play|a military coup]] in 1977 against the [[Socialism in Pakistan|leftist]] [[Pakistan Peoples Party|PPP]], which saw General [[General Zia-ul-Haq|Zia-ul-Haq]] become the [[President of Pakistan|president]] in 1978. From 1977 to 1988, President [[General Zia-ul-Haq|Zia]]'s [[Corporate sector of Pakistan|corporatisation]] and [[Islamization of Economy|economic Islamisation]] initiatives led to Pakistan becoming one of the fastest-growing economies in South Asia.&lt;ref name=&quot;Sushil Khanna&quot;&gt;{{cite web|author=Sushil Khanna|title=The Crisis in the Pakistan Economy|url=http://www.revolutionarydemocracy.org/rdv8n1/pakistan.htm|publisher=Revolutionary Democracy|accessdate=16 November 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt; While consolidating the [[Pakistan and its Nuclear Detterent Program|nuclear development]], increasing [[Zia-ul-Haq's Islamization|Islamization]],&lt;ref name=&quot;World Scientific&quot;&gt;{{cite book| title =State and Secularism: Perspectives from Asia |page=202| publisher =World Scientific | isbn=978-981-4282-37-6|author1=Michael Heng Siam-Heng|author2=Ten Chin Liew|year=2010|location=Singapore|url=https://books.google.com/?id=9cCtsWb9hoYC&amp;pg=PA202&amp;dq=zia+ul+haq#v=onepage&amp;q=zia%20ul%20haq&amp;f=false|accessdate=28 December 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt; and the rise of homegrown [[Conservatism in Pakistan|conservative]] [[philosophy]], Pakistan helped subsidize and distribute U.S. [[Operation Cyclone|resources to factions]] of the [[Afghan mujahideen|mujahideen]] against the [[Soviet Union|USSR]]'s [[Soviet war in Afghanistan|intervention]] in [[Communist Afghanistan|communist]] [[Afghanistan]].&lt;ref name=&quot;Steve Coll: Ghost Wars&quot;&gt;{{cite book |author=Steve Coll|authorlink=Steve Coll|title=Ghost Wars: The Secret History of the CIA, Afghanistan, and Bin Laden, from the Soviet Invasion to September 10, 2001|edition=23 February 2004|page=720|publisher=Penguin Press HC|isbn=978-1-59420-007-6}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Westad2005&quot;&gt;{{cite book|author=Odd Arne Westad|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=QhFZHRpQdu4C&amp;pg=PT343|title=The global Cold War: third world interventions and the making of our times|accessdate=22 January 2012|year=2005|publisher=Cambridge University Press|isbn=978-0-521-85364-4|pages=348â€“358}}&lt;/ref&gt;

[[Death of Muhammad Zia-ul-Haq|President Zia died]] in a [[plane crash]] in 1988, and [[Benazir Bhutto]], daughter of Zulfikar Ali Bhutto, was elected as the country's [[Women in Muslim societies|first female]] [[Prime Minister of Pakistan|Prime Minister]]. The [[Pakistan Peoples Party]] followed by conservative [[Pakistan Muslim League (N)]], and over the next decade whose two leaders fought for power, alternating in office while the country's situation worsened; economic indicators fell sharply, in contrast to the 1980s. This period is marked by prolonged [[Periods of stagflation in Pakistan|stagflation]], instability, [[Corruption in Pakistan|corruption]], [[Nationalism in Pakistan|nationalism]], [[Geostrategy|geopolitical]] rivalry with [[India]], and the clash of [[Socialism in Pakistan|left wing]]-[[Conservatism in Pakistan|right wing]] ideologies.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|author=Marie Chene|url=http://www.u4.no/publications/overview-of-corruption-in-pakistan/|title=Overview of corruption in Pakistan|work=Anti Corruption Resource Centre|accessdate=23 December 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite journal|author=Ishrat Husain|title=Pakistan &amp; Afghanistan: Domestic Pressures and Regional Threats : The Role of Politics in Pakistan's Economy|url=http://jia.sipa.columbia.edu/role-politics-pakistans-economy-0|journal=Journal of International Affairs|year=2009|volume=63|issue=1|pages=1â€“18}}&lt;/ref&gt; As [[Pakistan Muslim League (N)|PML(N)]] secured a [[supermajority]] in [[Pakistani general election, 1997|elections]] in 1997, Sharif authorised the [[List of nuclear weapons tests of Pakistan|nuclear testings]] (See:''[[Chagai-I]]'' and ''[[Chagai-II]]''), as a [[tit-for-tat|retaliation]] to [[Pokhran-II|second nuclear tests]] ordered by [[India]], led by [[Prime Minister of India|Prime Minister]] [[Atal Bihari Vajpayee]] in May 1998.&lt;ref name=&quot;Stanford University Press&quot;&gt;{{cite book|last1=Khan|first1=Feroz Hassan|title=Eating grass : the making of the Pakistani bomb|date=2012|publisher=Stanford University Press|location=Stanford, Calif.|isbn=9780804776004}}&lt;/ref&gt;

[[File:Musharaff and Bush in Islamabad.jpeg|left|thumb|[[President of the United States|President]] [[George W. Bush|Bush]] meets with President [[Pervez Musharraf|Musharraf]] in [[Islamabad]] during his 2006 visit to Pakistan.]]
Military tension between the two countries in the [[Kargil district]] led to the [[Kargil War]] of 1999, and a turbulence in [[Civil-military relations|civic-military relations]] allowed General [[Pervez Musharraf]] took over through a [[1999 Pakistani coup d'Ã©tat|bloodless coup d'Ã©tat]].&lt;ref name=&quot;kargil&quot;&gt;{{Cite news|title=India launches Kashmir air attack|date=26 May 1999|publisher=BBC News|url=http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/world/south_asia/352995.stm|accessdate=5 August 2008}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;1999coup&quot;&gt;{{Cite news|url=http://news.bbc.co.uk/2/hi/south_asia/472968.stm|title=Pakistan after the coup: Special report|date=12 October 2000|accessdate=17 March 2009|publisher=BBC}}&lt;/ref&gt; Musharraf governed Pakistan as [[head of government|chief executive]] from 1999 to 2001 and as President from 2001 to 2008â€” a period of [[Enlightened moderation|enlightenment]], social [[Liberal islam|liberalism]], extensive [[Economic reforms in Pakistan|economic reforms]],&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://web.worldbank.org/WBSITE/EXTERNAL/COUNTRIES/SOUTHASIAEXT/0,,contentMDK:20643510~menuPK:158937~pagePK:146736~piPK:146830~theSitePK:223547,00.html|title=Pakistan Among Top 10 Reformers|work=World Bank|date=12 September 2005|accessdate=23 December 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt; and [[Pakistan's role in the War on Terror|direct involvement]] in the U.S.-led [[War on Terrorism|war on terrorism]]. When the [[National Assembly (Pakistan)|National Assembly]] historically completed its first full five-year term on 15 November 2007, the new elections were called by the [[Election Commission of Pakistan|Election Commission]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Performance of 12th NationalAssembly of Pakistan-|url=http://www.pildat.org/Publications/publication/Democracy%26LegStr/5Yearsof12thNationalAssemblyofPakistan-CitizensReport.pdf|work=Pakistan Institute of Legislative Development and Transperency|format=PDF|page=5|accessdate=23 December 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt; After the [[assassination of Benazir Bhutto|assassination]] of [[Benazir Bhutto]] in 2007, the [[Pakistan Peoples Party|PPP]] secured [[Majority|largest votes]] in the [[Pakistani general election, 2008|elections]] of 2008, appointing party member [[Yousaf Raza Gillani]] as Prime Minister.&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite news|url=http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/world/south_asia/7312116.stm|title=New Pakistan PM Gillani sworn in|date=25 March 2008|accessdate=17 March 2009|publisher=BBC}}&lt;/ref&gt; Threatened with facing [[Movement to impeach Pervez Musharraf|impeachment]], President Musharraf resigned on 18 August 2008, and was succeeded by [[Asif Ali Zardari]].&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite news|url=http://afp.google.com/article/ALeqM5i9dz2ZxX6MRv5ZOKT4cx4-1O_qTQ|title=Zardari wins Pakistan presidential election: officials|date=5 September 2008|agency=AFP|accessdate=17 March 2009|deadurl=yes|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/20090707123120/http://afp.google.com/article/ALeqM5i9dz2ZxX6MRv5ZOKT4cx4-1O_qTQ|archivedate=7 July 2009}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite news|url=http://www.cnn.com/2008/WORLD/asiapcf/08/18/musharraf.address/|date=19 August 2008|accessdate=19 January 2010|title=Musharraf Exits, but Uncertainty Remains|work=The Washington Post|author=Candace Rondeaux}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;NEWSMUSH&quot;&gt;{{Cite news|author=Associated Press|url=http://www.foxnews.com/story/0,2933,405221,00.html|title=Pakistani President Musharraf Resigns Amid Impeachment Threats|publisher=Fox News|date=18 August 2008|accessdate=18 August 2008}}&lt;/ref&gt; Clashes with the [[Judiciary of Pakistan|judicature]] prompted [[Yousaf Raza Gillani|Gillani]]'s disqualification from the [[Parliament of Pakistan|Parliament]] and as the [[Prime Minister of Pakistan|Prime Minister]] in June 2012.&lt;ref name=&quot;thenews.com&quot;&gt;{{cite news|url=http://www.thenews.com.pk/article-55039-Gilani-disqualified-as-PM:-SC--|title=Gilani disqualified as PM: SC|newspaper=Daily The News International.com|accessdate=19 June 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt; By its own financial calculations, Pakistan's [[Pakistan's role in the War on Terror|involvement]] in the [[war on terrorism]] has cost up to ~$67.93 billion,&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Cost of War on Terror for Pakistan Economy|url=http://www.finance.gov.pk/survey/chapter_11/Special%20Section_1.pdf|work=Ministry of Finance, Pakistan.|format=PDF|accessdate=20 December 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|title=War on terror cost Pakistan $67.9 billion|url=http://www.dawn.com/2011/06/20/war-on-terror-cost-pakistan-679-billion.html|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/web/20120111172537/http://www.dawn.com/2011/06/20/war-on-terror-cost-pakistan-679-billion.html|archivedate=11 January 2012|accessdate=20 December 2011|newspaper=DAWN|date=20 June 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt; [[List of terrorist incidents in Pakistan since 2001|thousands of casualties]] and nearly 3&amp;nbsp;million displaced civilians.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=3.4&amp;nbsp;million displaced by Pakistan fighting|url=http://www.upi.com/Top_News/2009/05/30/34-million-displaced-by-Pakistan-fighting/UPI-68801243704876/|author=United Press International.|accessdate=24 July 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt; The [[Pakistani general election, 2013|general election]] held in 2013 saw the [[Pakistan Muslim League (N)|PML(N)]] almost achieve a [[supermajority]], following which [[Nawaz Sharif]] became elected as the [[Prime Minister of Pakistan|Prime Minister]], returning to the post for the third time after fourteen years, in a democratic transition.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|url=http://www.abc.net.au/news/2013-06-05/an-pervaz-sharif-officially-endorsed-as-pakistan27s-prime-mini/4735828|title=Nawaz Sharif sworn in as Pakistani PM|work=ABC|date=5 June 2013|accessdate=6 June 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt;

==Government and politics==
{{Main article|Government of Pakistan|Politics of Pakistan|Political history of Pakistan|Human rights in Pakistan}}
[[File:Parliament_House,Islamabad_by_Usman_Ghani.jpg|thumb|220px|right|[[Parliament of Pakistan|Parliament House]]]]
Pakistan is a [[Democracy in Pakistan|democratic]] [[Parliamentary republic|parliamentary]] [[Federal parliamentary republic|federal republic]] with [[Islam]] as the [[state religion]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://pakistani.org/pakistan/constitution/part1.html|title=Part I: &quot;Introductory&quot;|work=pakistani.org}}&lt;/ref&gt; The [[Constitution of Pakistan of 1956|first set]] was adopted in 1956 but suspended by Ayub Khan in 1958 who replaced it with the [[Constitution of Pakistan of 1962|second set]] in 1962.&lt;ref name=&quot;Brookings Institution Press&quot; /&gt; Complete and comprehensive [[Constitution of Pakistan|Constitution]] was adopted in 1973â€”suspended by [[Zia-ul-Haq]] in 1977 but reinstated in 1985â€”is the country's most important document, laying the foundations of the current government.&lt;ref name=&quot;ciafactbook&quot; /&gt; The Pakistani [[Establishment (Pakistan)|military establishment]] has played an influential role in mainstream politics throughout [[Political history of Pakistan|Pakistan's political history]].&lt;ref name=&quot;Brookings Institution Press&quot; /&gt; There were [[Military coups in Pakistan|military coups]] which resulted in imposition of [[Martial law in Pakistan|martial law]] and military commanders continued governing as de-facto presidents from [[1958 Pakistani coup d'Ã©tat|1958â€“1971]], [[1977 Pakistani coup d'Ã©tat|1977â€“1988]], and [[1999 Pakistani coup d'Ã©tat|1999â€“2008]].&lt;ref name=&quot;military&quot;&gt;{{Cite news|url=http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/world/south_asia/472953.stm|title=World: South Asia Pakistan's army and its history of politics|date=10 December 1999|accessdate=16 March 2009|publisher=BBC}}&lt;/ref&gt; As of now, Pakistan has a [[multi-party system|multi-party]] [[parliamentary system]] with clear [[Separation of powers|division of powers]] and [[Check and balance|responsibilities between branches of government]]. The first successful [[Pakistani general election, 2013|demonstrative transaction]] was held in May 2013. [[Politics in Pakistan|Politics]] in Pakistan is centered and dominated by the homegrown conceive [[social philosophy]], consisting the ideas of [[Socialism in Pakistan|socialism]], [[Conservatism in Pakistan|conservatism]], and the [[Third Way|third way]]. As of the [[Pakistani general election, 2013|general elections]] held in 2013, the three main dominated political parties in the country: the [[Centre-right politics|centre-right]] [[Conservatism in Pakistan|conservative]] [[Pakistan Muslim League (N)|Pakistan Muslim League-N]] (PML-N); the [[Centre-left politics|centre-left]] [[Socialism in Pakistan|socialist]] [[Pakistan Peoples Party]] (PPP); and the [[Centre politics|centrist]] and [[Third way politics|third-way]] [[Pakistan Tehreek-e-Insaf|Pakistan Movement for Justice]] (PTI) led by [[cricket]]er [[Imran Khan]].
* [[Head of State]]: The [[President of Pakistan|President]] who is elected by an [[Electoral College of Pakistan|Electoral College]] is the ceremonial head of the state and is the civilian [[commander-in-chief]] of the [[Pakistan Armed Forces]] (with [[Chairman Joint Chiefs of Staff Committee]] as its principal military adviser), but military appointments and key confirmations in the armed forces are made by the [[Prime minister of Pakistan|Prime Minister]] after reviewing the reports on their merit and performances. Almost all appointed officers in the [[Judiciary of Pakistan|judicature]], [[Pakistani military|military]], [[Chairman Joint Chiefs of Staff Committee|chairman joint chiefs]] and [[Joint Chiefs of Staff Committee|joint staff]], and legislatures require the executive confirmation from the [[Prime minister of Pakistan|Prime Minister]], whom the President must consult, by law. However, the powers to pardon and grant clemency vest with the [[President of Pakistan]].
* [[Legislative]]: The [[bicameral]] legislature comprises a 100-member [[Senate of Pakistan|Senate]] ([[Upper House|upper house]]) and a 342-member [[National Assembly of Pakistan|National Assembly]] ([[Lower House|lower house]]). [[Member of the National Assembly of Pakistan|Members]] of the [[National Assembly (Pakistan)|National Assembly]] are elected through the [[first-past-the-post]] system under [[universal adult suffrage]], representing electoral districts known as National Assembly [[Constituencies of Pakistan|constituencies]]. According to the constitution, the 70 seats reserved for women and religious minorities are allocated to the political parties according to their proportional representation. Senate members are elected by provincial legislators, with all of provinces have equal representation.
[[File:A night side view of Prime Minister's Secretariat Building.jpg|thumb|220px|left|[[Prime Minister's Secretariat (Pakistan)|Prime Minister's Secretariat]]]]
* [[Executive (government)|Executive]]: The [[Prime Minister of Pakistan|Prime Minister]] is usually the leader of the [[Majority rule|majority rule party]] or a coalition in the [[National Assembly (Pakistan)|National Assembly]]â€” the [[lower house]]. The [[Prime Minister of Pakistan|Prime Minister]] serves as the [[head of government]] and is designated to exercise as the country's [[chief executive]]. The [[Prime Minister of Pakistan|Prime Minister]] is responsible for appointing a [[Cabinet of Pakistan|cabinet]] consisting of ministers and advisers as well as running the government operations, taking and authorizing executive decisions, appointments and recommendations that require executive confirmation of the Prime Minister.
* [[List of provincial governments of Pakistan|Provincial governments]]: Each of the [[Four Provinces (Pakistan)|four province]] has a similar [[List of Chief Ministers in Pakistan|system of government]], with a [[Elections in Pakistan|directly elected]] [[:Category:Provincial Assemblies of Pakistan|Provincial Assembly]] in which the leader of the largest party or coalition is elected [[Chief Minister (Pakistan)|Chief Minister]]. Chief Ministers oversees the [[List of provincial governments of Pakistan|provincial governments]] and head the provincial cabinet, it is common in Pakistan to have different ruling parties or coalitions in each provinces. The provincial assemblies have power to make laws and approve provincial budget which is commonly presented by the provincial finance minister every fiscal year. [[List of Governors of Pakistan|Provincial governors]] who play role as the ceremonial head of province are appointed by the [[President of Pakistan|President]].&lt;ref name=&quot;ciafactbook&quot; /&gt;
[[File:Supreme Court of Pakistan,Islamabad by Usman Ghani.jpg|thumb|220px|right|[[Supreme Court of Pakistan]]]]
* [[Judiciary of Pakistan|Judicature]]: The [[Court system of Pakistan|judiciary]] of Pakistan is a hierarchical system with two classes of courts: the superior (or higher) judiciary and the subordinate (or lower) judiciary. The [[Chief Justice of Pakistan|Chief Justice]] of Pakistan is the [[chief judge]] who oversees the judicature's [[Court system of Pakistan|court system]] at all levels of command. The superior judiciary is composed of the [[Supreme Court of Pakistan|Supreme Court]] of Pakistan, the [[Federal Shariat Court]] and five [[High Courts of Pakistan|High Courts]], with the Supreme Court at the apex. The [[Constitution of Pakistan]] entrusts the superior judiciary with the obligation to preserve, protect and defend the constitution. Neither the Supreme Court nor a High Court may exercise jurisdiction in relation to Tribal Areas, except otherwise provided for. The disputed regions of [[Azad Kashmir]] and Gilgitâ€“Baltistan have separate court systems.

===Foreign relations of Pakistan===
{{Main article|Foreign relations of Pakistan}}
[[File:Motorcade in Arrival Ceremonies for Muhammad Ayub Khan, President of Pakistan use.jpg|thumb|200px|alt=(L-R) English: Motorcade for President Mohammad Ayub Khan of Pakistan. In open car (Lincoln-Mercury Continental with bubble top): Secret Service agent William Greer (driving); Military Aide to the President General Chester V. Clifton (front seat, center); Secret Service Agent Gerald &quot;Jerry&quot; Behn (front seat, right; partially hidden); President Mohammad Ayub Khan (standing); President John F. Kennedy (standing). Crowd watching. 14th Street, Washington, D.C.| [[Ayub Khan (President of Pakistan)]] with US President [[John F. Kennedy]] in 1961.]]

As the [[Muslim world]]'s second [[Muslim population growth|most populous]] [[nation-state]] (after [[Indonesia]]) and its only [[List of states with nuclear weapons|nuclear power]] state, Pakistan has an important role in the [[international community]].&lt;ref name=&quot;Mill City Press&quot;&gt;{{cite book|last1=Colgrove|first1=Rosemary|title=Eye on the sparrow : the remarkable journey of Father Joseph Nisari, Pakistani priest|date=2010|publisher=Mill City Press|location=Minneapolis|isbn=1936400871}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;ABC-CLIO&quot;&gt;{{cite book|title=Conflict between India and Pakistan : an encyclopedia|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=vLwOck15eboC&amp;pg=PA108&amp;dq=pakistan+second+most+populous&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;ei=SJbQVK7qCtfroASPxYDYBQ&amp;ved=0CCQQuwUwAQ#v=onepage&amp;q=pakistan%20second%20most%20populous&amp;f=false|accessdate=3 February 2015|last1=Lyon|first1=Peter|date=2008|publisher=ABC-CLIO|location=Santa Barbara, Calif.|isbn=1576077128}}&lt;/ref&gt; With a [[Agriculture in Pakistan|semi-agricultural]] and [[Industry of Pakistan|semi-industrialized]] [[Economy of Pakistan|economy]], its [[Foreign policy of Pakistan|foreign policy]] determines its standard of interactions for its organizations, corporations and individual citizens.&lt;ref name=&quot;Scrib, 19 November 2012&quot;&gt;{{cite journal|last=Ahmad|first=Hafeez Ashfaq|title=Determinants of Foreign Policy of Pakistan|journal=Scrib, 19 November 2012|url=http://www.scribd.com/doc/30773560/Foreign-Policy-of-Pakistan|accessdate=19 November 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Official policy statements&quot;&gt;{{cite web|title=Ministry of Foreign Affairs|url=http://www.mofa.gov.pk/mfa/pages/home.aspx|work=Pakistan Government|publisher=Official policy statements|accessdate=19 November 2012|deadurl=yes|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/20121031125313/http://www.mofa.gov.pk:80/mfa/pages/home.aspx|archivedate=31 October 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt; Its [[Pakistani geostrategy|geostrategic intentions]] were explained by Jinnah in a broadcast message in 1947, which is featured in a prominent quotation on the homepage of Pakistan's Ministry of Foreign Affairs website: &quot;The foundation of our foreign policy is friendship with all nations across the globe.&quot;&lt;ref name=&quot;Mofa&quot;&gt;{{cite web |title=Ministry of Foreign Affairs Homepage|work=MoFA.gov.pk|url=http://www.mofa.gov.pk/index.php|publisher=Government of Pakistan|date=2013|accessdate=4 August 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;

Since then, Pakistan has attempted to balance its relations with foreign nations.&lt;ref name=&quot;Deep &amp; Deep Publ.&quot;&gt;{{cite book|last1=Grover|first1=ed. by Verinder|last2=Arora|first2=Ranjana|title=Political system in Pakistan|date=1995|publisher=Deep &amp; Deep Publ.|location=New Delhi|isbn=8171007392}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Lancer Publ.&quot;&gt;{{cite book|last1=KrishnaRao|first1=K.V.|title=Prepare or perish : a study of national security|date=1991|publisher=Lancer Publ.|location=New Delhi|isbn=817212001X}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Dawn newspapers, 2013&quot;&gt;{{cite news|last1=Associate press|title=Pakistan wants friendly ties will all countries|url=http://www.dailytimes.com.pk/national/28-Dec-2013/pakistan-wants-friendly-ties-will-all-countries-pm.|accessdate=3 February 2015|agency=Dawn newspapers|publisher=Dawn newspapers, 2013|date=December 28, 2013}}{{dead link|date=October 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt; A non-[[Treaty on the Non-Proliferation of Nuclear Weapons|signatory party]] of the [[Treaty on the Non-Proliferation of Nuclear Weapons|Treaty on the Nuclear Non-Proliferation]], Pakistan is an influential member of the [[IAEA]].&lt;ref name=&quot;Routledge, UK&quot;&gt;{{cite book|last1=Chakma|first1=Bhumitra|title=Pakistan's nuclear weapons|date=2009|publisher=Routledge, UK|location=London|isbn=0415408717}}&lt;/ref&gt; In recent events, Pakistan has blocked an [[Fissile Material Cut-off Treaty|international treaty]] to limit [[fissile material]], arguing that the &quot;treaty would target Pakistan specifically.&quot;&lt;ref name=&quot;NPT News Directorate&quot;&gt;{{cite news|last=Officials reports|title=Pakistan a Responsible Nuclear Power, Official Asserts|url=http://www.nti.org/gsn/article/pakistan-a-responsible-nuclear-power-official-asserts/|accessdate=3 December 2012|newspaper=NPT News Directorate|date=June 18, 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt; In the 20th century, Pakistan's [[Pakistan and weapons of mass destruction|nuclear deterrence]] program focused on countering India's nuclear ambitions in the [[South Asia|region]], and [[Pokhran-II|nuclear tests]] by [[India]] eventually led Pakistan to [[Pakistan's nuclear testing series|reciprocate]] the event to maintain geopolitical balance as becoming a [[Nuclear power in Pakistan|nuclear power]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|title=World: Monitoring Nawaz Sharif's speech|url=http://news.bbc.co.uk/2/hi/world/monitoring/102445.stm|accessdate=11 March 2012|newspaper=BBC|date=28 May 1998}}&lt;/ref&gt; Currently, Pakistan maintains a policy of [[N-deterrence|credible minimum deterrence]], calling its program vital [[Pakistan and its Nuclear Detterent Program|nuclear deterrence]] against foreign aggression.&lt;ref name=&quot;United Book Press.&quot; /&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|title=N-deterrence to be pursued|url=http://www.dawn.com/2011/07/15/n-deterrence-to-be-pursued.html|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/web/20110718102116/http://www.dawn.com/2011/07/15/n-deterrence-to-be-pursued.html|archivedate=18 July 2011|accessdate=11 March 2012|newspaper=Dawn|date=15 July 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt;

[[File:Hassan Rouhani in meeting with Pakistani Prime Minister Nawaz Sharif in Saadabad Palace 05 (cropped).jpg|thumb|200px|left|[[President of Iran]] [[Hassan Rouhani]] in conversation with [[Prime Minister of Pakistan|Prime Minister]] [[Nawaz Sharif]] in January 2016.]]
Located in strategic and geopolitical corridor of the world's major maritime [[Oil supplies|oil supply lines]], communication [[Fiber-optic communication|fiber optics]], Pakistan has proximity to the natural resources of [[Central Asia]]n countries.&lt;ref name=&quot;Tauris&quot;&gt;{{cite book|last1=Shah|first1=Mehtab Ali|title=The foreign policy of Pakistan : ethnic impacts on diplomacy, 1971â€“1994|date=1997|publisher=Tauris|location=London [u.a.]|isbn=1860641695}}&lt;/ref&gt; Pakistan is an influential and founding member of the [[Organisation of Islamic Cooperation]] (OIC) and is a major [[Major non-NATO ally|non-NATO ally]] of the [[United States]] in the [[war against terrorism]]â€” a status achieved in 2004.&lt;ref name=&quot;Oxford University Press, Shahi&quot;&gt;{{cite book|last1=Shahi|first1=Abdul Sattar ; foreword by Agha|title=Pakistan's foreign policy, 1947â€“2012 : a concise history|date=2013|publisher=Oxford University Press, Shahi|location=Karachi|isbn=0199069107|edition=Third}}&lt;/ref&gt; Pakistan's [[Foreign policy of Pakistan|foreign policy]] and [[Pakistani geostrategy|geostrategy]] mainly focus on economy and security against threats to its [[Nationalism in Pakistan|national identity]] and territorial integrity, and on the cultivation of close relations with other Muslim countries.&lt;ref name=&quot;Govt of Pakistan&quot;&gt;{{cite web|last1=Govt of Pakistan|title=Foreign Policy of Pakistan|url=http://www.mofa.gov.pk/content.php?pageID=Foreign%20Policy|website=http://www.mofa.gov.pk/|publisher=Govt of Pakistan|accessdate=3 February 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt; Briefing on country's foreign policy in 2004, the [[Pakistan Senate|Pakistani senator]] reportedly explains: &quot;Pakistan highlights sovereign equality of states, bilateralism, mutuality of interests, and non-interference in each other's domestic affairs as the cardinal features of its foreign policy.&quot;&lt;ref name=&quot;rizvi&quot;&gt;{{cite web|author=Hasan Askari Rizvi|title=Pakistan's Foreign Policy:An Overview 1947â€“2004|url=http://www.millat.com/democracy/Foreign%20Policy/Briefing_Paper_english_11.pdf|format=PDF|publisher=Pakistan Institute of Legislative Development and Transparency|pages=10â€“12, 20|accessdate=20 December 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt; Pakistan is an active member of the [[United Nations]] and has a [[Permanent Representative of Pakistan to the United Nations|Permanent Representative]] to represent Pakistan's policy in international politics.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.un.org/en/members/index.shtml#p|title=United Nations Member States|date=3 July 2006|publisher=United Nations|accessdate=8 July 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt; Pakistan has lobbied for the concept of &quot;[[Enlightened Moderation]]&quot; in the [[Muslim world]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://foreignaffairscommittee.org/includes/content_files/PAKISTANANDTHEOIC.pdf|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/web/20090219073408/http://foreignaffairscommittee.org/includes/content_files/PAKISTANANDTHEOIC.pdf|archivedate=19 February 2009|title=Senate OIC Report|date=September 2005|format=PDF|publisher=Senate of Pakistan: Senate Foreign Relations Committee|pages=16â€“18|accessdate=8 July 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|title=A Plea for Enlightened Moderation|url=http://www.washingtonpost.com/wp-dyn/articles/A5081-2004May31.html|accessdate=24 December 2011|newspaper=The Washington Post|date=1 June 2004}}&lt;/ref&gt; Pakistan is also a member of [[Commonwealth of Nations]],&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.thecommonwealth.org/YearbookHomeInternal/138945/|title=Pakistan|publisher=Commonwealth Secretariat|accessdate=8 July 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt; the [[South Asian Association for Regional Cooperation]] (SAARC), the [[Economic Cooperation Organisation]] (ECO)&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Member Countries|url=http://www.ecosecretariat.org/|work=ECO|accessdate=24 December 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|author=A.R.Kemal|title=Exploring Pakistan's Regional Economic Cooperation Potential|url=http://www.pide.org.pk/pdf/psde20AGM/EXPLORING%20PAKISTANS%20REGIONAL%20ECONOMIC%20COOPERATION%20POTENTIAL.pdf|work=PIDE|format=PDF|pages=1â€“2|accessdate=24 December 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt; and the [[G20 developing nations]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=G-20 Ministerial Meeting|url=http://commerce.nic.in/wto_sub/g20/pressrel.htm#_ftn1|work=Commerce.nic.in|publisher=Department of Commerce, Ministry of Commerce and Industry, India|date=19 March 2005|accessdate=4 January 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt; Pakistan does not have [[Pakistan-Israel relations|diplomatic relations]] with [[Israel]];&lt;ref name=&quot;Washington Post, Pakistan Bureau&quot;&gt;{{cite news|last1=Tharoor|first1=Ishaan|title=The Pakistani origins of the Israeli state|url=http://www.washingtonpost.com/blogs/worldviews/wp/2014/12/03/the-pakistani-origins-of-the-israeli-state/|accessdate=2 March 2015|agency=Washington Post|publisher=Washington Post, Pakistan Bureau|date=3 December 2014}}&lt;/ref&gt; nonetheless some Israeli citizens have visited the country on a tourist visas.&lt;ref name=&quot;Haaretz&quot;&gt;{{cite news|last1=Khoury|first1=Jack|title=Israeli lecturer takes part in Pakistan conference|url=http://www.haaretz.com/.premium-1.644835|accessdate=2 March 2015|agency=Haaretz|publisher=Haaretz|date=28 February 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt; Based on mutual cooperation, the security exchange have taken place between [[Pakistan-Israel relations|two countries]] using [[Turkey]] as a communication conduit.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news |url=http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/world/south_asia/4203788.stm|title=Pakistan-Israel in landmark talks|publisher=BBC News|date=1 September 2005|accessdate=July 4, 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt; Despite Pakistan being the only country in the world that has not established a [[Armeniaâ€“Pakistan relations|diplomatic relations]] with [[Armenia]], the [[Armenians in Pakistan|Armenian community]] still resides in Pakistan.&lt;ref name=&quot;Armenian TImes&quot;&gt;{{cite news|last1=Staff work|title=Pakistan the only country not recognizing Armenia â€“ envoy|url=http://www.news.az/articles/armenia/86325|accessdate=2 March 2015|agency=Armenian TImes|publisher=Armenian TImes|date=5 February 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;

[[File:Huseyn Shaheed Suhrawardy and Zhou Enlai signing the Treaty of Friendship Between China and Pakistan in Beijing.jpg|200px|thumb|Pakistan PM [[Huseyn Shaheed Suhrawardy]] with Chinese Premier [[Zhou Enlai]] signing the Treaty of Friendship Between China and Pakistan. Pakistan, today, hosts China's largest embassy.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://asiancorrespondent.com/130710/china-unveils-pakistan-embassy-its-largest-in-the-world/|title=China opens â€˜largestâ€™ embassy in Pakistan, strengthens South Asia presence â€“ Asian Correspondent|work=asiancorrespondent.com}}&lt;/ref&gt;]]
Maintaining cultural, political, social, and economic relations with the [[Arab World|Arab world]] and other countries in the [[Muslim World]] is a vital factor in Pakistan's foreign policy.&lt;ref name=&quot;Taylor &amp; Francis.&quot;&gt;{{cite book|last1=Pande|first1=Aparna|title=Explaining Pakistan's Foreign Policy: Escaping India|date=2006|publisher=Taylor &amp; Francis.|isbn=1136818944}}&lt;/ref&gt; Pakistan was the first country to have established diplomatic relations with [[Peoples Republic of China|China]] and relations continues to be warm since China's [[Sino-Indian War|war with]] [[India]] in 1962.&lt;ref name=&quot;Council on Foreign Relations, China Pakistan&quot;&gt;{{cite web|last1=Afridi|first1=Jamal|last2=Bajoria|first2=Jayshree|title=China-Pakistan Relations|url=http://www.cfr.org/china/china-pakistan-relations/p10070|website=http://www.cfr.org/|publisher=Council on Foreign Relations, China Pakistan|accessdate=3 February 2015|date=6 July 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt; In the 1960sâ€“1980s, Pakistan greatly helped [[Peoples Republic of China|China]] in reaching out to the world's major countries and helped facilitate [[President of the United States|U.S. President]] [[Richard Nixon|Nixon]]'s [[1972 Nixon visit to China|state visit]] to [[Peoples Republic of China|China]].&lt;ref name=&quot;Council on Foreign Relations, China Pakistan&quot; /&gt; Despite the change of [[GoP|governments]] in Pakistan, variations in the regional and global situation, China policy in Pakistan continues to be dominant factor at all time.&lt;ref name=&quot;Council on Foreign Relations, China Pakistan&quot; /&gt; In return, China is Pakistan's largest trading partner and economic cooperation have reached high points, with substantial Chinese investment in Pakistan's infrastructural expansion including the Pakistani deep-water port at [[Gwadar]]. Sino-Pak friendly relations touched new heights as both the countries signed 51 agreements and Memorandums of Understanding (MoUs) in 2015 for cooperation in different fields.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.thenews.com.pk/Todays-News-13-37104-Pak-China-trade-volume-to-be-taken-to-$20-bn|title=ISLAMABAD: Pakistan and China agreed to raise their trade volume up to $20 billion and pledged to continue their cooperation in civil nuclear technology.|work=http://www.thenews.com.pk/}}&lt;/ref&gt;
&lt;ref name=&quot;china&quot;&gt;{{cite web|author=Urvashi Aneja|url=http://www.ipcs.org/pdf_file/issue/136564802IPCS-Special-Report-26.pdf|title=PAKISTAN-CHINA RELATIONS|publisher=Institute of Peace and Conflict Studies|date=June 2006|page=1|accessdate=28 December 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|url=http://www.reuters.com/article/2006/11/24/us-pakistan-china-chro-idUSISL9262520061124|title=CHRONOLOGY-Main events in Chinese-Pakistani relations|publisher=Reuters|work=Thomson Reuters|accessdate=24 November 2006|date=24 November 2006}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.cfr.org/china/china-pakistan-relations/p10070|title=China-Pakistan Relations|author=Jamal Afridi|work=Council on Foreign Relations|accessdate=6 July 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt; Both countries have signed the [[Chinaâ€“Pakistan Free Trade Agreement|Free Trade Agreement]] in the 2000s, and Pakistan continues to serve as [[Peoples Republic of China|China]]'s communication bridge in the [[Muslim World]].&lt;ref name=&quot;Stanford University Press, California, [u.s]&quot;&gt;{{cite book|last1=Gillette|first1=Maris Boyd|title=Between Mecca and Beijing|date=2000|publisher=Stanford University Press, California, [u.s]|location=California, [u.s]|isbn=0804764344}}&lt;/ref&gt;

Because of difficulties in relations with its geopolitical rival [[India]], Pakistan maintains close political relations with [[Turkey]] and [[Iran]].&lt;ref name=&quot;AuthorHouse. 2006&quot;&gt;{{cite book|last1=Anwar|first1=Muhammad|title=Friends Near Home: Pakistan's Strategic Security Options|date=2006|publisher=AuthorHouse. 2006|location=Islamabad, Pakistan|isbn=1467015415}}&lt;/ref&gt; [[Saudi Arabia]] also maintains a respected position in Pakistan's foreign policy, and both countries has been a focal point in Pakistan's foreign policy.&lt;ref name=&quot;AuthorHouse. 2006&quot; /&gt; The [[Kashmir conflict]] remains the major point of rift; three of [[Indo-Pakistani wars and conflicts|their four wars]] were over this territory.&lt;ref name=&quot;kashmir1&quot; /&gt;  Due to ideological differences, Pakistan opposed the [[Soviet Union]] in the 1950s and during [[Soviet-Afghan War]] in the 1980s, Pakistan was one of the closest allies of the United States.&lt;ref name=&quot;rizvi&quot; /&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;mostallied&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.fpa.org/newsletter_info2583/newsletter_info_sub_list.htm?section=Pakistan%3A%20The%20Most%20Allied%20Ally%20in%20Asia|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/web/20110725141127/http://www.fpa.org/newsletter_info2583/newsletter_info_sub_list.htm?section=Pakistan:%20The%20Most%20Allied%20Ally%20in%20Asia|archivedate=25 July 2011|title=Pakistan: The Most Allied Ally in Asia|author=Robert Nolan|publisher=Foreign Policy Association|accessdate=12 March 2009}}&lt;/ref&gt; Relations with [[Russia]] have greatly improved since 1999 and cooperation with various sectors have increased between Russia and Pakistan.&lt;ref name=&quot;Dawn, 2015&quot;&gt;{{cite news|last1=staff writer|title=Accord to diversify ties with Russia|url=http://dawn.com/news/1039487/accord-to-diversify-ties-with-russia|accessdate=3 February 2015|agency=Dawn, 2015|date=9 January 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt; Pakistan has had &quot;on-and-off&quot; relations with the United States. A close ally of the United States in the [[Cold war]], Pakistan's relation with the United States relations soured in the 1990s when the U.S. [[Pressler amendment|imposed sanctions]] because of Pakistan's secretive nuclear development.&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite news|author=Sabir Shah|title=US military aid to Pakistan suspended six times since 1954|url=http://www.thenews.com.pk/TodaysPrintDetail.aspx?ID=205102&amp;Cat=2&amp;dt=10/24/2009|publisher=[[The News International, Pakistan]]|accessdate=26 October 2009}}&lt;/ref&gt; Since [[September 11 attacks|9/11]], Pakistan has been a close ally with the United States on the issue of [[counter-terrorism]] in the regions of the [[Middle East]] and [[South Asia]], with the US supporting the latter with aid money and weapons.&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite web|title=2015 Joint Statement By President Barack Obama And Prime Minister Nawaz Sharif|url=https://www.whitehouse.gov/the-press-office/2015/10/22/2015-joint-statement-president-barack-obama-and-prime-minister-nawaz|website=whitehouse.gov|accessdate=2015-12-28}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite journal|url=http://idsa.in/system/files/strategicanalysis_shanthie_0906.pdf|title=US-Pakistan Counter-Terrorism Cooperation: Dynamics and Challenges|last=D'Souza|first=Shanthie|date=2006|journal=Strategic Analysis|doi=|pmid=|access-date=28 December 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;

The United States-led [[war on terrorism]] led initially to an improvement in the relationship, but it was strained by a divergence of interests and resulting mistrust during the [[War in Afghanistan (2001-present)|war in Afghanistan]] and by issues related to terrorism.&lt;ref name=&quot;mondediplo2007&quot;&gt;{{cite web|title=The United States' new backyard|url=http://mondediplo.com/2007/11/03mideast|author=Alain Gresh|publisher=Le Monde diplomatique|accessdate=24 July 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|author=C.J. Radin|url=http://www.longwarjournal.org/archives/2011/12/us_pakistani_relatio.php|title=Analysis: The US-Pakistan relationship|publisher=Long War Journal|accessdate=January 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.defencetalk.com/pakistan-usa-allies-in-the-war-on-terrorism-1865/|title=Pakistan &amp; USA&amp;nbsp;â€“ Allies in the war on Terrorism!|author=Nazir Khaja|work=Defence Talk|accessdate=15 February 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|url=http://www.washingtonpost.com/world/national-security/pakistan-backed-attacks-on-american-targets-us-says/2011/09/22/gIQAf0q6oK_story.html|title=Pakistan backed attacks on American targets, U.S. says|author=Karen DeYoung|work=Washington Post|accessdate=30 January 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt; Since 1948, there has been an ongoing, and at times fluctuating, [[Balochistan conflict|violent conflict]] in the southwestern province of [[Balochistan]] between various Baloch separatist groups, who seek greater political autonomy, and the central government of Pakistan.&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite news|url=http://www.economist.com/node/21552248|title=Balochistan: &quot;We only receive back the bodies&quot;|location=Quetta|date=7 April 2012|work=The Economist|quote=}}&lt;/ref&gt;

===Administrative divisions===
{{Main article|Administrative units of Pakistan|Districts of Pakistan}}
