===Nuclear power and energy===
{{main article|Nuclear power in Pakistan|Energy in Pakistan|Electricity sector in Pakistan}}
[[File:Tarbela_Dam_during_the_2010_floods.jpg|thumb|250px|right|[[Tarbela Dam]], the largest earth filled dam in the world, was constructed in 1968.]]
[[Energy (physics)|Energy]] from the [[nuclear power in Pakistan|nuclear power]] source is provided by three licensed-commercial [[nuclear power plants]], {{As of|2012|lc=y}} data.&lt;ref name=&quot;Pakistan Atomic Energy Commission Directorate for Public Press and International News Relations&quot;&gt;{{cite web|last=(PAEC)|first=Pakistan Atomic Energy Commission|authorlink=|title=Prime Minister inaugurates 340 MW Chashma Nuclear Power Plant Unit-2: Govt to provide full support to PAEC for Nuclear Power Projects Urges International Community to make nuclear technology accessible to Pakistan for power generation|work=Pakistan Atomic Energy Commission's Press Directorate|publisher=Pakistan Atomic Energy Commission Directorate for Public Press and International News Relations|date=12 May 2011|url=http://www.paec.gov.pk/p-mj11-news1b.htm|doi=|accessdate=}}{{dead link|date=May 2016|bot=medic}}{{cbignore|bot=medic}}&lt;/ref&gt; Pakistan is the first [[Muslim world|Muslim country]] in the world to construct and operate civil nuclear power plants.&lt;ref&gt;Nuclear power in Pakistan, Dr. Zia H. Siddiqui and Dr. I.H. Qureshi, pp.31â€“33.&lt;/ref&gt; The [[Pakistan Atomic Energy Commission]] (PAEC), the scientific and nuclear [[Government of Pakistan|governmental]] authority, is solely responsible for operating these power plants, while the [[Pakistan Nuclear Regulatory Authority]] regulates safe usage of the nuclear energy.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web
  |last=(PAEC)|first=Pakistan Atomic Energy Commission|authorlink=Pakistan Atomic Energy Commission|title=Nuclear Power Generation Programme|work=Government of Pakistan|publisher=PAEC|url=http://www.paec.gov.pk/paec-np.htm|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/web/20050209020648/http://www.paec.gov.pk/paec-np.htm|archivedate=9 February 2005|accessdate=2011}}&lt;/ref&gt; The [[Electricity generation|electricity generated]] by commercial nuclear power plants constitutes roughly ~5.8% of electricity generated in Pakistan, compared to ~62% from [[fossil fuel]] ([[petroleum]]), ~29.9% from [[hydroelectric power]] and ~0.3% from [[Coal power plant|coal]].&lt;ref name=&quot;Express Tribune, 2014&quot;&gt;{{cite news|last1=Kazmi|first1=Zahir|title=Pakistan's energy security|url=http://tribune.com.pk/story/655573/pakistans-energy-security/|accessdate=23 February 2015|work=Special report on Energy security efforts in Pakistan|agency=Express Tribune|publisher=Express Tribune, 2014|date=7 January 2014}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Directorate-General for Nuclear Power Generation&quot;&gt;{{cite web|last=Syed Yousaf|first=Raza|title=Current Picture of Electrical Energy In Pakistan|url=https://docs.google.com/viewer?a=v&amp;q=cache:T4QW3douApsJ:www.iaea.org/INPRO/4th_Dialogue_Forum/DAY_3_01_August-ready/2._-_DG-C3-4-31-07-2012.pdf+pakistan+nuclear+power+program+2050&amp;hl=en&amp;gl=us&amp;pid=bl&amp;srcid=ADGEESjUcYBzrkzBdSSwbflDwBpLkLAkFaFROisP_jK3E3S97aqHY9tMS-It6gaYDd-q4lZP8BEuD6e4C5E91EnlkiSKIw-JbWuYsNwjNNC1f1Nxyw9D0Ib_V424k5ghsCazU80qDKfF&amp;sig=AHIEtbRAsJSVdJ36dVxzvdggw_Xz16RLGg|work=Pakistan Atomic Energy Commission|publisher=Directorate-General for Nuclear Power Generation|accessdate=28 November 2012|date=31 July 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Pakistan Observer&quot;&gt;{{cite news|last=Zulfikar|first=Saman|title=Pak-China energy cooperation|url=http://pakobserver.net/detailnews.asp?id=109910|accessdate=23 April 2012|newspaper=Pakistan Observer|date=23 April 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt;  Pakistan is one of the four [[List of states with nuclear weapons|nuclear armed states]] (along with [[India]], [[Israel]], and [[North Korea]]) that is not a party to the [[Nuclear Non-Proliferation Treaty]] but is a member in good standing of the [[International Atomic Energy Agency]].&lt;ref name=&quot;IAEA Membership states&quot;&gt;{{cite web|last=UN Press Release|title=IAEA Publications: Pakistan Overview|url=http://ola.iaea.org/factSheets/CountryDetails.asp?country=PK|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/web/20070612063504/http://ola.iaea.org/factSheets/CountryDetails.asp?country=PK|archivedate=12 June 2007|work=IAEA, P.O. Box 100, Wagramer Strasse 5, A-1400 Vienna, Austria|publisher=IAEA Membership states|accessdate=17 April 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Dawn Newspapers, 25th April, 2011&quot;&gt;{{cite news|last=Associate Press of Pakistan (APP)|title=IAEA declares nuclear energy programme safe|url=http://dawn.com/2011/04/25/iaea-declares-pakistan-nuclear-program-safe/|accessdate=17 April 2012|newspaper=Dawn Newspapers, 25 April 2011|date=25 April 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Reuters, Vienna&quot;&gt;{{cite news|last=Dahl|first=Fredrik|title=Nuclear-armed Pakistan chairs board of U.N. atom body|url=http://www.reuters.com/article/2010/09/27/us-nuclear-iaea-pakistan-idUSTRE68Q1MN20100927|accessdate=17 April 2012|newspaper=Reuters, Vienna|date=27 September 2010|quote=&quot;Pakistan is a long-standing and &quot;very law-abiding&quot; member of the IAEA, got no opposition from any side at all}}&lt;/ref&gt;

For the commercial usage of the [[Nuclear power plant|nuclear power]], [[People's Republic of China|China]] has provided an avid support for commercializing the nuclear power sources in Pakistan from early on, first providing the [[CHASNUPP-1|Chashma-I]] reactor. The [[Karachi Nuclear Power Plant|Karachi-I]], a [[CANDU reactor|Candu-type]], was provided by [[Canada]] in 1971â€“ the country's first commercial [[nuclear power plant]]. In subsequent years, [[People's Republic of China]] sold the [[nuclear power plant]] for energy and industrial growth of the country. In 2005, [[China and Pakistan|both countries]] reached out towards working on joint energy security plan, calling for a huge increase in generating capacity to more than 160,000&amp;nbsp;[[Megawatt|MWe]] by 2030. Original admissions by Pakistan, the [[Pakistan Government|government]] plans for lifting nuclear capacity to 8800&amp;nbsp;[[Megawatt|MWe]], 900&amp;nbsp;[[Megawatt|MWe]] of it by 2015 and a further 1500&amp;nbsp;[[Megawatt|MWe]] by 2020.&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite journal|last=Ijaz|first=Muhammad, Director of Scientific Information and Public Relation (SIPR)|title=PAEC assigned 8,800 MWe nuclear power target by 2030:PAEC contributing to socio-economic uplift of the country|journal=PakAtom Newsletter|volume=49|issue=1â€“2|pages=1â€“8|publisher=Pakistan Atomic Energy Commission|location=Islamabad, Islamabad Capital Territory|date=December 2010|url=http://www.paec.gov.pk/newsletters/pkatm-n/p-nd10.pdf}}{{dead link|date=May 2016|bot=medic}}{{cbignore|bot=medic}}&lt;/ref&gt;

In June 2008, the nuclear [[Chashma Nuclear Power Complex|commercial complex]] was expanded with the ground work of installing and operationalizing the [[Chashma Nuclear Power Complex|Chashma-III]] and [[Chashma Nuclear Power Complex|Chashmaâ€“IV]] nuclear power plants at [[Chashma, Pakistan|Chashma]], [[Punjab (Pakistani province)|Punjab Province]], each with 320â€“340&amp;nbsp;MWe and costing [[Pakistan Rupee|â‚¨.]]&amp;nbsp;129&amp;nbsp;billion,; from which the [[Pakistan Rupee|â‚¨.]] 80&amp;nbsp;billion of this from international sources, principally China.

A further agreement for China's help with the project was signed in October 2008, and given prominence as a counter to the [[Indiaâ€“United States Civil Nuclear Agreement|U.S.â€“India agreement]] shortly preceding it. Cost quoted then was US$1.7&amp;nbsp;billion, with a foreign loan component of $1.07&amp;nbsp;billion. In 2013, the second nuclear [[Karachi Nuclear Power Complex|commercial complex]] in [[Karachi]] was marginalized and expanded to additional reactors, based on the [[Chashma Nuclear Power Complex|Chashma complex]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|last1=Bhutta|first1=Zafar|title=Govt to kick off work on 1,100MW nuclear power plant|url=http://tribune.com.pk/story/559885/govt-to-kick-off-work-on-1100mw-nuclear-power-plant/|accessdate=19 January 2015|agency=Express Tribune|date=7 June 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt;
[[File:Jhimpir Wind Farm 2012.jpg|thumb||Jhampir Wind Power Plants|
Jhimpir Wind Power Projects are currently producing around 250 mega-watt, are expected produce 3200 mega-watt in near future]]
The [[Electricity in Pakistan|electrical energy]] is generated by various [[List of electric supply companies in Pakistan|energy corporations]] and evenly distributed by the [[National Electric Power Regulatory Authority]] (NEPRA) among the [[Four Provinces (Pakistan)|four provinces]]. However, the [[Karachi]]-based [[K-Electric]] and the [[Water and Power Development Authority]] (WAPDA) generates much of the [[Electricity in Pakistan|electrical energy]] as well as gathering revenue nationwide.&lt;ref name=&quot;energy&quot;&gt;{{cite web|title=Power Sector Situation in Pakistan|url=http://www.rural-electrification.com/cms/upload/pdf/Pakistan-GTZ-power-sector-overview.pdf|accessdate=26 December 2011|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/web/20110124180708/http://www.rural-electrification.com/cms/upload/pdf/Pakistan-GTZ-power-sector-overview.pdf|archivedate=24 January 2011|work=Alternate Energy Development Board and GTZ|format=PDF|year=2005|page=1}}&lt;/ref&gt; Capacity to [[Electricity generation|generate]] ~22,797{{small|[[Megawatt|MWt]]}} [[electricity]] has been installed in 2014, with the initiation of several energy projects in 2014.&lt;ref name=&quot;Express Tribune, 2014&quot; /&gt; Energy from the [[Nuclear power in Pakistan|nuclear sources]] is provided by three licensed commercial nuclear power plants operated Pakistan [[Pakistan Atomic Energy Commission|Atomic Energy Commission]] (PAEC) under licensed by the [[Pakistan Nuclear Regulatory Authority|Nuclear Regulatory Authority]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|author=Muhammad Saleh Zaafir|title=PM inaugurates 330MW Chashma-2 N-power plant|url=http://www.thenews.com.pk/TodaysPrintDetail.aspx?ID=5952&amp;Cat=13|accessdate=26 December 2011|newspaper=The News|date=13 May 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt; Pakistan is the first Muslim country in the world to embark on a nuclear power program.&lt;ref name=&quot;siddiqui63&quot;&gt;{{Cite journal|author=Z.H. Siddiqui; I.H. Qureshi|title=Nuclear power in Pakistan|journal=The Nucleus|volume=42|issue=1â€“2|pages=63â€“66|publisher=The Nucleus PINSTECH publication|location=Nilore, Islamabad|date=13 October 2005|url=http://www.thenucleuspak.org.pk/nucleus/pdf%20Special%20Issue%20Nucleus%2042%281-4%29/Nuclear%20Power%20in%20Pakistan%20%28ms611%29.pdf|issn=0029-5698|accessdate=28 December 2011|deadurl=yes|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/20120306133728/http://www.thenucleuspak.org.pk/nucleus/pdf%20Special%20Issue%20Nucleus%2042(1-4)/Nuclear%20Power%20in%20Pakistan%20(ms611).pdf|archivedate=6 March 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt; Commercial nuclear power plants generate roughly 5.8% of Pakistan's electricity, compared with about 64.0% from [[Thermal power station|thermal]], 29.9% from [[Hydroelectric power|hydroelectric]] [[Power (physics)|power]], and  ~0.3% from the [[Coal electricity|Coal source]].&lt;ref name=&quot;energy&quot; /&gt;

===Tourism===
{{Main article|Tourism in Pakistan}}
[[File:Design work of Badshahi Mosque (Kingâ€™s Mosque).jpg|thumb|200px|right|[[Badshahi Mosque]] was commissioned by the [[Mughal tribe|Mughal]]s in 1671. It is listed as a [[World Heritage Site]].]]

Pakistan, with its diverse cultures, people and landscapes attracted 1.1 million foreign tourists annually in 2011 and 2012 contributing [[$]]351 million and [[$]]369 million to Pakistan's economy respectively.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://pakobserver.net/201210/21/detailnews.asp?id=179025|title=One million tourists visit Pakistan in 2012|work=Pakistan Observer|date=21 October 2012|accessdate=21 October 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt; A significant decline since the 1970s when the country received unprecedented amounts of foreign tourists due to to the popular [[Hippie trail]]. The trail attracted thousands of Europeans and American's in 1960s and 1970s who travelled via land through [[Turkey]], [[Iran]] into [[India]] through Pakistan.&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite web|url=http://www.richardgregory.org.uk/history/hippie-trail.htm|title=Richard Gregory|website=www.richardgregory.org.uk|access-date=2016-06-17}}&lt;/ref&gt; The main destinations of choice for these tourists were the [[Khyber Pass]], [[Peshawar]], [[Karachi]], [[Lahore]], [[Swat (Pakistan)|Swat]] and [[Rawalpindi]].&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite news|url=http://nadeemfparacha.wordpress.com/2008/07/20/before-the-lights-went-out/|work=http://nadeemfparacha.wordpress.com|location=Karachi|title=Before the Lights Went Out|first=Nadeem|last=Paracha|date=7 July 2008|accessdate=19 September 2011|deadurl=yes|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/20100306134648/http://nadeemfparacha.wordpress.com:80/2008/07/20/before-the-lights-went-out/|archivedate=6 March 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt; However, the trail declined after the [[Iranian Revolution]] and the [[Sovietâ€“Afghan War]].&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite web|url=http://www.huffingtonpost.com/christian-caryl/strange-rebels-excerpt_b_3427854.html|title=When Afghanistan Was Just a Stop on the 'Hippie Trail'|last=mag|first=Christian Caryl Legatum Institute / Foreign Policy|date=2013-06-12|website=The Huffington Post|access-date=2016-06-17}}&lt;/ref&gt;

The country however continues to attract an estimated of half a million foreign tourists.&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite web|url=http://tribune.com.pk/story/767886/number-of-foreign-tourists-in-2014-dips-by-50/|title=Number of foreign tourists in 2014 dips by 50% - The Express Tribune|date=2014-09-27|website=The Express Tribune|language=en-US|access-date=2016-06-17}}&lt;/ref&gt; Pakistan's attraction range from the ruin of civilisation such as [[Mohenjo-daro]], [[Harappa]] and [[Taxila]], to the Himalayan hill stations. Pakistan is home to several [[Mountain ranges of Pakistan|mountain peaks]] over 7000 m.&lt;ref name=&quot;autogenerated1&quot;&gt;{{Wayback |df=yes|date=20040111062341|url=http://www.tourism.gov.pk/mountain.html|title=PTDC page on mountaineering}}{{dead link|date=October 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;{{unreliable source?|date=July 2014}} The north part of Pakistan has many old fortresses, ancient architecture and the [[Hunza Valley|Hunza]] and Chitral valley, home to small pre-Islamic [[Animist]] [[Kalasha]] community claiming descent from [[Alexander the Great]]. Pakistan's cultural capital, Lahore, contains many examples of [[Mughal architecture]] such as [[Badshahi Masjid]], [[Shalimar Gardens (Lahore)|Shalimar Gardens]], [[Tomb of Jahangir]] and the [[Lahore Fort]].&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.pakistan.gov.pk/.../tourism.../Toruism(wup)(Folder-II).pdf]{{Dead link|date=March 2014}}&lt;/ref&gt;

In October 2006, just one year after the [[2005 Kashmir earthquake]], ''[[The Guardian]]'' released what it described as &quot;The top five tourist sites in Pakistan&quot; in order to help the country's tourism industry.&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite news|url=http://www.guardian.co.uk/travel/2006/oct/17/pakistan?page=all|work=The Guardian|location=London|title=Out of the rubble|first=Antonia|last=Windsor|date=17 October 2006|accessdate=25 May 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt; The five sites included [[Taxila]], Lahore, [[Karakoram Highway|The Karakoram Highway]], [[Karimabad (Hunza)|Karimabad]] and [[Lake Saiful Muluk]]. To promote Pakistan's unique and various cultural heritage.&lt;ref name=&quot;Visit Pakistan 2007&quot;&gt;[http://doodhpatti.blogspot.com/2006/10/visit-pakistan-year-2007-events.html Events taking place during 2007, Press released by Tourism of Pakistan]{{Dead link|date=September 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.tourism.gov.pk/fairs_festivals.html|title=Tourism Events in Pakistan in 2010|publisher=Tourism.gov.pk|accessdate=27 September 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt; In 2009, The World Economic Forum's Travel &amp; Tourism Competitiveness Report ranked Pakistan as one of the top 25% tourist destinations for its World Heritage sites. Tourist destinations range from mangroves in the south, to the 5,000-year-old cities of the [[Indus Valley Civilization]] which included [[Mohenjo-daro]] and [[Harappa]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.ft.com/cms/s/2/76d57272-6764-11de-925f-00144feabdc0.html|title=The road between China and Pakistan|work=Financial Times|date=4 July 2009|accessdate=27 September 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt;

===Transport===
{{Main article|Transport in Pakistan}}
[[File:Boeing 777-240ER Pakistan International Airlines AP-BGL.jpg|230px|thumb|right|[[Boeing 777]] owned and operated by the [[Pakistan International Airlines]]. The airline serves over 67 global destinations.]]

The [[Transportation in Pakistan|transport industry]] accounts for ~10.5% of the nation's [[Gross domestic product|GDP]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|author=The [[World Bank]]|title=Transportation in Pakistan|url=http://go.worldbank.org/7CYYM39VG0|year=2011|publisher=World Bank|accessdate=January 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt; Pakistan's [[Motorways of Pakistan|motorway infrastructure]] is better than those of [[India]], [[Bangladesh]], and [[Indonesia]], but the [[Railways in Pakistan|train system]] lags behind those of India and China, and aviation infrastructure also needs improvement.&lt;ref name=&quot;IDE-JETRO&quot;&gt;{{Cite thesis|type=Research Paper|chapter=Macroeconomic Performance and Infrastructure Development in India|title=Transport Infrastructure in India: Developments, Challenges and Lessons from Japan|url=http://www.ide.go.jp/English/Publish/Download/Vrf/pdf/465.pdf|author=Pravakar Sahoo|format=PDF|page=4|date=March 2011|publisher=Institute of Development Economies, Japan External Trade Organization|series=Visiting Research Fellows|accessdate=13 June 2012|id=465}}&lt;/ref&gt; There is scarcely any [[List of dry ports in Pakistan|inland water transportation system]], and coastal shipping only meets minor local requirements.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|author=Farrukh Javed|title=Sustainable financing for the maintenance of Pakistan Highways|url=http://www.unescap.org/ttdw/Publications/TPTS_pubs/bulletin75/Bulletin75_ch5.pdf|format=PDF|year=2005|publisher=UNESCAP|page=2|accessdate=31 December 2011|deadurl=yes|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/20070811075941/http://www.unescap.org/ttdw/Publications/TPTS_pubs/bulletin75/Bulletin75_ch5.pdf|archivedate=11 August 2007}}&lt;/ref&gt;

[[National Highways of Pakistan|Highways]] form the backbone of Pakistan's transport system; a total road length of 259,618&amp;nbsp;km accounts for 91% of passenger and 96% of freight traffic. Road transport services are largely in the hands of the [[Privatization in Pakistan|private sector]], which handles around 95% of freight traffic. The [[National Highway Authority (Pakistan)|National Highway Authority]] is responsible for the maintenance of national highways and motorways. The highway and motorway system depends mainly on northâ€“south links, connecting the southern ports to the populous provinces of [[Punjab]] and [[Khyber Pakhtunkhwa|Khyber-Pakhtunkhwa]]. Although this network only accounts for 4.2% of total road length, it carries 85% of the country's traffic.&lt;ref name=&quot;pc&quot;&gt;{{cite web|author=Ahmed Jamal pirzada|title=Draft: Role of Connectivity in Growth Strategy of Pakistan|url=http://www.pc.gov.pk/feg/PDFs/role%20of%20connectivity%20in%20growth%20strategy%20of%20pakistan.pdf|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/web/20120421064636/http://www.pc.gov.pk/feg/PDFs/role%20of%20connectivity%20in%20growth%20strategy%20of%20pakistan.pdf|archivedate=21 April 2012|format=PDF|year=2011|publisher=Planning Commission, Pakistan|pages=4, 7, 9|accessdate=31 December 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;unesep&quot;&gt;{{cite web|title=National Highway Development Sector Investment Program|url=http://www.adb.org/Documents/RRPs/PAK/37559-PAK-RRP.pdf|format=PDF|publisher=Asian Development Bank|year=2005|pages=11, 12|accessdate=31 December 2011|deadurl=yes|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/20071007150953/http://www.adb.org/Documents/RRPs/PAK/37559-PAK-RRP.pdf|archivedate=7 October 2007}}&lt;/ref&gt;

The [[Pakistan Railways]], under the [[Ministry of Railways (Pakistan)|Ministry of Railways]] (MoR), operates the railroad system. From 1947 until the 1970s, the [[Pakistan Railways|train system]] was the primary means of transport until the nationwide constructions of the [[National Highways of Pakistan|national highways]] and the [[economic boom]] of the [[Cars in Pakistan|automotive industry]]. Since the 1990s, there was a marked shift in traffic from rail to highways; dependence grew on roads after the introduction of [[Automotive industry in Pakistan|vehicles]] in the country. Now the railway's share of inland traffic is only 10% for passengers and 4% for freight traffic. Personal transportation dominated by the automobiles, the total rail track decreased from 8,775&amp;nbsp;km in 1990â€“91 to 7,791&amp;nbsp;km in 2011.&lt;ref name=&quot;pc&quot; /&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;nation&quot;&gt;{{cite web|title=PAKISTAN|url=http://www.nationsencyclopedia.com/economies/Asia-and-the-Pacific/Pakistan.html|work=Encyclopedia Nation|accessdate=31 December 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt; Pakistan expects to use the rail service to boost [[Foreign trade of Pakistan|foreign trade]] with China, Iran and Turkey.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|author=Syed Fazl-e-Haider|title=China-Pakistan rail link on horizon|url=http://www.atimes.com/atimes/South_Asia/IB24Df02.html|accessdate=31 December 2011|newspaper=Asia Times Online|date=24 February 2007}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|title=Pakistan-Turkey rail trial starts|url=http://news.bbc.co.uk/2/hi/8201934.stm|accessdate=13 March 2012|publisher=BBC|date=14 August 2009}}&lt;/ref&gt;

Rough estimates accounts for 139 [[airfield|airports]] in Pakistanâ€“both [[Military of Pakistan|military]] and [[Civil Aviation Authority (Panama)|civilian]] airports which are [[List of airports in Pakistan|mostly publicly owned]]. Though the [[Jinnah International Airport]] is the principal international gateway to Pakistan, the international airports in [[Allama Iqbal International Airport|Lahore]], [[Benazir Bhutto International Airport|Islamabad]], [[Peshawar International Airport|Peshawar]], [[Quetta International Airport|Quetta]], [[Faisalabad International Airport|Faisalabad]], [[Sialkot International Airport|Sialkot]] and [[Multan International Airport|Multan]] also handle significant amounts of traffic. The [[List of airlines of Pakistan|civil aviation industry]] is mixed with [[Nationalization in Pakistan|public]] and [[Privatization in Pakistan|private]] sectors, which has been [[Privatization in Pakistan|deregulated]] in 1993. While the [[State owned enterprises|state-owned]] [[Pakistan International Airlines]] (PIA) is the major and dominated air carrier that carries about 73% of domestic passengers and all domestic freight, the private airlines such as [[Airblue|airBlue]], [[Shaheen Air|Shaheen Air International]], and [[Air Indus]], also provide the similar services with [[Low cost carrier|low cost]] expenses.  Major seaports are in [[Karachi]], [[Sindh]] (the [[Port of Karachi|Karachi port]] and [[Port Qasim]]).&lt;ref name=&quot;pc&quot; /&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;nation&quot; /&gt; Since the 1990s, some seaport operations have been moved to [[Balochistan, Pakistan|Balochistan]] with the construction of [[Gwadar port|Gwadar Port]] and [[Gadani ship-breaking yard|Gadani Port]].&lt;ref name=&quot;pc&quot; /&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;nation&quot; /&gt; According to Mundi Index, quality ratings of Pakistan's seaports increased from 3.6 to 4 between 2006 and 2009.{{sfn|Robert Stimson|Kingsley E. Haynes|2012|p=44}}

===Science and technology===
{{Main article|Science and technology in Pakistan|List of Pakistani inventions and discoveries}}

{{multiple image
|align=right
|image1=Abdus Salam 1987.jpg

|caption2=[[Atta ur Rahman (scientist)|Atta-ur-Rahman]] won the [[UNESCO Science Prize]] for pioneering contributions in chemistry in 1999, the first Muslim to win it.
|image2=Atta-Ur-Rahman (cropped).jpg

}}

Development on [[Science and technology in Pakistan|science and technology]] plays an influential role in Pakistan's infrastructure and helped the country to reach out to the world.&lt;ref name=&quot;Ministry of Science and Technology&quot;&gt;{{cite web|last1=Ministry of Science and Technology|title=National Science, Technology and Innovation Policy 2012|url=http://most.comsatshosting.com/%5CPolicies%5CNational%20Science,%20Technology%20and%20Innovation%20Policy%202012.pdf|publisher=Ministry of Science and Technology|accessdate=3 February 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt; Every year, scientists from around the world are invited by the [[Pakistan Academy of Sciences]] and the Pakistan Government to participate in the [[International Nathiagali Summer College on Physics]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Address by Prime Minister|url=http://www.pid.gov.pk/pm%20address%20on%2027-6-2011.doc|work=Press Information Department (Government of Pakistan)|format=DOC|accessdate=24 December 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt; Pakistan hosted an international seminar on ''Physics in Developing Countries'' for International Year of Physics 2005.&lt;ref name=&quot;comsats&quot;&gt;{{cite journal |author=Hameed A. Khan|title=Physics in Developing Countries â€“ Past, Present &amp; Future|url=http://www.comsats.org/Publications/Books_SnT_Series/08.%20Physics%20in%20Developing%20Countries%20-%20Past,%20Present%20and%20Future%20(April%202006).pdf|format=PDF|page=9|publisher=COMSATS|year=2006|accessdate=1 January 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt; Pakistani theoretical physicist [[Abdus Salam]] won a [[Nobel Prize in Physics]] for his work on the [[electroweak interaction]].&lt;ref name=&quot;Nobel Prize&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://nobelprize.org/nobel_prizes/physics/laureates/1979/|title=1979 Nobel Prize in Physics|work=Nobel Prize|accessdate=24 December 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt; Influential publications and the critical scientific works in the advancement of [[mathematics]], [[biology]], [[economics]], [[computer science]], and [[genetics]] have been produced by the Pakistani scientists at the domestic and international standings.&lt;ref name=&quot;Zed, 2001&quot;&gt;{{cite book|last1=Mian|first1=ed. by Smitu Kothari &amp; Zia|title=Out of the nuclear shadow|date=2001|publisher=Zed, 2001|location=London|isbn=1842770594}}&lt;/ref&gt;

In [[chemistry]], [[Salimuzzaman Siddiqui]] was the first Pakistani scientist to bring the therapeutic constituents of the [[neem]] tree to the attention of natural products chemists.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://technologytimes.pk/documents/mag/PDF_Old/Vol02-Issue11.pdf|title=Technology Times â€“ Vol 2 â€“ Issue 11|format=PDF|accessdate=20 April 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://pakistanpressclub.com/pakistan-2012-2020-pakistan/|title=Section Science &amp; Technology|doi=10.1504/WREMSD.2007.012130|publisher=Pakistan Press Club|accessdate=20 April 2013|deadurl=yes|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/20131015220401/http://pakistanpressclub.com/pakistan-2012-2020-pakistan/|archivedate=15 October 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|last=Ahmed|first=Irshad|url=http://www.academia.edu/2425893/Using_RP_Model_to_Solve_the_Current_Challenges_of_PAKISTAN|title=Using RP Model to solve Current Challenges of Pakistan by PHd Scholar Irshad Ahmed Sumra|publisher=Academia.edu|accessdate=20 April 2013}}{{dead link|date=May 2016|bot=medic}}{{cbignore|bot=medic}}&lt;/ref&gt; Pakistani neurosurgeon [[Ayub K. Ommaya|Ayub Ommaya]] invented the [[Ommaya reservoir]], a system for treatment of brain tumours and other brain conditions.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|author=Leonidas C. Goudas|title=Decreases in Cerebrospinal Fluid Glutathione Levels after Intracerebroventricular Morphine for Cancer Pain|url=http://www.anesthesia-analgesia.org/cgi/content/full/89/5/1209|publisher=International Anesthesia Research Society|year=1999|accessdate=1 January 2012|display-authors=etal}}&lt;/ref&gt; Scientific research and development plays a pivotal role in Pakistani [[Pakistani universities|universities]], collaboration with the government sponsored national laboratories, [[science park]]s, and co-operation with the [[Industry of Pakistan|industry]].&lt;ref name=&quot;Nature&quot;&gt;{{cite journal|last1=Osama|first1=Athar|last2=Najam|first2=Adil|last3=Kassim-Lakha|first3=Shamsh|last4=Zulfiqar Gilani|first4=Syed|last5=King|first5=Christopher|title=Pakistan's reform experiment|journal=Nature|date=3 September 2009|volume=461|issue=7260|pages=38â€“39|doi=10.1038/461038a|url=http://www.nature.com/nature/journal/v461/n7260/full/461038a.html|accessdate=18 February 2015|pmid=19727184}}&lt;/ref&gt; [[Abdul Qadeer Khan]] regarded as the founder of [[High-enriched uranium|HEU]]-based [[Zippe-type centrifuge|Gas-centrifuge]] [[uranium enrichment]] program for Pakistan's integrated [[Pakistan and weapons of mass destruction|atomic bomb project]].&lt;ref name=&quot;International Institute for Strategic Studies&quot;&gt;{{cite web |last=(IISS)|first=International Institute for Strategic Studies|authorlink=International Institute for Strategic Studies|title=Bhutto was father of Pakistan's Atom Bomb Program|publisher=International Institute for Strategic Studies|year=2006|url=http://www.iiss.org/whats-new/iiss-in-the-press/press-coverage-2007/may-2007/bhutto-was-father-of-pakistani-bomb/?locale=en|accessdate=24 July 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt; He founded and established the [[Kahuta Research Laboratories]] (KRL) in 1976, being both its senior scientist and the [[Director-General]] until his retirement in 2001, and he was an early and vital figure in other [[Integrated Missile Research and Development Program|science projects]]. Apart from participating in Pakistan's [[Pakistan and weapons of mass destruction|atomic bomb project]], he made major contributions in [[Morphology (biology)|molecular morphology]], physical [[martensite]], and its integrated applications in [[Condensed matter physics|condensed]] and [[material physics]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|title=A.Q. Khan &amp; Iran|url=http://www.globalsecurity.org/wmd/world/iran/khan-iran.htm|accessdate=24 July 2015|publisher=Global Security}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|author=NY Times Staff|title=Chronology: A.Q. Khan|url=http://www.nytimes.com/2006/04/16/world/asia/16chron-khan.html?pagewanted=all|accessdate=24 July 2015|publisher=NY Times|date=April 16, 2006}}&lt;/ref&gt;

In 2010, Pakistan was ranked 43rd in the world in terms of published scientific papers.&lt;ref name=&quot;Dawn news, 2010&quot;&gt;{{cite news|last1=Junaidi|first1=Ikram|title=Pakistan ranks 43rd in scientific research publication|url=http://www.dawn.com/news/683203/pakistan-ranks-43rd-in-scientific-research-publication-2.|accessdate=18 February 2015|agency=Dawn news, 2010|publisher=Dawn news, 2010|date=25 December 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt; The Pakistan [[Pakistan Academy of Sciences|Academy of Sciences]], a strong scientific community, plays an influential and vital role in formulating the science policies recommendation to the government.&lt;ref name=&quot;Introduction of the Academy&quot;&gt;{{cite web|title=Introduction to the Academy|url=http://www.paspk.org/Introduction-to-the-Academy-19|website=http://www.paspk.org/|publisher=Inbtroduction of the Academy|accessdate=16 February 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;

The 1960s era saw the emergence of the active [[Asian Space Race|space program]] led by the [[Space and Upper Atmosphere Research Commission|SUPARCO]] that produced advances in domestic [[rocket]]ry, [[Electronics Engineering|electronics]], and [[aeronomy]].&lt;ref name=&quot;SUPARCO&quot;&gt;{{cite web|title=History of SUPARCO|url=http://www.suparco.gov.pk/pages/history.asp|work=SUPARCO|accessdate=24 December 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt;  The [[Space and Upper Atmosphere Research Commission|space program]] recorded a few notable feats and achievements. The successful launch of its [[Rehbar-I|first rocket]] into space made Pakistan the first South Asian country to have achieved such a task.&lt;ref name=&quot;SUPARCO&quot; /&gt; Successfully producing and launching the nation's [[Badr-I|first space satellite]] in 1990, Pakistan became the first Muslim country and second South Asian country to put a satellite into space.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=The Launching of Badr-I|url=http://www.aerospaceguide.net/worldspace/pakistan.html|work=Aero Space Guide|accessdate=24 December 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book |title=Asian Space Race: Rhetoric or Reality?|last=Lele|first=Ajey|publisher=Springer Science &amp; Business Media|year=2012|isbn=978-81-322-0733-7|pages=46}}&lt;/ref&gt;

As an aftermath of the [[Indo-Pakistani War of 1971|1971 war with India]], the clandestine [[Pakistan and weapons of mass destruction|crash program]] developed [[nuclear weapon|atomic weapons]] in a fear and to prevent any [[Foreign interventionism|foreign intervention]], while ushering in the [[Atomic Age|atomic age]] in the [[Post Cold War era|post cold war]] era.&lt;ref name=&quot;United Book Press.&quot;&gt;{{cite book|title=Pakistan : between mosque and military|date=2005|last1=Haqqani|first1=Husain|publisher=United Book Press.|location=Washington, DC|isbn=978-0-87003-214-1|edition=1. print.|chapter=Â§Chapter 3|quote={{small|&quot;''The trauma was extremely severe in Pakistan when the news of secession of East Pakistan as Bangladesh arrived â€” a psychological setback, complete and humiliating defeat that shattered the prestige of Pakistan Armed Forces.''&quot;}}}}&lt;/ref&gt; Competition with India and tensions eventually led Pakistan's decision of [[List of nuclear weapons tests of Pakistan|conducting underground]] [[Chagai-I|nuclear tests]] in 1998; thus becoming the [[Nuclear club|seventh country]] in the world to successfully develop [[nuclear weapon]]s.&lt;ref name=&quot;Federation of American Scientists&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=https://fas.org/nuke/guide/pakistan/nuke/index.html|title=Pakistan Nuclear Weapons|work=Federation of American Scientists|accessdate=22 February 2007}}&lt;/ref&gt;

The Islamic Republic of Pakistan is the only Muslim country that maintains a research station in Antarctica and it has maintained a presence there since 1991.&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite journal|url=http://www.fountainmagazine.com/Issue/detail/Should-We-Exploit-The-Last-Wilderness|title=Should We Exploit The Last Wilderness?|last=Sayar|first=M.A.|date=Aprilâ€“June 1995|journal=The Fountain Magazine|doi=|pmid=|access-date=9 February 2016}}&lt;/ref&gt; After establishing an [[Pakistan Antarctic Programme|Antarctic program]], Pakistan is one of the small number of countries that have an active research presence in [[Antarctica]]. The [[Pakistan Antarctic Programme|Antarctic program]] oversees two summer research stations on the continent and plans to open another base, which will operate all year round.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.niopk.gov.pk/Antarctic.aspx|title=Antarctic Research|work=[[National Institute of Oceanography (Pakistan)]]|accessdate=29 December 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt; Energy consumption by [[computer]]s and usage has grown since the 1990s when the [[Personal computer|PCs]] were introduced; Pakistan has over 20 million internet users and is ranked as one of the top countries that have registered a high growth rate in internet penetration, {{As of|2011|lc=y}}.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|url=http://tribune.com.pk/story/283253/internet-users-in-pakistan-cross-20-million-mark/|title=Internet users in Pakistan cross 20 million mark|work=Express Tribune|date=28 October 2011|accessdate=26 May 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt; Key publications has been produced by Pakistan, and domestic software development has gained a lot international praise.&lt;ref name=&quot;Lahore Tech&quot;&gt;{{cite news|last1=staff works|title=Pakistani Computer Scientist wins global Supercomputer Design Award|url=http://www.techlahore.com/2010/05/10/pakistani-computer-scientist-wins-global-supercomputer-design-award/.|accessdate=19 February 2015|agency=Lahore Tech|publisher=Lahore Tech|date=10 May 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt;

Overall, it has the [[List of countries by number of Internet users|27th largest population]] of internet users in the world. Since the 2000s, Pakistan has made significant amount of progress in [[Supercomputing in Pakistan|supercomputing]], and various institutions offers research in [[parallel computing]]. [[Government of Pakistan|Pakistan government]] reportedly spends [[Pakistan Rupee|â‚¨.]] 4.6 billion on [[Information technology in Pakistan|information technology]] projects, with emphasis on [[E-Government in Pakistan|e-government]], human resource and infrastructure development.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|url=http://tribune.com.pk/story/432124/govt-to-spend-rs4-6b-on-it-projects/|title=Govt to spend Rs4.6b on IT projects|work=Express Tribune|date=6 September 2012|accessdate=6 September 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt;

===Education===
{{Main article|Education in Pakistan|Higher Education Commission}}
[[File:Government College University.jpg|thumb|200px|right|[[Government College University (Lahore)|Government College University]] is one of the oldest universities in Pakistan as well as one of the oldest institutions of higher learning in the Muslim world.]]
The [[Constitution of Pakistan|Constitution]] of Pakistan requires the state to provide [[Free education|free]] [[Primary education|primary]] and [[Secondary education|secondary]] [[education]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.pakistani.org/pakistan/constitution/part2.ch1.html|title=Chapter 1: &quot;Fundamental Rights&quot; of Part II: &quot;Fundamental Rights and Principles of Policy&quot;|work=pakistani.org}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Right to Education in Pakistan|publisher=World Council of Churches|url=http://www.oikoumene.org/resources/documents/wcc-commissions/international-affairs/human-rights-and-impunity/the-right-to-education-in-pakistan.html|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/web/20120313083147/http://www.oikoumene.org/resources/documents/wcc-commissions/international-affairs/human-rights-and-impunity/the-right-to-education-in-pakistan.html|archivedate=13 March 2012|date=21 April 2006|accessdate=25 July 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt; At the time of [[Creation of Pakistan|establishment]] of Pakistan as state, the country had only one university, the [[University of the Punjab|Punjab University]] in [[Lahore]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|author1=Sajida Mukhtar|author2=Ijaz Ahmed Talat|author3=Muhammad Saeed|title=An Analytical Study of Higher Education of Pakistan|url=http://www.ijar.lit.az/pdf/10/2011%2810-51%29.pdf|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/web/20120111100415/http://www.ijar.lit.az/pdf/10/2011%2810-51%29.pdf|archivedate=11 January 2012|publisher=International Journal of Academic Research|format=PDF|date=March 2011|page=1|accessdate=2 January 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt; On immediate basis, the [[Government of Pakistan|Pakistan government]] established public universities in each [[Four Provinces (Pakistan)|four provinices]] including the [[Sindh University]] (1949), [[University of Peshawar|Peshawar University]] (1950), [[University of Karachi|Karachi University]] (1953), and [[University of Balochistan|Balochistan University]] (1970). Pakistan has a large network of both [[public universities|public]] and [[public universities|private]] [[Universities in Pakistan|universities]]; a collaboration of [[Public Private Partnerships|public-private]] [[Universities in Pakistan|universities]] to provide research and [[Higher education in Pakistan|higher education]] in the country, although there is concern about the low quality of teaching in many of the newer schools.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Number of universities rises while education standard falls|url=http://www.dailytimes.com.pk/islamabad/10-Sep-2015/number-of-universities-rises-while-education-standard-falls|publisher=DailyTimes|date=10 September 2015|accessdate=11 September 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt; It is estimated that there are 3193 [[vocational education|technical and vocational institutions]] in Pakistan,&lt;ref name=&quot;edu2&quot; /&gt; and there are also [[Madrassas in Pakistan|''madrassah''s]] that provide free Islamic education and offer free board and lodging to students, who come mainly from the poorer strata of society.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Pakistani madrassahs:|publisher=United States Institute of Peace|url=http://www.uvm.edu/~envprog/madrassah.html#_ftn8|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/web/20050214194645/http://www.uvm.edu/~envprog/madrassah.html#_ftn8|archivedate=14 February 2005|accessdate=21 February 2009}}&lt;/ref&gt; Strongly instigated public pressure and popular criticism over the [[Pakistan Taleban|extremists]] usage of [[Madrassas|''madrassahs'']] for recruitment, the [[GoP|Pakistan government]] has made repeated efforts to [[Education reform|regulate and monitor]] the quality of education in the [[Madrassas|''madrassahs'']].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|first=Ron|author=Synovitz|title=Pakistan: Despite Reform Plan, Few Changes Seen At Most Radical Madrassahs|url=http://www.rferl.org/content/article/1051650.html|publisher=Radio Free Europe Radio Liberty|date=24 February 2004|accessdate=21 February 2009}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Jinnah Institute of Peace&quot;&gt;{{cite web|last1=Ali|first1=Syed Mohammad|title=Policy Brief: Another Approach to Madrassa Reforms in Pakistan|url=http://jinnah-institute.org/policy-brief-another-approach-to-madrassa-reforms-in-pakistan-3/|website=http://jinnah-institute.org/|publisher=Jinnah Institute of Peace|accessdate=21 February 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;

[[Education in Pakistan|Education]] in Pakistan is divided into six main levels: [[Nursery education|nursery]] (preparatory classes); [[Elementary school|primary]] (grades one through five); [[middle school|middle]] (grades six through eight); [[High school|matriculation]] (grades nine and ten, leading to the [[Secondary School Certificate|secondary certificate]]); [[Community college|intermediate]] (grades eleven and twelve, leading to a [[Higher Secondary (School) Certificate|higher secondary certificate]]); and university programmes leading to graduate and postgraduate programs.&lt;ref name=&quot;edu2&quot;&gt;{{cite web|title=Economic Survey 2009â€“10|url=http://www.finance.gov.pk/survey/chapter_10/10_Education.pdf|publisher=Ministry of Finance, Pakistan|format=PDF|date=2009â€“2010|pages=16, 3|accessdate=2 January 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt; Network of Pakistani [[Schools in Pakistan|private schools]] also operate a parallel secondary education system based on the curriculum set and administered by the [[Cambridge International Examinations]] of the [[United Kingdom]]. Some students choose to take the [[Ordinary Level|O-level]] and [[GCE Advanced Level|A level]] exams conducted by the [[British Council]].&lt;ref name=&quot;britishcouncil&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.britishcouncil.org/pakistan-exams-gce.htm|title=GCE O and A level exams in Pakistan|publisher=The British Council|accessdate=13 February 2008|deadurl=yes|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/20080201090835/http://www.britishcouncil.org:80/pakistan-exams-gce.htm|archivedate=1 February 2008}}&lt;/ref&gt; According to the International Schools Consultancy, Pakistan has 439 international schools.&lt;ref name=&quot;iscresearch.com&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.iscresearch.com/information/isc-news.aspx|title=International School Consultancy Group &gt; Information &gt; ISC News|work=iscresearch.com}}&lt;/ref&gt;

[[File:Girls in school in Khyber Pakhtunkhwa, Pakistan (7295675962).jpg|thumb|250px|left|Girls in a public school in [[Khyber Pakhtunkhwa]].]]
Initiatives taken in 2007, the [[English medium education]] has been made compulsory to all schools across the country.&lt;ref name=&quot;British Council Pakistan Bureau&quot;&gt;{{cite web|last1=McNicoll|first1=Kristen|title=English medium education improvement in Pakistan supported|url=http://www.britishcouncil.org/organisation/press/english-medium-education-improvement-pakistan-supported|website=http://www.britishcouncil.org/|publisher=British Council Pakistan Bureau|accessdate=21 February 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;moe3&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.moe.gov.pk/mediacell.htm|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/web/20070105215406/http://www.moe.gov.pk/mediacell.htm|archivedate=5 January 2007|title=Ministry of Education-Government of Pakistan|publisher=Moe.gov.pk|accessdate=1 January 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt; Additional reforms taken in 2013, all educational institutions in [[Sindh]] began instructions in [[Chinese language]] courses, reflecting China's growing role as a superpower and [[People's Republic of Chinaâ€“Pakistan relations|increasing influence]] in Pakistan.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|url=http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-south-asia-14787216|title=Schools in Pakistan's Sindh province to teach Chinese|publisher=BBC|date=5 September 2011|accessdate=23 October 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt; The literacy rate of the population is ~58 %. Male literacy is ~70.2% while female literacy rate is 46.3%.&lt;ref name=&quot;edu&quot;&gt;{{cite web|title=Labour Force Survey 2010â€“11|url=http://www.pbs.gov.pk/sites/default/files/Labour%20Force/publications/lfs2010_11/results.pdf|work=|publisher=Federal Bureau of Statistics, Pakistan|year=2011|page=12|accessdate=2 July 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt; Literacy rates vary by region and particularly by sex; for instance, female literacy in tribal areas is 3.0%.&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite news|author=Nicholas D Kristof|title=Pakistan and Times Sq|url=http://www.nytimes.com/2010/05/13/opinion/13kristof.html|newspaper=New York Times|date=12 May 2010|accessdate=1 January 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;!---note that hardcopy and softcopy refs are from different sources. Nonetheless, they are the same article--&gt; With the launch of the [[computer literacy]] in 1995, the government launched a nationwide initiative in 1998 with the aim of eradicating [[illiteracy]] and providing a basic education to all children.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=Education in Pakistan|publisher=UNICEF|url=http://www.unicef.org/infobycountry/pakistan_pakistan_statistics.html#67|accessdate=25 July 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt; Through various educational reforms, by 2015 the [[Minister for Education (Pakistan)|MoEd]] expects to attain 100.00% enrollment levels among children of primary school age and a literacy rate of ~86% among people aged over 10.&lt;ref name=&quot;moe4&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.moe.gov.pk/npaEFA.zip|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/web/20060517232352/http://www.moe.gov.pk/npaEFA.zip|archivedate=17 May 2006|format=ZIP|title=National Plan of Action 2001â€“2015|publisher=Ministry of Education, Government of Pakistan|accessdate=13 February 2008}}&lt;/ref&gt;

After earning their [[Higher Secondary (School) Certificate|HSC]], students may study in a professional college or the university for [[Bachelor's degree|bachelorate program]] courses such as [[Bachelor of Engineering|science and engineering]] ([[Bachelor of Engineering|BEng]], [[Bachelor of Science|BS]]/[[Bachelor of Science|BSc]], [[Bachelor of Technology|BTech]]) [[Bachelor of Medicine, Bachelor of Surgery|surgery and medicine]] ([[Bachelor of Medicine, Bachelor of Surgery|MBBS]], [[Doctor of Medicine|MD]]), [[Dental degree|dentistry]] ([[Bachelor of Dentistry|BDS]]), [[veterinary medicine]] ([[Doctor of Veterinary Medicine|DVM]]), [[Bachelor of Laws|criminal justice and law]] ([[Bachelor of Laws|LLB]], [[Master of Laws|LLM]], [[Juris Doctor|JD]]), [[Bachelor of Architecture|architecture]] ([[Bachelor of Architecture|BArch]]), [[Doctor of Pharmacy#Pakistan|pharmacy]] ([[Doctor of Pharmacy|Pharm D.]]) and [[nursing]] ([[Bachelor of Nursing|BNurs]]). Students can also attend a university for a [[bachelorate]] degree for [[Business Administration|business administration]], [[literature]], and [[Management science|management]] including the [[Bachelor of Arts|BA]], [[Bachelor of Commerce|BCom]], [[Bachelor of Business Administration|BBA]], and [[Master of Business Administration|MBA]] programs. The [[Higher education in Pakistan|higher education]] mainly supervises by the [[Higher Education Commission]] (HEC) that sets out the policies and issues rankings of the [[Pakistani universities|nationwide universities]].  In October 2014, education activist [[Malala Yousafzai]] became by far the youngest ever person in the world to receive the [[Nobel peace prize]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.breakingnewsenglish.com/1410/141014-malala-yousafzai.html|title=Breaking News English|work=www.breakingnewsenglish.com}}&lt;/ref&gt;

=== Water supply and sanitation ===
{{Main article|Water supply and sanitation in Pakistan}}Despite high population growth the country has increased the share of the population with access to an [[improved water source]] from 85% in 1990 to 92% in 2010, although this does not necessarily mean that the water from these sources is safe to drink. The share with access to [[improved sanitation]] increased from 27% to 48% during the same period, according to the [[Joint Monitoring Program for Water Supply and Sanitation]].&lt;ref name=&quot;ADB 11&quot;&gt;{{cite journal|last=Bridges|first=Geoff|author2=Asian Development Bank (ADB)|authorlink2=Asian Development Bank|title=Asian Water Development Outlook 2007. Country Paper Pakistan|year=2007|url=http://www.adb.org/publications/asian-water-development-outlook-2007|page=11|accessdate=2008-05-28}}&lt;/ref&gt; There has also been considerable innovation at the grass-root level, in particular concerning sanitation. The [[Orangi Pilot Project]] in Karachi&lt;ref name=&quot;Welle&quot;&gt;{{cite journal|last=Welle|first=Katherina|title=Mapping as a basis for sanitation implementation in Pakistan: the case of the Orangi Pilot Project|journal=Beyond construction. Use by all. A collection of case studies from sanitation and hygiene promotion practitioners in South Asia|pages=95â€“110|publisher=[[WaterAid]], IRC International Water and Sanitation Centre|location=London|date=March 2008|url=http://www.irc.nl/redir/content/download/133761/379575/file/IRC-2008-Beyond_construction.pdf|isbn=978-9937-2-0472-9|accessdate=2013-10-09}}{{dead link|date=May 2016|bot=medic}}{{cbignore|bot=medic}}&lt;/ref&gt; and [[community-led total sanitation]] in rural areas are two examples of such innovation.

However, the sector still faces major challenges. The quality of the services is poor, as evidenced by intermittent water 
supply in urban areas and limited [[Sewage treatment|wastewater treatment]]. Poor drinking water quality and sanitation lead to major outbreaks of [[waterborne diseases]].&lt;ref name=&quot;ADB 9&quot;&gt;{{cite journal|last=Bridges|first=Geoff|author2=Asian Development Bank (ADB)|authorlink2=Asian Development Bank|title=Asian Water Development Outlook 2007. Country Paper Pakistan|year=2007|url=http://www.adb.org/publications/asian-water-development-outlook-2007|page=9|accessdate=2008-05-28}}&lt;/ref&gt; major outbreaks of waterborne diseases swept the cities of [[Faisalabad]], [[Karachi]], [[Lahore]] and [[Peshawar]] in 2006.&lt;ref name=&quot;ADB 9&quot; /&gt; Estimates indicate that each year, more than three million Pakistanis become infected with waterborne diseases.&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite news|newspaper=Weekly Independent|date=2005-03-17}}
&lt;nowiki&gt;;&lt;/nowiki&gt; cited in&lt;nowiki&gt;:&lt;/nowiki&gt;{{cite journal|last=Water and Sanitation Program|title=Managing Karachi's water supply and sanitation services: lessons from a workshop|date=August 2004|url=http://www-wds.worldbank.org/external/default/WDSContentServer/WDSP/IB/2005/11/28/000160016_20051128162902/Rendered/PDF/344430PK0Karachi0water0supply.pdf|page=3|accessdate=2008-06-04}}&lt;/ref&gt;
In addition, many service providers do not even cover the costs of and maintenance due to low tariffs and poor efficiency.&lt;ref name=&quot;ADB 12-13&quot;&gt;{{cite journal|last=Bridges|first=Geoff|author2=Asian Development Bank (ADB)|authorlink2=Asian Development Bank|title=Asian Water Development Outlook 2007. Country Paper Pakistan|year=2007|url=http://www.adb.org/publications/asian-water-development-outlook-2007|pages=12â€“13|accessdate=2008-05-28}}&lt;/ref&gt; Consequently, the service providers strongly depend on government subsidies and external funding.&lt;ref name=&quot;MOPW5 105&quot;&gt;{{cite journal|last=Government of Pakistan. Ministry of Water and Power|title=Pakistan Water Sector Strategy. Water Sector Profile. Volume 5|date=October 2002|url=http://www.waterinfo.net.pk/pdf/vol5.pdf|page=105|accessdate=2008-05-29|deadurl=yes|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/20061231173832/http://www.waterinfo.net.pk:80/pdf/vol5.pdf|archivedate=31 December 2006}}&lt;/ref&gt;
A National Sanitation Policy and a National Drinking Water Policy have been approved in 2006 and 2009 respectively with the objective to 
improve water and sanitation coverage and quality.&lt;ref name=&quot;Rupee 2004&quot;&gt;1 Pakistani Rupee = US$0.01684 (2004-12-31); source: [http://oanda.com http://oanda.com]&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite journal|last=Government of Pakistan. Ministry of Planning and Development|title=Medium Term Development Framework 2005â€“10. Section 10: Water and Sanitation|location=Islamabad|year=2004|url=http://www.pakistan.gov.pk/ministries/planninganddevelopment-ministry/mtdf/10-Water%20and%20Sanitation/10-Water%20&amp;%20Sanitation.pdf|accessdate=2008-05-29}} {{Dead link|date=October 2010|bot=H3llBot}}
&lt;/ref&gt; However, the level of annual investment (US$4/capita) still remains 
much below what would be necessary to achieve a significant increase in access and service quality.

==Demographics==
{{Main article|Demographics of Pakistan|Demographic history of Pakistan|Pakistanis}}
[[File:Nagan_Ch_Karachi.jpg|thumb|300px|left|[[Karachi]] is the largest city of Pakistan and [[Megacity|7th largest in the world]] with a population of about 24 million.]]
[[File:Kalash women traditional clothing.jpg|thumb|right|[[Kalash people]] maintain a unique identity and religion within Pakistan.]]
As per [[United States Census Bureau]] estimates the country's population is at 199,085,847 (199.1 million) {{As of|2015|lc=y}},&lt;ref name=&quot;USCB&quot; /&gt; which is equivalent to 2.57% of the [[world population]].&lt;ref name=&quot;World Meters&quot;&gt;{{cite web|last1=World Meters staff works|title=Pakistan Population|url=http://www.worldometers.info/world-population/pakistan-population/|website=http://www.worldometers.info/|publisher=World Meters|accessdate=2 March 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt; Noted as the sixth [[List of countries and dependencies by population|most populated]] country in the world, its growth rate is reported at ~2.03%, which is the highest of the [[South Asian Association for Regional Cooperation|SAARC nations]] and gives an annual increase of 3.6 million. The population is projected to reach 210.13 million by 2020 and to double by 2045.

At the time of the [[Partition of India|partition]] in 1947, Pakistan had a population of 32.5&amp;nbsp;million,&lt;ref name=&quot;young&quot; /&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;popu1&quot;&gt;{{cite news|title=High population growth rate affecting economy'|url=http://www.dailytimes.com.pk/default.asp?page=2011\07\12\story_12-7-2011_pg7_9|accessdate=19 December 2011|newspaper=Daily Times|date=12 July 2011|deadurl=yes|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/20120112000723/http://www.dailytimes.com.pk/default.asp?page=2011%5C07%5C12%5Cstory_12-7-2011_pg7_9|archivedate=12 January 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt; but the population increased by ~57.2% between the years [[Fiscal year|1990 and 2009]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=CO2 Emissions from Fuel Combustion|url=http://iea.org/co2highlights/co2highlights.pdf|publisher=International Energy Agency (IEA) Paris|format=PDF|year=2011|page=88|accessdate=2 January 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt; By 2030, it is expected to surpass [[Indonesia]] as the largest Muslim-majority country in the world.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.aina.org/news/20110127104753.htm|title=World Muslim Population Doubling, Report Projects|publisher=Assyrian International News Agency|date=27 January 2011|accessdate=16 April 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.samaa.tv/newsdetail.aspx?ID=27990|title=Pakistan set to become most populous Muslim nation|publisher=Samaa Tv|date=27 January 2011|accessdate=16 April 2011|deadurl=yes|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/20110226230233/http://www.samaa.tv:80/newsdetail.aspx?ID=27990|archivedate=26 February 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt; Pakistan is classified as a &quot;young nation&quot; with a median age of about 22,  and 104 million people under the age of 30 in 2010. Pakistan's fertility rate stands at 3.07, higher than its neighbor [[India]] (2.57). Around 35% of the people are under 15.&lt;ref name=&quot;young&quot; /&gt;

Vast majority residing in [[Southern Pakistan]] lives along the [[Indus River]], with [[Karachi]] being its most populous commercial city.&lt;ref name=&quot;The Urban Frontierâ€”Karachi&quot;&gt;{{cite web|title=The Urban Frontierâ€”Karachi|url=http://www.npr.org/templates/story/story.php?storyId=91009748|publisher=National Public Radio|date=2 June 2008|accessdate=2 July 2008}}&lt;/ref&gt; In the eastern, [[Khyber Pakhtunkhwa|western]], and [[Northern Pakistan]], most of the population lives in an arc formed by the cities of [[Lahore]], [[Faisalabad]], [[Rawalpindi]], [[Sargodha]], [[Islamabad]], [[Gujranwala]], [[Sialkot]], [[Gujrat city|Gujrat]], [[Jhelum]], [[Sheikhupura]], [[Nowshera, Khyber-Pakhtunkhwa|Nowshera]], [[Mardan]] and [[Peshawar]].&lt;ref name=&quot;ciafactbook&quot; /&gt; During [[Fiscal year|1990â€“2008]], the city dwellers made up 36% of Pakistan's population, making it the [[Urbanisation in Pakistan|most urbanised nation]] in [[South Asia]] which further increased to 38% by 2013.&lt;ref name=&quot;ciafactbook&quot; /&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;young&quot; /&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;who.int&quot; /&gt; Furthermore, 50% of Pakistanis live in towns of 5,000 people or more.&lt;ref name=&quot;Jason Burke&quot;&gt;{{Cite news|url=http://www.guardian.co.uk/world/2008/aug/17/pakistan|work=The Guardian|location=London|title=Pakistan looks to life without the general|author=Jason Burke|date=17 August 2008|accessdate=20 May 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt;

=== Health ===
[[2009â€“10 Pakistan federal budget|Expenditure]] spend on [[Health care in Pakistan|healthcare]] was ~2.8% of [[GDP]] in 2013. Life expectancy at birth was 67 years for females and 65 years for males in 2013.&lt;ref name=&quot;who.int&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.who.int/countries/pak/en/|title=WHO &amp;#124; Pakistan|publisher=World Health Organization|date=6 October 2015|accessdate=6 October 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt; The private sector accounts for about 80% of outpatient visits. Approximately 19% of the population and 30% of children under five are malnourished.&lt;ref name=&quot;raid&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.rad-aid.org/UploadedFiles/RAD-AID%20Pakistan%20Health%20Care%20Radiology%20Report%202011.pdf|title=Pakistan Country Report|work=RAD-AID|format=PDF|year=2010|pages=3, 7|accessdate=26 December 2011|deadurl=yes|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/20120112021042/http://www.rad-aid.org/UploadedFiles/RAD-AID%20Pakistan%20Health%20Care%20Radiology%20Report%202011.pdf|archivedate=12 January 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt; Mortality of the under-fives was 86 per 1,000 live births in 2012.&lt;ref name=&quot;who.int&quot; /&gt;

=== Languages ===
{{bar box
|float = right
|title = First languages of Pakistan&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.bbc.com/news/world-asia-34215293|title=Uncommon tongue: Pakistan's confusing move to Urdu|work=BBC News|accessdate=7 February 2016}}&lt;/ref&gt;
|bars =
{{bar percent|[[Punjabi language|Punjabi]]|DarkSlateGray|48}}
{{bar percent|[[Sindhi language|Sindhi]]|DarkSlateGray|12}}
{{bar percent|Punjabi ([[Saraiki dialect|Saraiki]])|DarkSlateGray|10}}
{{bar percent|[[Pashto language|Pashto]]|DarkSlateGray|8}}
{{bar percent|[[Urdu language|Urdu]]|DarkSlateGray|8}}
{{bar percent|[[Balochi language|Balochi]]|DarkSlateGray|3}}
{{bar percent|Others|DarkSlateGray|11}}
}}
More than [[Languages of Pakistan|sixty languages]] are spoken in Pakistan, including [[Provincial languages of Pakistan|a number of provincial languages]]. [[Urdu]]â€” the ''[[lingua franca]]'', a symbol of [[Muslim nationalism in South Asia|Muslim identity]], and national unityâ€” is the national language which is understood by over 75% of Pakistanis and the main source of [[Communications in Pakistan|nationwide]] [[communication]] but is only the primary language of 8% of Pakistan's population.&lt;ref name=&quot;yasmeen&quot; /&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;National language&quot;&gt;{{cite book|title=Language in South Asia|publisher=Cambridge University Press|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=O2n4sFGDEMYC&amp;pg=PA138#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false|author=Braj B. Kachru|author2=Yamuna Kachru|author3=S.N. Sridhar|page=138|isbn=9781139465502|date=27 March 2008}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;UrduOfficial?&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://nation.com.pk/editorials/31-Dec-2015/urdu-in-contempt|title=Urdu In Contempt|date=31 December 2015|work=The Nation|accessdate=12 January 2016}}&lt;/ref&gt; [[Urdu]] and [[English language|English]] are the [[official language]]s of Pakistan, however English is primarily used in official business, government, and legal contracts;&lt;ref name=&quot;ciafactbook&quot; /&gt; the local dialect is known as [[Pakistani English]]. The [[Punjabi language]] is the most common in Pakistan and is mother-tongue of 66% of Pakistan's population mostly of people in [[Punjab, Pakistan|Punjab]].{{sfn|Pakistan Narcotics Control Board|1986|p=7}} This includes 48% of Standard Punjabi speakers&lt;ref name=&quot;UrduOfficial?&quot; /&gt; as well as regional [[Punjabi dialects]] such as [[Saraiki dialect|Saraiki]] and [[Hindko dialect|Hindko]]. Saraiki dialect is mainly spoken in [[South Punjab]] and counts up to 10% of Pakistan's population, while the Hindko dialect is spoken in [[Khyber Pakhtunkhwa]]. In [[Khyber Pakhtunkhwa|Khyber-Pakhtunkhwa]], the [[Pashto language|Pashto]] language is the provincial language and is well understood in [[Sindh]] and [[Balochistan, Pakistan|Balochistan]].&lt;ref name=&quot;state.gov&quot;/&gt; The [[Sindhi language|Sindhi]] language is the common language spoken in [[Sindh, Pakistan|Sindh]] while the [[Balochi language|Balochi]] language is dominant in [[Balochistan, Pakistan|Balochistan]].&lt;ref name=&quot;state.gov&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.state.gov/r/pa/ei/bgn/3453.htm|title=Background Note: Pakistan-Profile|publisher=State.Gov|date=6 October 2010|accessdate=29 May 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;congress&quot; /&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;britishcouncil.org&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.britishcouncil.org/pakistan-ette-english-language-report.pdf|title=Teaching and Learning in Pakistan: The Role of Language in Education|publisher=British Council.Org|pages=13, 14, 15|year=2010|accessdate=29 May 2012|deadurl=yes|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/20110304130317/http://www.britishcouncil.org:80/pakistan-ette-english-language-report.pdf|archivedate=4 March 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt;

=== Immigration ===
The [[Census in Pakistan|Pakistan Census]] excludes the [[Immigration to Pakistan|immigrants]] such as the 1.7&amp;nbsp;million registered [[Afghans in Pakistan|Afghans]] from [[Afghanistan]], who are found mainly in the [[Khyber Pakhtunkhwa|Khyber-Pakhtunkhwa]] and [[Federally Administered Tribal Areas|tribal belt]] with small numbers residing in [[Karachi]] and [[Quetta]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|author=United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees|url=http://www.unhcr.org/cgi-bin/texis/vtx/page?page=49e487016|title=2010 UNHCR country operations profile â€“ Pakistan|publisher=UNHCR|accessdate=16 April 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.nrc.no/arch/_img/9268480.pdf|title=Future Floods of Refugees|format=PDF|work=Norwegian Refugee Council|date=April 2008|page=25|accessdate=17 December 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt; As of 1995, there were more than 1.6 million [[Bangladeshis in Pakistan|Bengalis]], 650,000 [[Afghans in Pakistan|Afghans]], 200,000 [[Burmese people in Pakistan|Burmese]], 2,320 [[Iranians in Pakistan|Iranians]], and [[Filipinos in Pakistan|Filipinos]], and hundreds of [[Nepalis in Pakistan|Nepalese]], [[Tamils in Pakistan|Sri Lankans]], and [[Indian Muslims in Pakistan|Indians]] living in [[Karachi]].&lt;ref name=&quot;1995refugee-breakup&quot;&gt;{{cite news|title=Homeless In Karachi|url=http://www.outlookindia.com/article.aspx?200305|accessdate=18 December 2011|newspaper=Outlookindia.com|date=29 November 1995|author=Owais Tohid|author2=Arshad Mahmud|quote=Officials say there are more than 1.6 million Bengalis, 650,000 Afghans, 200,000 Burmese, 2,320 Iranians and Filipinos and hundreds of Nepalese, Sri Lankans and Indians living in Karachi. The officials believe they pose a threat to Karachi, a city already stricken by political violence that has claimed more than 1,650 lives this year. Many of these immigrants have fake Pakistani passports and identity cards.}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Burmese in Pak&quot;&gt;{{cite news|author=Derek Flood|title=From South to South: Refugees as Migrants: The Rohingya in Pakistan|url=http://www.huffingtonpost.com/derek-flood/from-south-to-south-refug_b_100387.html|accessdate=1 February 2012|newspaper=The Huffington Post|date=12 May 2008}}&lt;/ref&gt; Pakistan hosts more refugees than any other country in the world.&lt;ref name=&quot;Pak-top refugee host-AusHRC&quot;&gt;{{cite web|title=Questions and Answers About Refugees &amp; Asylum Seekers|url=http://www.hreoc.gov.au/racial_discrimination/face_facts_05/refugee.html#endnote7|publisher=Australian Human Rights Commission|accessdate=December 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt;

=== Social groups ===
The population is dominated by [[Four Provinces (Pakistan)|four main ethnic groups]]: [[Punjabi people|Punjabis]], [[Pashtun people|Pashtuns]] (Pathans), [[Sindhi people|Sindhis]], and [[Baloch people|Balochs]].&lt;ref name=&quot;Brookings population 2010&quot; /&gt; Rough accounts from 2009 indicate that the [[Punjabi people|Punjabis]] dominate with 78.7&amp;nbsp;million (~45%) while the [[Pashtun people|Pashtuns]] are the second dominating group with ~29.3&amp;nbsp;million (15.42%).&lt;ref name=&quot;Brookings population 2010&quot; /&gt; The [[Sindhi people|Sindhis]] are estimated at 24.8&amp;nbsp;million (14.1%) with [[Saraiki people|Seraikis]] a sub-group of Punjabis is approximated at 14.8&amp;nbsp;million (8.4%).&lt;ref name=&quot;Brookings population 2010&quot; /&gt; The [[Urdu speaking|Urdu-speaking]] [[Muhajir (Pakistan)|''Muhajirs'']] (the [[Indian Muslims in Pakistan|Indian emigrants]]) stands at ~13.3&amp;nbsp;million (7.57%) while [[Baloch people|Balochs]] are accounted at 6.3&amp;nbsp;million (3.57%)â€“ the smallest group in population terms.&lt;ref name=&quot;Brookings population 2010&quot; /&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;diaspora&quot; /&gt; The remaining 11.1&amp;nbsp;million (4.66%) belong to various ethnic minorities such as [[Hazara people|Hazaras]] and [[Kalash people|Kalashs]].&lt;ref name=&quot;Brookings population 2010&quot;&gt;{{cite web|author1=Ian S. Livingston|author2=Michael O'Hanlon|title=Pakistan Index|url=http://www.brookings.edu/~/media/Files/Programs/FP/pakistan%20index/index.pdf|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/web/20100327044026/http://www.brookings.edu/~/media/Files/Programs/FP/pakistan%20index/index.pdf|archivedate=27 March 2010|publisher=Brookings population 2010|format=PDF|date=29 November 2011|page=13|accessdate=25 December 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt; There is also a large [[Pakistani diaspora]], numbering over seven million residing worldwide.&lt;ref name=&quot;diaspora&quot;&gt;{{cite web|author=Nadia Mushtaq Abbasi|title=The Pakistani Diaspora in Europe and Its Impact on Democracy Building in Pakistan|url=http://www.idea.int/resources/analysis/upload/Abbasi_low_2-2.pdf|format=PDF|work=[[International Institute for Democracy and Electoral Assistance]]|year=2010|page=5|accessdate=18 December 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt;
{{Largest cities of Pakistan}}

===Religion===
{{Main article|Religion in Pakistan}}
{{see also|Religion in South Asia}}
{{bar box
|title=Religions in Pakistan&lt;ref name=&quot;cia-rel&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=https://www.cia.gov/library/publications/the-world-factbook/fields/2122.html#pk|title=Religions in Pakistan|publisher=[[CIA World Factbook]]|accessdate=9 July 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;Curtis, Lisa; Mullick, Haider (4 May 2009). &quot;Reviving Pakistan's Pluralist Traditions to Fight Extremism&quot;. The Heritage Foundation. Retrieved 31 July 2011&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;a b c &quot;Religions: Islam 95%, other (includes Christian and Hindu, 2% Ahmadiyyah ) 5%&quot;. CIA. The World Factbook on Pakistan. 2010. Retrieved 28 August 2010.&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;# ^ International Centre for Political Violence and Terrorism Research at Nanyang Technological University, Singapore: &quot;Have Pakistanis Forgotten Their Sufi Traditions?&quot; by Rohan Bedi April 2006&lt;/ref&gt;
