[[File:Faisal Masjid.jpg|thumb|272x272px|[[Faisal Mosque]], built in 1986 by Turkish architect [[Vedat Dalokay]] on behalf of [[House of Saud|King]] [[Faisal bin Abdul-Aziz]] of Saudi Arabia.]]
Pakistan is the second most populous [[Islam by country|Muslim-majority country]]&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|author=Robert U. Ayres|title=Turning Point: The End of the Growth Paradigm.|year=1998|publisher=James &amp; James publishers|isbn=1-85383-439-4|page=63}}&lt;/ref&gt; and has the third largest [[Shia Islam in Pakistan|Shia population]] in the world after [[Iran]] and [[India]] with a Shia population of about 42.5 million.&lt;ref name=&quot;shianumbers.com&quot;&gt;http://shianumbers.com/shia-muslims-population.html&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;PewMapping&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://pewforum.org/Muslim/Mapping-the-Global-Muslim-Population%286%29.aspx|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/web/20100327201319/http://pewforum.org/Muslim/Mapping-the-Global-Muslim-Population%286%29.aspx|archivedate=27 March 2010|title=Mapping the Global Muslim Population: A Report on the Size and Distribution of the World's Muslim Population|editor=Tracy Miller|date=October 2009|publisher=[[Pew Research Center]]|accessdate=9 June 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;CIAr&quot; /&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;W.W. Norton&quot;&gt;{{cite book|last1=Nasr|first1=Vali|title=The Shia revival : how conflicts within Islam will shape the future|date=2007|publisher=W.W. Norton|location=New York|isbn=0393329682|edition=Paperback}}&lt;/ref&gt; About 97.0% of [[Pakistanis]] are Muslims. The majority are [[Sunni Islam|Sunni]], with an estimated 10â€“25% Shia.&lt;ref name=&quot;congress&quot; /&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;shianumbers.com&quot;/&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;CIAr&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=https://www.cia.gov/library/publications/the-world-factbook/fields/2122.html?countryName=Pakistan&amp;countryCode=pk&amp;regionCode=sas&amp;#pk|title=Field Listing : Religions|accessdate=24 August 2010|publisher=[[Central Intelligence Agency]]|work=[[The World Factbook]]|year=2010}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Pew&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.pewforum.org/2012/08/09/the-worlds-muslims-unity-and-diversity-1-religious-affiliation/#identity|title=Chapter 1: Religious Affiliation|date=August 9, 2012|work=The World's Muslims: Unity and Diversity|publisher=[[Pew Research Center]]'s Religion &amp; Public Life Project|accessdate=26 June 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt; The [[Ahmadiyya in Pakistan|Ahmadis]], are another minority sect in Pakistan, albeit in much smaller numbers and are officially considered non-Muslims by virtue of the [[Second Amendment to the Constitution of Pakistan|constitutional amendment]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.state.gov/g/drl/rls/irf/2008/108505.htm|title=International Religious Freedom Report 2008: Pakistan|publisher=[[United States Department of State|US State Department]]|accessdate=24 June 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt; There are also several [[Quraniyoon]] communities.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.southasianmedia.net/profile/pakistan/pk_leadingpersonalities_literature.cfm#gap|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/web/20110518210117/http://www.southasianmedia.net/profile/pakistan/pk_leadingpersonalities_literature.cfm#gap|archivedate=18 May 2011|title=South Asian Media Net|publisher=South Asian Free Media Association|accessdate=31 October 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite news|url=http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/world/south_asia/7896943.stm|work=BBC|title=Can Sufi Islam counter the Taleban?|date=24 February 2009|accessdate=20 May 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt; After the [[September 11 attacks|9/11 attacks]] in the [[United States]], the [[Sectarian violence in Pakistan|sectarian violence]] among Muslim denominations has increased with [[Targeted killings in Pakistan|systematic targeted killings]] of both sects, Sunnis and Shias.&lt;ref name=&quot;Nigeria Times&quot;&gt;{{cite news|title=Sunni Leader Killed in Pakistan|url=http://www.punchng.com/news/hardline-sunni-leader-killed-in-pakistan/|accessdate=21 February 2015|agency=Nigeria Times|publisher=Nigeria Times|date=15 February 2015|deadurl=yes|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/20150223031852/http://www.punchng.com/news/hardline-sunni-leader-killed-in-pakistan/|archivedate=23 February 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://timesofindia.indiatimes.com/world/pakistan/2012-bloodiest-year-for-Shias-in-Pakistan/articleshow/18055952.cms|title=2012 bloodiest year for Shias in Pakistan|last=Fatah|first=Sonya|date=17 January 2013|work=[[Times Internet]]|publisher=The Times of India|accessdate=20 September 2014}}&lt;/ref&gt; In 2013, there were country-wide protests by both Shias and Sunnis calling an end to sectarian violence in the country, toughen up the [[Law and order (politics)|law and order]], and urging for [[Shia Sunni relations|Shia-Sunni unity]] in the country.&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite news|url=http://articles.timesofindia.indiatimes.com/2013-01-17/pakistan/36393344_1_shias-bloodiest-year-ali-dayan-hasan|title=Bomb kills four at Pakistan Shiite funeral: police|agency=The Times of India|date=17 January 2013|accessdate=17 January 2013|work=The Times Of India}}&lt;/ref&gt; The [[Ahmadis]] are particularly persecuted, especially since 1974 when they were [[Second Amendment to the Constitution of Pakistan|banned]] from calling themselves Muslims. In 1984, Ahmadiyya places of worship were banned from being called &quot;mosques&quot;.&lt;ref&gt;New Approaches to the Analysis of Jihadism: Online and Offline â€“ Page 38, RÃ¼diger Lohlker â€“ 2012&lt;/ref&gt; {{As of|2012}}, 12% of Pakistani Muslims self-identify as [[non-denominational Muslims]].&lt;ref&gt;[http://www.pewforum.org/2012/08/09/the-worlds-muslims-unity-and-diversity-1-religious-affiliation/#identity Chapter 1: Religious Affiliation] retrieved 4 September 2013&lt;/ref&gt;

Islam to some extent [[Syncretism|syncretized]] with pre-Islamic influences, resulting in a religion with some traditions distinct from those of the [[Arab world]].&lt;ref name=&quot;Ishtiaq Ahmed&quot;&gt;{{cite book|title=Islam Outside the Arab World|publisher=Routledge|isbn=978-0700711246|pages=201â€“222|author=Ishtiaq Ahmed|editor=Ingvar Svanberg, David Westerlund|chapter=South Asia|date=12 August 1999}}&lt;/ref&gt; Two Sufis whose shrines receive much national attention are [[Ali Hajweri]] in [[Lahore]] (c. 12th century)&lt;ref name=&quot;Amer Morgahi&quot;&gt;{{cite book|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=WO-5e-HSdOoC&amp;pg=PA47|title=Producing Islamic Knowledge: Transmission and Dissemination in Western Europe|author=Amer Morgahi|accessdate=30 July 2013|year=2011|publisher=Routledge|isbn=978-0415355926|page=47|editor=Martin van Bruinessen, Stefano Allievi|chapter=An emerging European Islam: The case of the Minhaj ul Quran in the Netherlands}}&lt;/ref&gt; and [[Shahbaz Qalander]] in [[Sehwan]], [[Sindh]] (c. 12th century).&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.dawn.com/news/1136858|title=Sehwan: The undisputed throne of Lal Shahbaz Qalandar|author=Farooq Soomro|work=dawn.com|accessdate=13 January 2016}}&lt;/ref&gt; [[Sufism]], a mystical Islamic tradition, has a long history and a large popular following in Pakistan. Popular Sufi culture is centered on Thursday night gatherings at shrines and annual festivals which feature Sufi music and dance. Contemporary Islamic fundamentalists criticize its popular character, which in their view, does not accurately reflect the teachings and practice of the Prophet and his companions.&lt;ref name=&quot;NYTSufivideo&quot;&gt;{{cite news|title=Sufism Under Attack in Pakistan|url=http://video.nytimes.com/video/2011/01/06/world/asia/1248069532117/sufism-under-attack-in-pakistan.html|accessdate=21 May 2012|newspaper=The New York Times|author=Produced by Charlotte Buchen|format=video}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;NYTAtWarSufi&quot;&gt;{{cite news|title=The Islam That Hard-Liners Hate|url=http://atwar.blogs.nytimes.com/2011/01/06/the-islam-that-hard-liners-hate/|accessdate=21 May 2012|newspaper=The New York Times|date=6 January 2011|author=Huma Imtiaz|author2=Charlotte Buchen|format=blog}}&lt;/ref&gt;

After Islam, [[Hinduism in Pakistan|Hinduism]] and [[Christianity in Pakistan|Christianity]] are the largest religions in Pakistan, with 2,800,000 (1.6%) adherents each in 2005.&lt;ref name=&quot;congress&quot; /&gt;  In 1998 following a census, they were followed by the [[BahÃ¡'Ã­ Faith in Pakistan|BahÃ¡'Ã­ Faith]], which had a following of 30,000, then [[Sikhism in Pakistan|Sikhism]], [[Buddhism in Pakistan|Buddhism]] and [[Zoroastrianism]], each back then claiming 20,000 adherents,&lt;ref name=&quot;State&quot;&gt;{{cite web|title=Pakistan&amp;nbsp;â€” International Religious Freedom Report 2008 |url=http://www.state.gov/g/drl/rls/irf/2008/108505.htm |accessdate=28 August 2010 |quote=''The majority of Muslims in the country are Sunni, with a Shi'a minority ranging between 10 to 20 percent.'' |year=2008|work=[[United States Department of State]]}}&lt;/ref&gt; and a very small [[Jainism in Pakistan|community of Jains]]. There is a [[Roman Catholicism in Pakistan|Roman Catholic]] community in [[Karachi]] which was established by [[Goa]]n and [[Tamil people|Tamil]] migrants when Karachi's infrastructure was being developed by the [[United Kingdom|British]] during colonial administration between [[World War I]] and [[World War II]]. Influence of [[Irreligion in Pakistan|atheism]] is very little with 1.0% of the population aligned as atheist in 2005.&lt;ref name=&quot;Dawn, Irfan&quot; /&gt; However, the figure rose to 2.0% in 2012 according to [[WIN/GIA|Gallup]].&lt;ref name=&quot;Dawn, Irfan&quot;&gt;{{cite news|last=Husain|first=Irfan|title=Faith in decline|url=http://dawn.com/2012/08/27/faith-in-decline/|accessdate=16 December 2012|newspaper=Dawn, Irfan|date=27 Aug 2012|quote=Interestingly, and somewhat intriguingly, 2 per cent of the Pakistanis surveyed see themselves as atheists, up from 1pc in 2005.|deadurl=yes|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/20121204113125/http://dawn.com/2012/08/27/faith-in-decline/|archivedate=4 December 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt;

==Culture and society==
{{main article|British heritage of Pakistan|Culture of Pakistan|Public holidays in Pakistan}}
[[File:Culture of Pakistan (cropped).jpg|thumb|right|[[Truck art in Pakistan]] is a unique feature of Pakistani culture.]]
&lt;!-- Urdu#Heritage has a link to this section (in case of name change) --&gt;
[[Pakistani society|Civil society]] in Pakistan is largely hierarchical, emphasising [[Etiquette in Pakistan|local cultural etiquettes]] and traditional Islamic values that govern personal and political life. The basic family unit is the [[extended family]],&lt;ref name=&quot;nuclear&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.kwintessential.co.uk/resources/global-etiquette/pakistan.html|title=Pakistan- Language, Religion, Culture, Customs and Etiquette|publisher=Kwint Essential|accessdate=17 March 2009}}&lt;/ref&gt; although there has been a growing trend towards [[Nuclear family|nuclear families]] for socio-economic reasons.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite journal|author=Anwar Alam|title=Factors and Consequences of Nuclearization of Family at Hayatabad Phase-II, Peshawar|journal=Sarhad J. Agric.|year=2008|volume=24|publisher=Department of Sociology and Anthropology, University of Peshawar|url=http://www.aup.edu.pk/sj_pdf/FACTORS%20AND%20CONSEQUENCES%20OF%20NUCLEARIZATION.pdf|accessdate=21 April 2012|issue=3}}&lt;/ref&gt; The traditional dress for both men and women is the ''[[Shalwar Kameez]]''; [[trouser]]s, [[Jeans]], and shirts are also popular among men.&lt;ref name=&quot;taxila&quot; /&gt; The [[middle class]] has increased to around 35&amp;nbsp;million and the upper and upper-middle classes to around 17&amp;nbsp;million in recent decades, and power is shifting from rural landowners to the urbanised elites.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=The rise of Mehran man|url=http://archives.dawn.com/archives/19124|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/web/20101125011513/http://news.dawn.com/wps/wcm/connect/dawn-content-library/dawn/the-newspaper/columnists/irfan-husain-the-rise-of-mehran-man-740|archivedate=25 November 2010|work=Dawn|location=Pakistan News|author=Irfan Husain|date=17 April 2010|accessdate=25 July 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt;  Pakistani festivals such as [[Eid ul-Fitr|''Eid-ul-Fitr'']], [[Eid al-Adha|''Eid-ul-Azha'']], [[Ramadan|''Ramazan'']], [[Christmas]], [[Easter]], [[Holi]], and [[Diwali]] are mostly religious in origin.&lt;ref name=&quot;nuclear&quot; /&gt; Increasing globalisation has resulted in Pakistan ranking 56th on the [[A.T. Kearney]]/FP [[Globalization Index]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=A.T. Kearney/Foreign Policy Magazine Globalization Index 2006|url=http://www.atkearney.com/images/global/pdf/Globalization-Index_FP_Nov-Dec-06_S.pdf|publisher=A.T. Kearney|format=PDF|date=Novâ€“Dec 2006|page=4|accessdate=1 January 2012}}{{dead link|date=May 2016|bot=medic}}{{cbignore|bot=medic}}&lt;/ref&gt;

===Clothing, arts, and fashion===
{{main article|Suit (clothing)#suits|l1=Suits in Pakistan|Pakistani clothing|Sherwani|Jinnah cap|Peshawari chappal}}
The [[Shalwar kameez|''Shalwar Kameez'']] is the [[national dress]] of Pakistan and is worn by both men and women in all [[Four Provinces (Pakistan)|four provinces]]: [[Punjab, Pakistan|Punjab]], [[Sindh]], [[Balochistan, Pakistan|Balochistan]], and [[Khyber Pakhtoonkhwa|Khyber-Pakhtunkhwa]] as well as in [[Tribal belt of Pakistan|FATA]] and [[Azad Kashmir]]. Each province has its own style of wearing the ''Shalwar Kameez''. Pakistanis wear clothes range from exquisite colors and designs to the type of fabric ([[silk]], [[chiffon (fabric)|chiffon]], [[cotton]], etc).&lt;ref name=&quot;Books.google.com&quot;&gt;{{cite book|url=https://books.google.com/?id=7li2jpQgYvAC&amp;pg=PA382&amp;dq=national+dress+of+pakistan|title=Unquiet Pasts: Risk Society, Lived Cultural Heritage, Re-Designing Reflexivity â€“ Stephanie Koerner, Ian Russell â€“ Google Books|publisher=Books.google.com|date=16 August 2010|accessdate=14 June 2012|isbn=9780754675488}}&lt;/ref&gt; Besides the national dress, the domestically [[tailor]]ed [[Suit (clothing)|suits]] and [[necktie]]s are often and usually worn by men in the country, and it is customary in offices, schools, and other necessary places and popular gatherings.&lt;ref name=&quot;Books.google.com&quot; /&gt;

The [[Pakistani fashion|fashion industry]] has flourished well in the changing environment of the fashion world. Since Pakistan came into being, its fashion has historically evolved from different phases and made its unique identity apart from Indian fashion and culture. At this time, Pakistani fashion is a combination of traditional and modern dresses and it has become the cultural identification of Pakistan. Despite all modern trends, the regional and traditional dresses have developed their own significance as a symbol of native tradition. This regional fashion is not static but evolving into more modern and pure forms. The Pakistan Fashion Design Council based in [[Lahore]] organizes ''Fashion Week'' and Fashion Pakistan based in [[Karachi]] organizes fashion shows in that city. Pakistan's first fashion week was held in November 2009.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|author=Michele Langevine Leiby|url=http://www.washingtonpost.com/world/asia_pacific/in-pakistan-fashion-weeks-thrive-beyond-the-style-capitals-of-the-world/2012/04/24/gIQAt3qcgT_story.html|title=In Pakistan, fashion weeks thrive beyond the style capitals of the world|publisher=Washingtonpost.com|date=25 April 2012|accessdate=20 April 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt;

===Role of women in Pakistani society===
{{main article|Women in Pakistan|Role of women in Pakistani media|Women in the Pakistan Armed Forces|Protection of Women Against Violence Bill 2015}}
{{multiple image
 |align=right
 |image1=Benazir Bhutto.jpg
 |width1=100
 |caption1= [[Benazir Bhutto]] was the first woman elected to lead a Muslim state, and is the only one to be elected twice
 |alt1=
 |width2=100
 |image2=Malala_Yousafzai_at_Girl_Summit_2014.jpg
 |caption2=[[Malala Yousafzai]] is the youngest-ever [[Nobel Prize laureate]].
 |alt2=
}}
The [[Pakistani cultural influence|social status]] of [[women in Pakistan]] varies and considerably depends on the [[social class]], [[Nature versus nurture|upbringings, and regional divide]] due to uneven [[socioeconomic development]] and the [[Socioeconomic impact of female education|impact]] of social formations on women's lives in the country.&lt;ref name=&quot;adb_wom_pak&quot; /&gt; Pakistan has had a long history of feminist activism since its birth.&lt;ref name=&quot;adb_wom_pak&quot; /&gt; Since 1947, the [[All Pakistan Women's Association|APWA]] and [[Aurat Foundation]]â€“ the influential feminist organizationsâ€” have played strong roles in inculcating awareness about women's rights in the country.&lt;ref name=&quot;adb_wom_pak&quot; /&gt; Personalities such as [[Sheila Irene Pant|Begum Rana'a]], [[Benazir Bhutto]], [[Malala Yousafzai]] and [[Kalsoom Nawaz Sharif|Kalsoom Nawaz]] have been influential in Pakistan's feminist culture.&lt;ref name=&quot;adb_wom_pak&quot; /&gt; The status of women, overall, has improved due to enhanced religious and educational knowledge. However, with regard to the global average, the situation is quite alarming. In 2014, the [[World Economic Forum]] ranked Pakistan as the second worst country in the world in [[gender equality]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://timesofindia.indiatimes.com/world/pakistan/Pakistan-second-worst-country-in-gender-equality/articleshow/44961322.cms|title=Pakistan second-worst country in gender equality|work=The Times of India}}&lt;/ref&gt;

The relationship of women with the opposite gender is culturally that of [[gender subordination]]. There are certain assumed and assigned roles of women that are related to domestic chores compared with men who are the breadwinners and professionals of the family. Contrastingly, in urban areas of the country, more and more women are assuming professional roles and are contributing to family economics but the ratio of these women compared with those in traditional roles is way less. Most favoured occupations for females accepted by society are that of [[Teaching]] and [[Tutoring]].&lt;ref name=&quot;adb_wom_pak&quot;&gt;{{cite book|url=http://www.adb.org/sites/default/files/institutional-document/32562/files/women-pakistan.pdf|title=Women in Pakistan: Country Briefing Paper|author=Mariam S. Pal|year=2000|publisher=Asian Development Bank|isbn=971-561-297-0|deadurl=yes|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/web/20141105234435/http://www.adb.org/sites/default/files/institutional-document/32562/files/women-pakistan.pdf |archivedate=5 November 2014 }}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;whistory&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://womenshistory.about.com/library/ency/blwh_pakistan_women.htm|title=Pakistan: Status of Women &amp; the Women's Movement|publisher=Womenshistory.about.com|date=28 July 2001|accessdate=2012-01-24}}&lt;/ref&gt; Due to heightened awareness among people, educational opportunities for Pakistani women have increased over the years.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.pakcitizen.com/educational-opportunities-for-women-in-pakistan/|title=Women Education in Pakistan|publisher=Pakcitizen.com|date=17 December 2011|accessdate=2012-01-24}}&lt;/ref&gt; On 24 February 2016, the elected assembly of Pakistan's Punjab province passed a new law called &quot;Punjab Protection of Women Against Violence Bill 2015 &quot; which provides women with protection against a multitude of crimes including: cyber crime, domestic violence, emotional, economic and psychological abuse.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.dawn.com/news/1241751|title=PA approves bill for protection of women against violence|author=Intikhab Hanif|work=dawn.com|accessdate=16 March 2016}}&lt;/ref&gt;

===Media and entertainment===
{{main article|Cinema of Pakistan|Media of Pakistan|Music of Pakistan|History of Pakistani pop music|Theatre of Pakistan|Pakistani dramas}}
[[File:Sharmeen Obaid Chinoy World Economic Forum 2013.jpg|thumb|180px|right|[[Sharmeen Obaid-Chinoy]] have won [[Academy Awards|Oscars]] in 2012 and 2016 for her documentaries]]
The private [[Newspapers in Pakistan|print media]], state-owned [[Pakistan Television Corporation]] (PTV) and [[Pakistan Broadcasting Corporation]] (PBC) for [[Radio Pakistan|radio]] were the dominant media outlets until the 21st century. Pakistan now has a large network of domestic [[Privatization in Pakistan|private]] [[24 hour news cycle|24-hour]] [[News channels in Pakistan|news media]] and [[Television in Pakistan|television channels]].&lt;ref name=&quot;media&quot;&gt;{{Cite news|url=http://www.i-m-s.dk/files/publications/1491%20Pakistan.final.web.pdf|title=Media in Pakistan|publisher=International Media Support|format=PDF|pages=14â€“16, 21|accessdate=17 December 2011|date=July 2009|deadurl=yes|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/20091229205633/http://www.i-m-s.dk:80/files/publications/1491%20Pakistan.final.web.pdf|archivedate=29 December 2009}}&lt;/ref&gt; According to a 2016 report by the [[Reporters Without Borders]] ranked Pakistan in 147th on the [[Press Freedom Index]], while terming the Pakistani media &quot;among the freest in Asia when it comes to covering the squabbling among politicians.&quot;&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite web|url=http://tribune.com.pk/story/1089039/press-freedom-index-pakistani-media-targeted-on-all-sides-says-report/|title=Pakistani media targeted on all sides, says report â€“ The Express Tribune|website=The Express Tribune|language=en-US|access-date=2016-04-23}}&lt;/ref&gt; [[BBC]] term's Pakistani media as &quot;among the most outspoken in South Asia.&quot;&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite web|url=http://www.bbc.com/news/world-south-asia-12965785|title=Pakistan profile â€“ Media â€“ BBC News|website=BBC News|language=en-GB|access-date=2016-04-23}}&lt;/ref&gt;

The [[Lollywood]], an Urdu, Punjabi and Pashto film industry is based in [[Karachi]], [[Lahore]], and [[Peshawar]]. While [[Bollywood]] films were banned from public cinemas from 1965 until 2008, they have remained important in popular culture.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://sg.news.yahoo.com/bollywood-films-may-banned-pakistan-094000464.html|title=Bollywood films may be banned in Pakistan|author=Naseem Randhava|publisher=[[Yahoo! News]]|date=11 October 2011|accessdate=31 October 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;bbcentertainment&quot;&gt;{{Cite news|url=http://news.bbc.co.uk/2/hi/entertainment/4639216.stm|title=Pakistan to show Bollywood film|publisher=BBC News|accessdate=13 February 2008|date=23 January 2006}}&lt;/ref&gt; Contrary to the ailing film industry, the Urdu [[Pakistani dramas|televised dramas]] and theatrical performances are popular, as many [[Television in Pakistan|entertainment media]] air the series regularly.&lt;ref name=&quot;Brown Girl&quot;&gt;{{cite web|last1=Shaikh|first1=Naila|title=The Evolving World of Pakistani Dramas Builds Stronger Relations With India|url=http://www.browngirlmagazine.com/2015/01/evolving-world-pakistani-dramas-builds-stronger-relations-india/|website=http://www.browngirlmagazine.com/|publisher=Brown Girl|accessdate=25 May 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt; [[Pakistani dramas|Urdu dramas]] dominate the [[Television in Pakistan|TV entertainment industry]], and have debuted critically acclaimed [[miniseries]], and have featured popular actors and actresses since the 1990s.&lt;ref name=&quot;Daily Times 2014&quot;&gt;{{cite news|last1=Daily Times Monitor|first1=Editorial|title=Pakistani dramas contribute to the evolution of Indian television|url=http://www.dailytimes.com.pk/entertainment/25-Dec-2014/pakistani-dramas-contribute-to-the-evolution-of-indian-television|accessdate=25 May 2015|agency=Daily Times, Pakistan|publisher=Daily Times 2014|date=25 December 2014}}&lt;/ref&gt; In the 1960sâ€“1970s, [[Pakistani pop music|pop music]] and [[Disco Deewane|disco]] (1970s) dominated the country's music industry. In the 1980sâ€“1990s, [[new wave of British heavy metal|British influenced]] [[Pakistani rock|rock music]] appeared and jolted the country's entertainment industry.&lt;ref name=&quot;Dawn News, Nadeem F. Paracha&quot;&gt;{{cite news|last=Nadeem F. Paracha|title=Times of the Vital Sign|url=http://dawn.com/2013/03/28/times-of-the-signs/|accessdate=3 April 2013|newspaper=Dawn News, Nadeem F. Paracha|date=28 March 2013|deadurl=yes|archiveurl=https://web.archive.org/20130402133212/http://dawn.com/2013/03/28/times-of-the-signs/|archivedate=2 April 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt; In the 2000s, [[heavy metal music]] gained popular and critical acclaim.&lt;ref name=&quot;CNN Pakistan&quot;&gt;{{cite news|last=Reza Sayah|title=Underground musicians aim to change Pakistan's image|url=http://edition.cnn.com/2009/WORLD/asiapcf/04/12/pakistan.music/|accessdate=5 April 2013|newspaper=CNN Pakistan|date=12 April 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt;

Pakistani music ranges from diverse provincial folk music and traditional styles such as [[Qawwali]] and [[Ghazal|Ghazal Gayaki]] to modern forms fusing traditional and western music.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|author=[[Adam Nayyar]]|title=Origin and History of the Qawwali|format=PDF|year=1988|url=http://webcitation.org/query.php?url=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.utoronto.ca%2Fjkcourses%2Fmus200%2FNayyar_Qawwali.pdf|publisher=University of Toronto|page=1|accessdate=20 January 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|title=The stilled voice|url=http://www.hindu.com/fline/fl1418/14181230.htm|work=[[Frontline (magazine)|Frontline]]|accessdate=30 June 2011|author=Amit Baruah, R. Padmanabhan|date=6 September 1997|location=Chennai, India}}&lt;/ref&gt; Pakistan has many famous folk singers. The arrival of Afghan refugees in the western provinces has stimulated interest in Pashto music, although there has been intolerance of it in some places.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|author=Owais Tohid|title=Music soothes extremism along troubled Afghan border|url=http://www.csmonitor.com/2005/0607/p07s01-wosc.html|accessdate=20 January 2012|newspaper=The Christian Science Monitor.|date=7 June 2005}}&lt;/ref&gt; Pakistan has some of the world's modern vibrant and open media.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|url=http://blogs.independent.co.uk/2010/11/11/who-has-the-most-free-media-india-or-pakistan/|title=Who has the &quot;most free&quot; media â€“ India or Pakistan?|publisher=Blogs.independent.co.uk|date=11 November 2010|accessdate=15 October 2013|location=London|first=Andrew|last=Buncombe}}&lt;/ref&gt; Pakistani media has also played a vital role in exposing [[corruption]].&lt;ref name=&quot;i-m-s.dk&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.i-m-s.dk/files/publications/1491%20Pakistan.final.web.pdf|format=PDF|title={{as written|Between radicalisation and democratisation in an unfolding conflict: Media in Pakistan}}|publisher=International Media Support|date=July 2009|accessdate=19 December 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt;

===Urbanisation===
{{main article|Urbanisation in Pakistan}}
[[File:Empress Market at Sunset.jpg|thumb|left|Long exposure of [[Empress Market]] in [[Karachi]].]]
Since [[Independence of Pakistan|achieving independence]] as a result of the [[Partition of India|partition]] of [[India]], the [[Urbanisation in Pakistan|urbanization]] has exponentially increased and has several different causes for it.&lt;ref name=&quot;The Urban Frontierâ€”Karachi&quot; /&gt; Majority of [[Southern Pakistan|southern side population]] resides along the [[Indus River]], with [[Karachi]] being its most populous commercial city.&lt;ref name=&quot;The Urban Frontierâ€”Karachi&quot; /&gt; On the [[Eastern Pakistan|east]], [[Khyber Pakhtunkhwa|west]], and [[Northern Pakistan|northern skirts]], the most of the population lives in an arc formed by the cities of [[Lahore]], [[Faisalabad]], [[Rawalpindi]], [[Islamabad]], [[Sargodha]], [[Gujranwala]], [[Sialkot]], [[Gujrat city|Gujrat]], [[Jhelum]], [[Sheikhupura]], [[Nowshera, Khyber-Pakhtunkhwa|Nowshera]], [[Mardan]] and [[Peshawar]]. During [[Fiscal year|1990â€“2008]], the city dwellers made up 36.0% of Pakistan's population, making it the most urbanised nation in [[South Asia]]. Furthermore, more than 50% of Pakistanis live in towns of 5,000 people or more.&lt;ref name=&quot;Jason Burke&quot; /&gt; [[Immigration to Pakistan|Immigration]], both from within and outside the country, is regarded as one of the main factors that has contributed to urbanisation in Pakistan. One analysis of the [[1998 Pakistan Census|national census]] held in 1998 highlighted the significance of the [[Partition of India]] in the 1940s in the context of understanding urban change in Pakistan.&lt;ref name=&quot;Clark&quot;&gt;{{cite book|last=Clark|first=David|title=The Elgar Companion to Development Studies|year=2006|publisher=Edward Elgar Publishing|isbn=978-1843764755|page=668}}&lt;/ref&gt;

During the independence period, Muslim [[Muhajir people|Muhajirs]] from [[India]] migrated in large numbers and shifted their domicile to Pakistan, especially to the port city of [[Karachi]], which is today the largest metropolis in Pakistan.&lt;ref name=&quot;Clark&quot; /&gt;
[[Immigration to Pakistan|Migration from other countries]], mainly those in the neighbourhood, has further catalysed the process of urbanisation in Pakistani cities. Of particular interest is migration that occurred in the aftermath of the independence of [[Bangladesh]] in 1971,&lt;ref name=&quot;Clark&quot; /&gt; in the form of stranded [[Stranded Pakistanis|Biharis]] who were relocated to Pakistan. Smaller numbers of [[Bengalis in Pakistan|Bengalis]] and [[Burmese people in Pakistan|Burmese]] immigrants followed suit much later. The [[Soviet war in Afghanistan|conflict]] in [[Afghanistan]] also forced millions of [[Afghans in Pakistan|Afghan refugees]] into Pakistan, particularly in the northwestern regions. Inevitably, the rapid urbanisation caused by these large population movements has also brought new political and socio-economic complexities.&lt;ref name=&quot;Clark&quot; /&gt; In addition to immigration, economic events such as the green revolution and political developments, among a host of other factors, are also important causes of urbanisation.&lt;ref name=&quot;Clark&quot; /&gt;

===Diaspora===
{{main article|Cinema of Pakistan|Overseas Pakistani}}{{Multiple image
| align = right
| direction = horizontal
| width = 97
| image1 = Sadiq Khan.jpg
| caption1 = [[Sadiq Khan]] is the [[Mayor of London]] and also served as the UK's [[Minister of State for Transport]]. He is a son of Pakistani expats.
| image2 = MAVALVALA-3.JPG
| caption2 = [[Nergis Mavalvala]] is a [[Pakistani American]] [[astrophysicist]] who played a key role in the [[first observation of gravitational waves]].
}}According to the UN Department of Economic and Social Affairs, Pakistan has the sixth largest diaspora in the world.&lt;ref&gt;{{Cite web|url=http://www.tribuneindia.com/news/nation/india-has-largest-diaspora-population-in-world-un/183731.html|title=India has largest diaspora population in world: UN|last=Service|first=Tribune News|website=http://www.tribuneindia.com/news/nation/india-has-largest-diaspora-population-in-world-un/183731.html|access-date=2016-03-03}}&lt;/ref&gt; [[Statistics]] approximated by the [[Government of Pakistan|Pakistan government]], there are around 7 million [[Pakistan people|Pakistanis]] residing abroad with vast majority living in the [[Middle East]], [[Europe]] and [[North America]].&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|url=http://archives.dawn.com/archives/142435|title=Pride and the Pakistani Diaspora|publisher=Archives.dawn.com|date=14 February 2009|accessdate=15 October 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt; Pakistan ranks 10th in the world for remittances sent home in 2012 at $13 billion.&lt;ref name=&quot;overseaspakistanis1&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.overseaspakistanis.net/category/op-news/page/2/|title=OP News Discussions Archives|publisher=Overseaspakistanis.net|accessdate=15 October 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;worldbank1&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://siteresources.worldbank.org/INTPROSPECTS/Resources/334934-1199807908806/Top10.pdf|format=PDF|title=Migration and Remittances: Top Countries|publisher=Siteresources.worldbank.org|year=2010|accessdate=19 December 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt;

The term ''[[Overseas Pakistani]]'' is officially recognized by the [[Government of Pakistan]]; the [[Ministry of Overseas Pakistanis]] was established in 2008 to exclusively deal with all the matters and affairs of the overseas Pakistanis such as attending to their needs and problems, intending schemes and projects for their welfare and working for resolution of their problems and issues. [[Overseas Pakistani]] workers are the second largest source of Foreign Exchange Remittances to Pakistan after exports and over the last several years, the foreign exchange remittances have maintained a steady rising trend, with a recorded increase of 150% from US$6 billion in 2009 to estimated US$15 billion during 2015.&lt;ref name=&quot;remit&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.dawn.com/news/1176411|title=Pakistan's remittances|author=Sakib Sherani|work=dawn.com|accessdate=17 December 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;

In 2009â€“10, Pakistanis sent home US$9.4 billion, the eleventh-largest total remittance in the world.&lt;ref name=&quot;worldbank1&quot; /&gt; By 2012, Pakistan increased its ranking to tenth in the world for remittances with a total sum of US$13 billion.&lt;ref name=&quot;overseaspakistanis1&quot; /&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;worldbank1&quot; /&gt; The Overseas Pakistani Division (OPD) was created in September 2004 within the [[Ministry of Labour (Pakistan)|Ministry of Labour]] (MoL), and has since recognized the importance of overseas Pakistanis and their contribution to the [[Economy of Pakistan|nation's economy]]. Together with Community Welfare Attaches (CWAs) and the Overseas Pakistanis Foundation (OPF), the OPD is improving the welfare of Pakistanis who reside abroad. The division aims to provide better services through improved facilities at [[airports]], and suitable schemes for housing, [[education]] and [[health care]]â€”its largest effort is the facilitation of the rehabilitation of returning overseas Pakistanis.

===Literature and philosophy===
[[File:Iqbal.jpg|thumb|right|140px|alt=Muhammad Iqbal|[[Muhammad Iqbal]], Pakistan's national poet who conceived the [[Conception of Pakistan|''idea'']] of Pakistan.]]
{{Main article|Literature of Pakistan|Urdu poetry|Pakistani philosophy}}

Pakistan has literature in [[Urdu literature|Urdu]], [[Sindhi literature|Sindhi]], [[Punjabi literature|Punjabi]], [[Pashto literature and poetry|Pashto]], [[Balochi Academy|Baluchi]], [[Persian literature|Persian]], [[Pakistani English literature|English]] and many other languages.&lt;ref name=&quot;MohanramRajan1996&quot;&gt;{{cite book|author=Alamgir Hashmi|editor=Radhika Mohanram|others=Gita Rajan|title=English postcoloniality: literatures from around the world|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=H_uwA78YZDoC&amp;pg=PA107|accessdate=2 January 2012|year=1996|publisher=Greenwood Publishing Group|isbn=978-0-313-28854-8|pages=107â€“112}}&lt;/ref&gt; The [[Pakistan Academy of Letters]] is a large literary community that promotes literature and poetry in Pakistan and abroad.&lt;ref&gt;Official website in English [http://pal.gov.pk/home/ Pakistan Academy of Letters] {{wayback|url=http://pal.gov.pk/home/|date=20130306135947|df=y}}&lt;/ref&gt; The [[National Library of Pakistan|National Library]] publishes and promotes literature in the country. Before the 19th century, literature consisted mainly of [[lyric poetry|lyric]] and [[Sufi poetry|religious poetry]], mystical and [[Pop culture#Folklore|folkloric]] works. During the colonial age, the native literary figures were influenced by western [[literary realism]] and took up increasingly varied topics and narrative forms. Prose fiction is now very popular.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|author=Gilani Kamran|title=Pakistani Literature â€“ Evolution &amp; trends|url=http://www.the-south-asian.com/Jan2002/Pakistani-Literature3-the-Novel.htm|publisher=the-south-asian magzine|date=January 2002|accessdate=24 December 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;granta&quot;&gt;{{cite web|author=Huma Imtiaz|title=Granta: The global reach of Pakistani literature|url=http://tribune.com.pk/story/53272/granta-the-global-reach-of-pakistani-literature/|work=The Express Tribune|date=26 September 2010|accessdate=24 December 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt;

[[File:Tomb of Shah Rukn-e-Alam, Multan - Front Courtyard.jpg|thumb|150px|left|[[Tomb of Shah Rukn-e-Alam]] is part of Pakistan's [[sufi]] heritage.]]
The [[List of national poets|national poet]] of Pakistan, [[Muhammad Iqbal]], wrote poetry in Urdu and Persian. He was a strong proponent of the political and spiritual revival of Islamic civilisation and encouraged Muslims binding all over the world to bring about successful revolution.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|author=Annemarie Schimmel|title=Iqbal, Muhammad|url=http://www.iranicaonline.org/articles/iqbal-muhammad|publisher=EncyclopÃ¦dia Iranica|date=15 December 2004|accessdate=1 January 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|author=Nadeem Shafique|title=Global Apprecaition of Allama Iqbal|url=http://www.bzu.edu.pk/jrlanguages/Vol-1%202001/Nadeem%20Shafiq-3.pdf|work=Bahauddin Zakariya University|publisher=Journal of Research, Faculty of Languages and Islamic Studies|pages=47â€“49|accessdate=1 January 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;allamaiqbal.com&quot;&gt;{{cite web |author=Iqbal Academy|date=26 May 2006|url=http://www.allamaiqbal.com/person/biography/biotxtread.html|title=Allama Iqbal â€“ Biography|format=PHP|accessdate=7 January 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt; Well-known representatives of contemporary Pakistani Urdu literature include [[Faiz Ahmed Faiz]]. [[Sadequain]] is known for his calligraphy and paintings.&lt;ref name=&quot;granta&quot; /&gt; Sufi poets [[Shah Abdul Latif]], [[Bulleh Shah]], [[Mian Muhammad Bakhsh]] and [[Khawaja Farid]] are very popular in Pakistan.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|author=Muhammad Zahid Rifat|title=Paying tributes to popular Sufi poets|url=http://www.highbeam.com/doc/1G1-268638505.html|work=highbeam.com|publisher=The Nation|date=3 October 2011|accessdate=25 December 2011}}&lt;/ref&gt; [[Mirza Kalich Beg]] has been termed the father of modern Sindhi prose.&lt;ref name=&quot;Karnani2003&quot;&gt;{{cite book|title=L.H. Ajwani|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=dL5owdAV5TcC&amp;pg=PA50|publisher=Sahitya Akademi|accessdate=25 December 2011|author=Chetan Karnani|year=2003|isbn=978-81-260-1664-8|pages=50â€“}}&lt;/ref&gt;

Historically, philosophical development in the country was dominated by [[Muhammad Iqbal]], [[Syed Ahmad Khan|Sir Syed]], [[Muhammad Asad]], [[Abu Ala' Mawdudi|Maududi]], and [[Mohammad Ali Jouhar|Ali Johar]].&lt;ref&gt;Javed, Kazi. Philosophical Domain of Pakistan (Pakistan Main Phalsapiana Rojhanat) (in Urdu). Karachi: Karachi University Press, 1999.&lt;/ref&gt; Ideas from [[British philosophy|British]] and [[American philosophy]] greatly shaped [[Pakistani philosophy|philosophical development]] in Pakistan. [[Analytical psychology|Analysts]] such as [[M.M. Sharif]] and [[Syed Zafarul Hasan|Zafar Hassan]] established the first major Pakistani philosophical movement in 1947.&lt;ref name=&quot;Work published by Pakistan Philosophical Congress&quot;&gt;{{cite web|last=et. al.|first=Richard V. DeSemet [[Sitara-e-Jurat|SeJ]]|title=Philosophical Activities in Pakistan:1947â€“1961|url=http://www.crvp.org/book/Series02/IIA-3/appendix.htm|work=Work published by Pakistan Philosophical Congress|publisher=Work published by Pakistan Philosophical Congress|accessdate=25 November 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt; After the [[Indo-Pakistani war of 1971|1971 war]], philosophers such as [[Jalaludin Abdur Rahim]], [[Sobho Gianchandani|Gianchandani]], and [[Malik Meraj Khalid|Malik Khalid]] incorporated [[Marxism]] into Pakistan's [[Pakistani philosophy|philosophical development]].&lt;ref name=&quot;Council for Research in Values and Philosophy&quot; /&gt; Influential work by [[Manzoor Ahmad]], [[Jon Elia]], [[Hasan Askari Rizvi]], and [[Abdul Khaliq (philosopher)|Abdul Khaliq]] brought mainstream [[Social philosophy|social]], [[Political thought|political]], and [[analytical philosophy]] to the fore of Pakistani philosophical academia.&lt;ref name=&quot;Council for Research in Values and Philosophy&quot;&gt;{{cite book|last1=Ahmad|first1=ed. by Naeem|title=Philosophy in Pakistan|date=1998|publisher=Council for Research in Values and Philosophy|location=Washington, DC|isbn=1-56518-108-5}}&lt;/ref&gt; Global works by [[Noam Chomsky]] have influenced [[Pakistani philosophy|philosophical ideas]] in various fields of social and political philosophy.&lt;ref name=&quot;Dawn news election cells&quot;&gt;{{cite news|last1=Mallick|first1=Ayyaz|title=Exclusive interview with Noam Chomsky on Pakistan elections|url=http://www.dawn.com/news/812481/exclusive-interview-with-noam-chomsky-on-pakistan-elections|accessdate=21 February 2015|agency=Dawn news election cells|publisher=Dawn news election cells|date=7 May 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;PTV archives&quot;&gt;{{cite web|last1=Hoodbhoy|first1=Pervez|title=Noam Chomsky interviewed by Pervez Hoodbhoy|url=http://www.chomsky.info/interviews/20011127.htm|website=http://www.chomsky.info/|publisher=PTV archives|accessdate=21 February 2015}}&lt;/ref&gt;

===Architecture===
{{Main article|Pakistani architecture|Hindu and Buddhist architectural heritage of Pakistan}}
[[File:Mohatta_Palace_Renovated.JPG|thumb|300px|left|[[Mohatta Palace]], Karachi was built by Shivratan Chandraratan Mohatta, a Hindu Marwari businessman from modern day Rajasthan in India, in 1927, as his summer home.]]
[[File:Minar-E-Pakistan in all its' splendour.JPG|thumb|150px|right|[[Minar-e-Pakistan]] is a public monument marking Pakistan's independence movement.]]
Pakistani architecture has four recognised periods: [[History of Pakistan#Early history|pre-Islamic]], [[History of Pakistan#Muslim period|Islamic]], [[History of Pakistan#Colonial era|colonial]], and [[History of Pakistan#Independence|post-colonial]]. With the beginning of the [[Indus civilization|Indus civilisation]] around the middle of the 3rd millennium BC,&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|author=Vidja Dehejia|title=South Asian Art and Culture|url=http://www.metmuseum.org/toah/hd/sasa/hd_sasa.htm|work=The Metropolitan Museum of Art|accessdate=10 February 2008}}&lt;/ref&gt; an advanced urban culture developed for the first time in the region, with large buildings, some of which survive to this day.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=The Indus Valley And The Genesis Of South Asian Civilization|url=http://history-world.org/indus_valley.htm|work=History World International|accessdate=6 February 2008}}&lt;/ref&gt; [[Mohenjo-daro|Mohenjo Daro]], Harappa and [[Kot Diji]] are among the pre-Islamic settlements that are now tourist attractions.&lt;ref name=&quot;mountains&quot; /&gt; The rise of [[Buddhism]] and the [[Greeks|Greek]] influence led to the development of the Greco-Buddhist style,&lt;ref&gt;Sachindra Kumar Maity. [https://books.google.com/books?id=x8Iz5C5auqEC&amp;pg=PA46&amp;dq=greco-buddhist+style+greek+and+indian&amp;hl=nl&amp;sa=X&amp;ved=0CDAQ6AEwAmoVChMIuNK5n7GLxgIVaWvbCh1H9gDD#v=onepage&amp;q=greco-buddhist%20style%20greek%20and%20indian&amp;f=false ''Cultural Heritage of Ancient India''] Abhinav Publications, 1983 ISBN 039102809X p 46&lt;/ref&gt; starting from the 1st century AD.  The high point of this era was reached at the peak of the [[Gandhara#Art|Gandhara style]]. An example of Buddhist architecture is the ruins of the Buddhist monastery [[Takht Bhai|Takht-i-Bahi]] in Khyber Pakhtunkhwa.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite web|title=UNESCO Advisory Body Evaluation of Takht Bhai|publisher=International Council on Monuments and Sites|pages=1â€“2|url=http://whc.unesco.org/archive/advisory_body_evaluation/140.pdf|format=PDF|date=29 December 1979|accessdate=25 July 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt;
[[File:Lahore Fort.jpg|thumb|left|150px|The [[Lahore Fort]], a landmark built during the Mughal era, is a [[UNESCO World Heritage Site]].]]
The arrival of Islam in today's Pakistan meant a sudden end of Buddhist architecture in the area and a smooth transition to the predominantly pictureless [[Islamic architecture]]. The most important [[Indo-Islamic architecture|Indo-Islamic]]-style building still standing is the [[Rukn-e-Alam|tomb of the Shah Rukn-i-Alam]] in Multan. During the Mughal era, design elements of Persian-Islamic architecture were fused with and often produced playful forms of Hindustani art. Lahore, occasional residence of Mughal rulers, exhibits many important buildings from the empire. Most prominent among them are the [[Badshahi mosque]], the [[Lahore Fort|fortress of Lahore]] with the famous [[Alamgiri Gate]], the colourful, the [[Mughal architecture|Mughal]]-style [[Wazir Khan Mosque]],&lt;ref&gt;Simon Ross Valentine. [https://books.google.com/books?id=MdRth02Q6nAC&amp;pg=PA63&amp;dq=wazir+khan+mosque+mughal+architecture&amp;hl=nl&amp;sa=X&amp;ved=0CGEQ6AEwCWoVChMIpO7m7LGLxgIVxbLbCh0RyAAL#v=onepage&amp;q=wazir%20khan%20mosque%20mughal%20architecture&amp;f=false 'Islam and the Ahmadiyya Jama'at: History, Belief, Practice] Hurst Publishers, 2008 ISBN 1850659168 p 63&lt;/ref&gt; the [[Shalimar Gardens (Lahore)|Shalimar Gardens]] in Lahore and the [[Shahjahan Mosque]] in [[Thatta]]. In the British colonial period, predominantly functional buildings of the Indo-European representative style developed from a mixture of European and Indian-Islamic components. Post-colonial national identity is expressed in modern structures like the [[Faisal Mosque]], the [[Minar-e-Pakistan]] and the [[Mazar-e-Quaid]].&lt;ref name=&quot;=Concept Media Pte Ltd&quot;&gt;{{cite book|author=Kamil Khan Mumtaz|title=Architecture in Pakistan|year=1985|publisher=Concept Media Pte Ltd|isbn=9971-84-141-X|pages=32,51,160}}&lt;/ref&gt; Several of the architectural infrastructure has been influenced from the [[Architecture of the United Kingdom|British design]], and such [[Architecture of the United Kingdom|architectural designs]] can be found in [[Lahore]], [[Peshawar]], and [[Karachi]].&lt;ref name=&quot;=Concept Media Pte Ltd&quot; /&gt;

===Food and drink===
{{main article|Pakistani cuisine}}
[[File:Chicken Tikka.jpg|200px|thumbnail|left|A Pakistani dish cooked using the [[tandoori]] method.]]
Pakistani cuisine is similar to cuisine from other regions of [[South Asia]], since much of it originated from the royal kitchens of sixteenth-century Mughal emperors. Pakistan has a greater variety of meat dishes compared to the rest of the sub-continent. Most of those dishes have their roots in [[British cuisine|British]], [[Central Asian cuisine|Central Asian]] and [[Middle Eastern cuisine]].  Pakistani cooking uses large quantities of spices, herbs and seasoning. [[Garlic]], [[ginger]], [[turmeric]], red [[chilli]] and [[garam masala]] are used in most dishes, and home cooking regularly includes curry. [[Chapati]], a thin flat bread made from wheat, is a staple food, served with curry, meat, vegetables and lentils. Rice is also common; it is served plain, fried with spices, and in sweet dishes.&lt;ref name=&quot;yasmeen&quot;&gt;{{cite book|author=Yasmeen Niaz Mohiuddin|title=Pakistan: a global studies handbook|year=2006|publisher=ABC-CLIO {{!}}|isbn=1-85109-801-1|pages=3, 317, 323â€“324}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|author=Kathleen W. Deady|title=Countries of the world :Pakistan|year=2001|publisher=Capstone Press|isbn=0-7368-0815-9|pages=13â€“15}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|author=American Geriatrics Society. Ethnogeriatrics Committee|title=Doorway thoughts: cross-cultural health care for older adults|year=2006|publisher=Jones &amp; Bartlett Learning|isbn=978-0-7637-4355-0|pages=119â€“120}}&lt;/ref&gt;

[[Lassi]] is a traditional drink in the [[Punjab region]]. Black tea with milk and sugar is [[Pakistani tea culture|popular throughout Pakistan]] and is taken daily by most of the population.&lt;ref name=&quot;taxila&quot;&gt;{{cite book |title=Pakistan &amp; the Karakoram Highway|year=2008|publisher=Lonely Planet|isbn=978-1-74104-542-0|page=60,128,376|url=https://books.google.com/books?id=zn8I4qEew9oC|accessdate=28 December 2011|author1=Sarina Singh|author2=Lindsay Brow|author3=Paul Clammer|author4=Rodney Cocks|author5=John Mock}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref&gt;{{cite book|author=Tarla Dalal|title=Punjabi Khana|year=2007|publisher=Sanjay &amp; Co|isbn=978-81-89491-54-3|page=8}}&lt;/ref&gt; [[Sohan Halwa]] is a popular sweet dish from the southern region of Punjab province and is enjoyed all over Pakistan.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|url=http://www.dawn.com/news/1074194/sohan-halwa-a-gift-of-saints-city|title=Sohan Halwa a gift of saintsâ€™ city|date=16 December 2013|publisher=Dawn.com|accessdate=28 February 2014}}&lt;/ref&gt;
{{clear}}

===Sports===
{{Main article|Sports in Pakistan}}

The majority of the [[sports]] played in Pakistan are originated and were substantially developed from the [[United Kingdom]] who introduced in the [[Indian subcontinent|British India]]. [[Field hockey|Field Hockey]] is the [[national sport]] of Pakistan; it has won three [[Olympic Gold medal|Gold medallions]] in the [[Olympic Games]] held in [[Field hockey at the 1960 Summer Olympics|1960]], [[Field hockey at the 1968 Summer Olympics|1968]], and [[Field hockey at the 1984 Summer Olympics â€“ Men's tournament|1984]].&lt;ref name=&quot;Mallon&quot;&gt;{{cite book|author=Bill Mallon|title=Historical Dictionary of the Olympic Movement|year=2011|publisher=Scarecrow|isbn=978-0-8108-7249-3|edition=4th revised|author2=Jeroen Heijmans|page=291}}&lt;/ref&gt; Pakistan has also won the [[Hockey World Cup]] a record four times held in [[1971 Men's Hockey World Cup|1971]], [[1978 Men's Hockey World Cup|1978]], [[1982 Men's Hockey World Cup|1982]], and in [[1994 Men's Hockey World Cup|1994]].&lt;ref name=&quot;Suburaj: Hockey World Champion&quot;&gt;{{cite book|url=https://books.google.com/?id=Z0mjae1kdYUC&amp;pg=PA771&amp;dq=Hockey+World+Cup+Pakistan#v=onepage&amp;q=Hockey%20World%20Cup%20Pakistan&amp;f=false|title=Basic Facts of General Knowledge|author=V.V.K.Subburaj|date=30 August 2004|publisher=Sura College of Competition|accessdate=22 April 2012|isbn=978-81-7254-234-4|page=771}}&lt;/ref&gt;

[[Cricket]], however, is the most popular game across the country.&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|url=http://www.huffingtonpost.com/saad-khan/the-death-of-sports-in-pa_b_498758.html|title=The Death of Sports in Pakistan|author=Saad Khan|date=15 March 2010|publisher=The Huffington Post|accessdate=8 July 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt; The [[Pakistan national cricket team|cricket team]] (popular as [[Shaheen falcon|''Shaheen'']]) has won the [[Cricket World Cup]] held in [[1992 Cricket World Cup|1992]]; it had been runners-up once, in [[1999 Cricket World Cup|1999]], and co-hosted the tournament in [[1987 Cricket World Cup|1987]] and [[1996 Cricket World Cup|1996]]. Pakistan were runners-up in the inaugural [[2007 ICC World Twenty20|World Twenty20]] (2007) in [[South Africa]] and won the [[2009 ICC World Twenty20|World Twenty20]] in England in 2009. In March 2009, militants [[2009 attack on the Sri Lanka national cricket team|attacked]] the touring [[Sri Lanka]]'s [[Sri Lankan cricket team|cricket team]],&lt;ref&gt;{{cite news|title=Pakistan cricket future in doubt|url=http://news.bbc.co.uk/sport2/low/cricket/7921384.stm|publisher=BBC|date=4 March 2009|accessdate=1 January 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt; after which no international cricket was played in Pakistan until May 2015, when [[Zimbabwean cricket team in Pakistan in 2015|the Zimbabwean team agreed to a tour]].
[[File:A1 Team Pakistan topview.JPG|thumb|left|225px|The [[A1 Grand Prix|A1]] car of [[A1 Team Pakistan]] driven by the motorsport driver, [[Adam Khan]].]]
In athletics, [[Abdul Khaliq (athlete)|Abdul Khaliq]] participated in [[Athletics at the 1954 Asian Games|1954 Asian Games]] and the [[Athletics at the 1958 Asian Games|1958 Asian Games]]. He won 34 international gold, 15 international silver and 12 bronze medals for Pakistan.&lt;ref name=&quot;dw&quot;&gt;{{cite web|title=Did the 'fastest man of Asia' run in vain|publisher=Dawn.com|url=http://www.dawn.com/news/1077188/did-the-fastest-man-of-asia-run-in-vain|accessdate=13 January 2014}}&lt;/ref&gt;

In [[Squash (sport)|squash]], world-class players such as [[Jahangir Khan]]&lt;ref name=&quot;squashsite&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.squashsite.co.uk/olympics4.htm|title=Greatest player|publisher=[[Squashsite]]|accessdate=2 March 2010}}&lt;/ref&gt; and [[Jansher Khan]] won the [[World Open (squash)|World Open]] Squash Championship several times during their careers.&lt;ref name=&quot;squash&quot;&gt;{{cite book|author=Ian Graham|title=Pakistan|year=2003|publisher=Black Rabbit Books|isbn=1-58340-239-X|pages=20â€“21}}&lt;/ref&gt; Jahangir Khan also won the [[British Open Squash Championships|British Open]] a record ten times.&lt;ref name=&quot;squashsite&quot; /&gt;

Pakistan has competed many times at the Olympics in field hockey, boxing, athletics, swimming, and shooting.&lt;ref name=&quot;almanac&quot;&gt;{{cite book|author=K M Shariff|title=Pakistan almanac 2001â€“2002|year=2002|publisher=Royal Book Company|isbn=969-407-257-3|pages=561â€“574}}&lt;/ref&gt; Pakistan's Olympic medal tally stands at 10 of which 8 were earned in hockey.&lt;ref name=&quot;Mallon: Olympic medal tally&quot;&gt;{{cite book|title=Historical Dictionary of the Olympic Movement|url=https://books.google.com/?id=9mM0XzW03AcC&amp;pg=PA291&amp;dq=Pakistan+in+olympic#v=onepage&amp;q=Pakistan%20in%20olympic&amp;f=false|publisher=Scarecrow Press|accessdate=22 April 2012|author=Bill Mallon, Jeroen Heijmans|date=11 August 2011|isbn=978-0-8108-7249-3|page=291|edition=4th}}&lt;/ref&gt; The [[Commonwealth Games]] and [[Asian Games]] medal tallies stand at 65 and 160 respectively.&lt;ref name=&quot;CGF: 2013&quot;&gt;{{cite web|title=Pakistan â€“ Medals Tally by Games|url=http://www.thecgf.com/countries/tally.asp|publisher=Commonwealth Games Federation|accessdate=30 July 2013}}&lt;/ref&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;Asian Games Medal Count&quot;&gt;{{cite web|title=Asian Games Medal Count|url=http://ocasia.org/Game/NocsMedalCount.aspx?9QoyD9QEWPeJ2ChZBk5tvA==|work=Asian Games|publisher=Olympic Council of Asia|accessdate=22 April 2012}}&lt;/ref&gt;

At national level, polo is popular, with regular national events in different parts of the country. Boxing, billiards, snooker, rowing, kayaking, caving, tennis, contract bridge, golf and volleyball are also actively pursued, and Pakistan has produced regional and international champions in these sports.&lt;ref name=&quot;guide&quot; /&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;squash&quot; /&gt;&lt;ref name=&quot;almanac&quot; /&gt; Basketball enjoys regional popularity especially in [[Lahore]] and [[Karachi]].&lt;ref name=&quot;Welcome to Pakistan Olympic Association&quot;&gt;{{cite web|url=http://www.nocpakistan.org/sportsfederations.php?sptid=4|title=Welcome to the Pakistan Basketball Federation|publisher=Pakistan Olympic Association|accessdate=7 August 2014}}&lt;/ref&gt;
